Refuting the Doubt of Pointing: Vision Without Direction
Nasafi rebuts the doubt that visibility implies pointability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that visible things can be pointed to does not apply to Allah, and how Nasafi distinguishes between pointing (due to spatial extension) and vision (an attribute of existence).

Core Idea
Pointing requires spatial extension; vision requires existence, not location.

One of the objections raised against seeing Allah is that if He were visible, He could be pointed to. Nasafi responds by clarifying that pointing in the created world is based on spatial extension (tahayyuz), not on visibility. Vision, he argues, is an attribute of existence, not of extension. This distinction preserves the possibility of seeing Allah without direction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is formulated and how Nasafi's answer unfolds.
Pay attention to the key terms: işāra, tahayyuz, ru'ya.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find the sentence where Nasafi distinguishes between pointing (due to extension) and vision (attribute of existence). This is the core of the argument.
3
Consider the Ash'ari position
Nasafi mentions that Ash'aris allow pointing to Allah without direction. Think about what this implies for their view on seeing Allah.
This connects to the broader Maturidi-Ash'ari discussion on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدَ ذَلِكَ يَقُولُ: إِنَّ لَهُمْ شُبَهًا أُخْرَى وَيَذْكُرُ بَعْضَهَا مِنْهَا: أَنَّهُ إِذَا كَانَ الْبَارِي مَرْئِيًّا لَجَازَ أَنْ يُشَارَ إِلَيْهِ. وَيُجِيبُ بِأَنَّ الْأَشْعَرِيَّةَ يُجِيزُونَ الْإِشَارَةَ إِلَى اللهِ تَعَالَى لَا إِلَى جِهَةٍ، وَنَحْنُ نُجِيزُ الرُّؤْيَةَ لَا فِي جِهَةٍ، وَجَوَازُ الْإِشَارَةِ إِلَى شَيْءٍ فِي الشَّاهِدِ لَا لِجَوَازِ رُؤْيَتِهِ بَلْ لِكَوْنِهِ مُتَحَيِّزًا، فَالرُّؤْيَةُ مِنْ أَوْصَافِ الْوُجُودِ لَا مِنْ أَوْصَافِ التَّحَيُّزِ. وَيَقُولُ فِي آيَةٍ: {لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ} [الأنعام: ١٠٣] إِنَّ الْأَشْعَرِيَّةَ يَقُولُونَ: إِنَّ النَّفْيَ يَنْصَبُّ عَلَى الْأَبْصَارِ لَا عَلَى الْمُبْصِرِينَ، وَيُبْطِلُ هَذَا الْكَلَامَ، ثُمَّ يَقُولُ: الْمَنْفِيُّ هُنَا هُوَ الْإِدْرَاكُ بِمَعْنَى الْإِحَاطَةِ وَالشُّمُولِ، وَالرُّؤْيَةُ غَيْرُ مُنْتَفِيَةٍ، مُؤَكِّدًا كَلَامَهُ بِمَا قَالَهُ الْمَاتُرِيدِيُّ: إِنَّ الْإِدْرَاكَ هُوَ الْوُقُوفُ عَلَى أَطْرَافِ الشَّيْءِ، وَحُدُودِهِ، وَنِهَايَاتِهِ كَالْإِحَاطَةِ فَكَانَ الْإِدْرَاكُ فِي الرُّؤْيَةِ نَازِلًا مَنْزِلَةَ الْإِحَاطَةِ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ، ثُمَّ بِنَفْيِ الْإِحَاطَةِ لَا يَنْتَفِي الْعِلْمُ، فَكَانَ الْعِلْمُ هُوَ تَبَيُّنُ الْمَعْلُومِ عَلَى مَا هُوَ بِهِ، وَالْإِحَاطَةُ هِيَ الْوُقُوفُ عَلَى حُدُودِ الْمَعْلُومِ وَنِهَايَاتِهِ، فَكَانَ الْعِلْمُ بِاللهِ ثَابِتًا وَالْإِحَاطَةُ مُنْتَفِيَةً. وَأَثْنَاءَ الْحَدِيثِ يَعْرِضُ لِشُبَهٍ أُخْرَى، وَيُنَاقِشُهَا وَيَخْلُصُ إِلَى أَنَّ اللهَ تَعَالَى لَا يُرَى فِي الدُّنْيَا لِأَمْرَيْنِ: الْأَوَّلُ: أَنَّ اللهَ خَلَقَ فِي أَبْصَارِنَا ضِدَّ رُؤْيَتِهِ، وَهَذَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ
After that he says: They have other doubts, and he mentions some of them, including: that if the Creator is visible, then it would be permissible to point to Him. And he answers that the Ash'aris permit pointing to Allah (Exalted) not to a direction, and we permit vision without direction. And the permissibility of pointing to something in the witnessed world is not because of the permissibility of seeing it, but because it is extended (mutahayyiz). So vision is from the attributes of existence, not from the attributes of spatial extension. And regarding the verse: 'Vision perceives Him not' (al-An'am 103), he says that the Ash'aris say that the negation applies to the eyes (absar) not to the seers (mubsirin), and he invalidates this statement. Then he says: What is negated here is idrak (comprehension) in the sense of encompassing and including, and vision is not negated. He confirms his statement by what al-Maturidi said: that idrak is stopping at the edges of a thing, its limits, and its ends, like encompassing (ihata). So idrak in vision is like ihata in knowledge. Then by negating ihata, knowledge is not negated. So knowledge is discerning of the known as it is, and ihata is stopping at the limits and ends of the known. So knowledge of Allah is established and ihata is negated. And during the discussion he presents other doubts and discusses them and concludes that Allah (Exalted) is not seen in this world for two reasons: first: that Allah created in our eyes an opposite (didd) of seeing Him, and this is not impossible.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage refutes specific objections (shubuh) raised against the possibility of seeing Allah. The first objection is that if God were visible, He could be pointed to. Nasafi answers that the Ash'aris allow pointing to Allah without direction, and we allow seeing without direction. In the witnessed world, pointing to something is due to its being extended (mutahayyiz), not due to visibility. So visibility is an attribute of existence, not of extension. The second objection is based on verse 'Vision perceives Him not' (La tudrikuhu al-absar). Ash'aris reportedly interpret it as negation of the eyes themselves, which Nasafi invalidates. He clarifies that the negation is of idrak (comprehension), which means encompassing (ihata). Vision is not negated because one can see without encompassing. He supports this with al-Maturidi's analogy: idrak is to vision as ihata is to knowledge. Just as negating ihata does not negate knowledge, negating idrak does not negate vision. Therefore, knowledge of Allah is affirmed while comprehension is denied. Finally, Nasafi mentions that there are other doubts, and he concludes that Allah is not seen in this world for two reasons. The first reason given is that Allah creates in our eyes an opposite (didd) of seeing Him, which is not impossible. (The second reason is cut off.)

Key Terms
إشارة (pointing)
تحيز (tahayyuz)
أوصاف الوجود (attributes of existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the reason we can point to things in the witnessed world?
A) Because they are visible
B) Because they are extended (mutahayyiz)
C) Because they have color
D) Because they are known

Key Takeaway
Pointing requires spatial extension, not visibility; therefore, Allah can be visible without being pointable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find it uncomfortable to worship a God you cannot point to? What does that reveal about your reliance on physical concepts for spiritual realities?
?
If Allah is visible without being in any direction, how must you adjust your mental image of seeing Him on the Day of Judgment? Are you willing to let go of imagining a location?
?
This lesson distinguishes between existence and extension. In what areas of your life have you confused 'existing' with 'being somewhere'? How might that confuse your understanding of Allah?

Interpreting 'No Vision Comprehends Him': Idrak vs. Ru'ya
Nasafi interprets the idrak verse and explains non-vision in this world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's interpretation of the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' (Quran 6:103) and how he distinguishes between idrak (comprehension) and ru'ya (vision), using al-Maturidi's analogy with knowledge and ihata.

Core Idea
The verse negates comprehension (idrak), not vision itself.

The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' is often cited as proof that Allah cannot be seen. Nasafi, following al-Maturidi, argues that what is negated is idrak, which means 'comprehending' or 'encompassing' (ihata), not vision. Just as negating ihata does not negate knowledge, negating idrak does not negate vision. Nasafi also touches on a reason why Allah is not seen in this world: He creates in our eyes an opposite of seeing Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the verse interpretation
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on how Nasafi refutes the Ash'ari reading of the verse.
Notice that he first invalidates the Ash'ari claim that negation applies to the eyes.
2
Study al-Maturidi's analogy
Find the analogy between idrak/vision and ihata/knowledge. Understand how it works.
This is a key logical parallel used to defend the possibility of seeing Allah.
3
Grasp the distinction between idrak and ru'ya
Nasafi concludes that idrak (comprehension) is negated, not ru'ya. This distinction is central.
The verse is often misunderstood; this clarification is essential.
4
Read the concluding reason about this world
Nasafi gives the first reason why Allah is not seen in this world: an opposite created in the eyes.
This shows that non-vision in this life is due to a creaturely limitation, not an impossibility for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدَ ذَلِكَ يَقُولُ: إِنَّ لَهُمْ شُبَهًا أُخْرَى وَيَذْكُرُ بَعْضَهَا مِنْهَا: أَنَّهُ إِذَا كَانَ الْبَارِي مَرْئِيًّا لَجَازَ أَنْ يُشَارَ إِلَيْهِ. وَيُجِيبُ بِأَنَّ الْأَشْعَرِيَّةَ يُجِيزُونَ الْإِشَارَةَ إِلَى اللهِ تَعَالَى لَا إِلَى جِهَةٍ، وَنَحْنُ نُجِيزُ الرُّؤْيَةَ لَا فِي جِهَةٍ، وَجَوَازُ الْإِشَارَةِ إِلَى شَيْءٍ فِي الشَّاهِدِ لَا لِجَوَازِ رُؤْيَتِهِ بَلْ لِكَوْنِهِ مُتَحَيِّزًا، فَالرُّؤْيَةُ مِنْ أَوْصَافِ الْوُجُودِ لَا مِنْ أَوْصَافِ التَّحَيُّزِ. وَيَقُولُ فِي آيَةٍ: {لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ} [الأنعام: ١٠٣] إِنَّ الْأَشْعَرِيَّةَ يَقُولُونَ: إِنَّ النَّفْيَ يَنْصَبُّ عَلَى الْأَبْصَارِ لَا عَلَى الْمُبْصِرِينَ، وَيُبْطِلُ هَذَا الْكَلَامَ، ثُمَّ يَقُولُ: الْمَنْفِيُّ هُنَا هُوَ الْإِدْرَاكُ بِمَعْنَى الْإِحَاطَةِ وَالشُّمُولِ، وَالرُّؤْيَةُ غَيْرُ مُنْتَفِيَةٍ، مُؤَكِّدًا كَلَامَهُ بِمَا قَالَهُ الْمَاتُرِيدِيُّ: إِنَّ الْإِدْرَاكَ هُوَ الْوُقُوفُ عَلَى أَطْرَافِ الشَّيْءِ، وَحُدُودِهِ، وَنِهَايَاتِهِ كَالْإِحَاطَةِ فَكَانَ الْإِدْرَاكُ فِي الرُّؤْيَةِ نَازِلًا مَنْزِلَةَ الْإِحَاطَةِ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ، ثُمَّ بِنَفْيِ الْإِحَاطَةِ لَا يَنْتَفِي الْعِلْمُ، فَكَانَ الْعِلْمُ هُوَ تَبَيُّنُ الْمَعْلُومِ عَلَى مَا هُوَ بِهِ، وَالْإِحَاطَةُ هِيَ الْوُقُوفُ عَلَى حُدُودِ الْمَعْلُومِ وَنِهَايَاتِهِ، فَكَانَ الْعِلْمُ بِاللهِ ثَابِتًا وَالْإِحَاطَةُ مُنْتَفِيَةً. وَأَثْنَاءَ الْحَدِيثِ يَعْرِضُ لِشُبَهٍ أُخْرَى، وَيُنَاقِشُهَا وَيَخْلُصُ إِلَى أَنَّ اللهَ تَعَالَى لَا يُرَى فِي الدُّنْيَا لِأَمْرَيْنِ: الْأَوَّلُ: أَنَّ اللهَ خَلَقَ فِي أَبْصَارِنَا ضِدَّ رُؤْيَتِهِ، وَهَذَا لَا يَسْتَحِيلُ
After that he says: They have other doubts, and he mentions some of them, including: that if the Creator is visible, then it would be permissible to point to Him. And he answers that the Ash'aris permit pointing to Allah (Exalted) not to a direction, and we permit vision without direction. And the permissibility of pointing to something in the witnessed world is not because of the permissibility of seeing it, but because it is extended (mutahayyiz). So vision is from the attributes of existence, not from the attributes of spatial extension. And regarding the verse: 'Vision perceives Him not' (al-An'am 103), he says that the Ash'aris say that the negation applies to the eyes (absar) not to the seers (mubsirin), and he invalidates this statement. Then he says: What is negated here is idrak (comprehension) in the sense of encompassing and including, and vision is not negated. He confirms his statement by what al-Maturidi said: that idrak is stopping at the edges of a thing, its limits, and its ends, like encompassing (ihata). So idrak in vision is like ihata in knowledge. Then by negating ihata, knowledge is not negated. So knowledge is discerning of the known as it is, and ihata is stopping at the limits and ends of the known. So knowledge of Allah is established and ihata is negated. And during the discussion he presents other doubts and discusses them and concludes that Allah (Exalted) is not seen in this world for two reasons: first: that Allah created in our eyes an opposite (didd) of seeing Him, and this is not impossible.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage refutes specific objections (shubuh) raised against the possibility of seeing Allah. The first objection is that if God were visible, He could be pointed to. Nasafi answers that the Ash'aris allow pointing to Allah without direction, and we allow seeing without direction. In the witnessed world, pointing to something is due to its being extended (mutahayyiz), not due to visibility. So visibility is an attribute of existence, not of extension. The second objection is based on verse 'Vision perceives Him not' (La tudrikuhu al-absar). Ash'aris reportedly interpret it as negation of the eyes themselves, which Nasafi invalidates. He clarifies that the negation is of idrak (comprehension), which means encompassing (ihata). Vision is not negated because one can see without encompassing. He supports this with al-Maturidi's analogy: idrak is to vision as ihata is to knowledge. Just as negating ihata does not negate knowledge, negating idrak does not negate vision. Therefore, knowledge of Allah is affirmed while comprehension is denied. Finally, Nasafi mentions that there are other doubts, and he concludes that Allah is not seen in this world for two reasons. The first reason given is that Allah creates in our eyes an opposite (didd) of seeing Him, which is not impossible. (The second reason is cut off.)

Key Terms
إدراك (idrak)
إحاطة (ihata)
رؤية (ru'ya)
ضد (didd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' completely negates any possibility of seeing Allah.
Correction: Nasafi shows the verse negates idrak (comprehension/encompassing), not ru'ya (vision) itself. This distinction allows affirming vision of Allah in the afterlife while denying full comprehension.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does the word 'idrak' mean in the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'?
A) Seeing
B) Encompassing (ihata)
C) Pointing
D) Existence

Key Takeaway
The verse negates comprehension (idrak), not vision; therefore, seeing Allah is possible without encompassing Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you catch yourself assuming you have fully understood who Allah is, and what would it mean for your prayer to approach Him knowing you will never encompass Him?
?
If the only thing standing between you and seeing Allah in this life is a 'didd' He placed in your eyes, what 'didd' have you placed in your heart that prevents you from seeking His pleasure today?
?
This lesson teaches that not seeing Allah now is intentional, not impossible. Does your longing for the afterlife reflect a genuine desire to see Him, or are you more focused on other promises of Paradise?

Refutation from Ahl al-Hadith: Creating Evil is Not Evil
Al-Nasafi presents the Ahl al-Hadith argument against Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Hadith argument that God's creation of evil cannot be evil because God is subject to no prohibition and suffers no harm.

Core Idea
Evil is defined by prohibition and harm; God transcends both, so His actions are not evil.

Before we can assess the Mu'tazili claim that creating evil is itself evil, we need to ask: what makes an action evil? The People of Hadith define evil in terms of divine prohibition and harm. Since God is above both, His actions—including the creation of disbelief—fall outside the category of evil. This passage presents their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice that the argument uses two distinct definitions of evil.
The passage is concise but packed.
2
Identify the two definitions of evil
The passage gives two criteria for something being evil: (1) it is prohibited, and (2) it causes pure harm to the doer. Find where each appears.
These definitions are taken from the Ahl al-Hadith tradition.
3
Apply to God and reflect
Consider why God fails both criteria: He has no prohibitor, and He cannot be harmed. Conclude that His actions cannot be evil. Ask yourself how this refutes the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك يبين فساد قول المعتزلة : أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح، فيقول: إن أهل الحديث يقولون : إن القبيح ما ورد النهي عنه، والله سبحانه فيما يفعل ليس عليه نهي، ولم يتجاوز حدا حُدَّ له، ولا رسما رسم له، فلم يكن فيما يفعل سفها، ولا فعله قبيحا، وكما أن القبيح ما يعود به على فاعله الضرر المحض، والله تعالى لا يعود عليه بأي فعل ضرر، فلا يكون خلقه الكفر قبيحا. ويقول مشايخ الماتريدية : كل ما له عاقبة حميدة فهو حكمة، وما ليس كذلك فهو سفه، ولا شيء يعود إلى الله، فلا سَفَه، وكل أفعاله حكمة.
Then after that he explains the corruption of the Muʿtazila's statement: that bringing evil into existence (ījād al-qabīḥ) is evil. He says: Indeed the People of Hadith (ahl al-ḥadīth) say: Evil (al-qabīḥ) is what the prohibition is issued against. And God, exalted is He, in what He does, has no prohibition upon Him, and He has not transgressed any limit set for Him, nor any boundary drawn for Him. So there is no foolishness (safah) in what He does, nor is His action evil. And just as evil is what returns to its doer pure harm (ḍarar maḥḍ), and God Almighty is not returned upon by any action with harm, so His creation of disbelief (kufr) is not evil. And the shuyūkh of the Māturīdiyya say: Everything that has a praiseworthy outcome (ʿāqiba ḥamīda) is wisdom (ḥikma), and what is not like that is foolishness, and nothing returns to God, so there is no foolishness, and all His actions are wisdom.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazili doctrine that God's act of creating evil (e.g., kufr) is itself evil. Two arguments are presented. First, from Ahl al-Hadith: evil is defined by divine prohibition; God is subject to no prohibition, nor has He any imposed limit, so nothing He does can be evil. Moreover, evil is that which causes harm to the doer; God suffers no harm from any action, so creating kufr does not harm Him and thus is not evil. Second, from Maturidi scholars: actions are judged by their outcomes; a wise act has a good outcome, a foolish act has a bad or no good outcome. Since nothing returns to God (He is self-sufficient), no action of His can be foolish; all His actions are wise, including the creation of kufr, which serves divine purposes such as manifesting power, justice, etc. This safeguards God's transcendence above human moral categories.

Key Terms
إيجاد القبيح
القبيح
النهي
الضرر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that creating evil (e.g., kufr) is itself evil.
Correction: The Ahl al-Hadith argue that evil is defined by prohibition and harm; God is not subject to prohibition and cannot be harmed, so His creation of kufr is not evil.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what are the two bases for defining evil?
A) Prohibition and harm
B) Harm and benefit
C) Reason and revelation
D) Absolute and relative

Key Takeaway
God's actions are not evil because He is subject to no prohibition and cannot be harmed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a tragedy or experience something you consider deeply unfair, do you ever find yourself questioning God’s goodness or thinking His action was ‘wrong’? After studying this passage, what would it mean for you to truly believe that God’s actions are beyond the categories of ‘good’ and ‘evil’ that apply to creatures? How would that change your reaction to hardship?
?
You have likely judged God’s actions—perhaps silently—based on your own sense of justice. This lesson says God cannot be harmed or prohibited. But do you still feel entitled to call His actions ‘evil’ when they hurt you? What would it take for you to surrender that judgment completely and trust that His wisdom is beyond your moral framework?
?
Imagine the next time you see someone committing disbelief or sin, and you feel anger at God for allowing it. This lesson argues that creating disbelief is not evil. Does your heart accept that, or does it still blame God? What is the root of that blame—and what step can you take today to replace it with awe at His transcendence?

Refutation from Maturidi Shuyukh: All God's Actions are Wisdom
Al-Nasafi presents the Maturidi argument that God's actions are all wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi argument that all of God's actions are wisdom because they have praiseworthy outcomes, and since nothing returns to God, there is no foolishness.

Core Idea
Actions are judged by outcomes; God's actions always have praiseworthy ends, so they are all wisdom.

The Maturidi scholars offer a second, complementary argument. They shift the focus from prohibition and harm to purpose and outcome. Every action with a good outcome is wise; one with a bad outcome is foolish. Since God is self-sufficient and nothing benefits or harms Him, no action of His can be foolish. This passage explains that all of God's actions, including creating kufr, are inherently wise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the concise structure: definition, application to God, conclusion.
This is a single, tight argument.
2
Understand the criterion of wisdom
The Maturidi shuyukh define wisdom (ḥikma) as an action that has a praiseworthy outcome (ʿāqiba ḥamīda). Foolishness is the opposite.
3
Apply to God and reflect
Since nothing returns to God (He is self-sufficient), no action of His can be foolish. Therefore all His actions are wisdom. Ask yourself how this refutes the Mu'tazila.
Consider that even creating kufr has a praiseworthy outcome from God's perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك يبين فساد قول المعتزلة : أن إيجاد القبيح قبيح، فيقول: إن أهل الحديث يقولون : إن القبيح ما ورد النهي عنه، والله سبحانه فيما يفعل ليس عليه نهي، ولم يتجاوز حدا حُدَّ له، ولا رسما رسم له، فلم يكن فيما يفعل سفها، ولا فعله قبيحا، وكما أن القبيح ما يعود به على فاعله الضرر المحض، والله تعالى لا يعود عليه بأي فعل ضرر، فلا يكون خلقه الكفر قبيحا. ويقول مشايخ الماتريدية : كل ما له عاقبة حميدة فهو حكمة، وما ليس كذلك فهو سفه، ولا شيء يعود إلى الله، فلا سَفَه، وكل أفعاله حكمة.
Then after that he explains the corruption of the Muʿtazila's statement: that bringing evil into existence (ījād al-qabīḥ) is evil. He says: Indeed the People of Hadith (ahl al-ḥadīth) say: Evil (al-qabīḥ) is what the prohibition is issued against. And God, exalted is He, in what He does, has no prohibition upon Him, and He has not transgressed any limit set for Him, nor any boundary drawn for Him. Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazili doctrine that God's act of creating evil (e.g., kufr) is itself evil. Two arguments are presented. First, from Ahl al-Hadith: evil is defined by divine prohibition; God is subject to no prohibition, nor has He any imposed limit, so nothing He does can be evil. Moreover, evil is that which causes harm to the doer; God suffers no harm from any action, so creating kufr does not harm Him and thus is not evil. Misconception: Some argue that creating disbelief is foolish because it causes harm to people.
Correction: The Maturidi scholars reply that harm to creatures is not a criterion for God; His actions always have praiseworthy outcomes from His perspective, such as manifesting justice.

Checkpoint

A) Because God never does anything
B) Because nothing returns to God
C) Because God only does good deeds
D) Because foolishness is impossible

All of God's actions are wisdom because they have praiseworthy outcomes and nothing returns to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If all of God's actions are wisdom, then the existence of disbelief and evil in the world serves a wise purpose. Does your soul accept this, or does it secretly rebel? How can you train your heart to submit to His wisdom even when you don't understand?
?
Consider an area of your life where you have questioned God's wisdom—perhaps a hardship, a loss, or a unanswered prayer. Will you now commit to saying, 'This too is wisdom from my Lord,' and trust that He is never foolish? What step will you take to act on that trust?

Refutation of Objection from Verse 51:56
Al-Nasafi refutes objections from Qur'anic verses about creation for worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi refutes the objection that verse 51:56 proves Allah only wills worship from every individual, by presenting alternative interpretations that allow the verse to apply to both believers and disbelievers.

Core Idea
A verse's general wording does not force a specific theological conclusion.

In this passage, Al-Nasafi addresses a common objection raised by theological opponents who use the Qur'anic verse 'I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me' to argue that Allah cannot will disbelief. Al-Nasafi counters by showing that the verse can be interpreted in ways that do not conflict with the Maturidi position on divine will. He draws on established exegetical principles to demonstrate that the opponents' reading is not the only—or even the most appropriate—interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the refutation.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Al-Nasafi presents two main interpretations of the verse. Locate them in the text: the first is 'except to be servants', the second is 'except for My commanding them to worship'.
Notice how each interpretation affects the opponent's argument.
3
Understand the logical consequences
Consider how each interpretation undermines the opponent's claim. The first broadens the meaning to include all as servants; the second shifts to command rather than actual worship. Note the concession about children and the insane.
This shows the depth of Al-Nasafi's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرد على ما تعلقوا به من الآيات مثل قوله تعالى: وَمَا خَلَقْتُ الْجِنَّ وَالْإِنسَ إِلَّا لِيَعْبُدُونَ [الذاريات: ٥٦] فيقول: إن كثيرا من أهل التأويل قالوا: معناه إلا ليكونوا عبادا لي، وهذا في الكافر والمؤمن، لا أن يقيدونه بفعلهم الاختياري، وهذا يجعل الآية جارية على العموم، وإجراؤها كذلك أولى من إجرائها على الخصوص، ومنهم من يقول: إلا لأمرهم بالعبادة، فلو حملت الآية على هذا لم يبق للخصم أي متعلق حتى ولو سلمنا أن المراد منها العبادة، إلا أنه خص منها الصغار، والمجانين لعدم التكليف، فيخص المتنازع وهم الكفرة، وتبقى الآية محمولة على من علم منهم الإيمان.
He responds to what they clung to of the verses such as His statement: 'And I did not create the jinn and mankind except to worship Me' [al-Dhariyat:56]. He says: Indeed many of the people of interpretation said: its meaning is 'except that they be servants to Me', and this applies to the disbeliever and the believer, not that they restrict it to their voluntary action. This makes the verse apply generally (al-`umum), and applying it thus is more appropriate than applying it specifically (al-khusus). And among them are those who say: 'except for My commanding them with worship.' So if the verse were interpreted in this way, there remains for the opponent no point of attachment. Even if we concede that what is meant by it is worship, except that it excludes children and the insane due to the lack of obligation (taklif), then the disputed ones (the disbelievers) are excluded, and the verse remains carried upon those of them whom He knows to be of faith.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection based on Qur'anic verse 51:56. The opponents (likely Mu'tazila) argue that since Allah created humans for worship, He cannot will kufr (disbelief) from them. Al-Nasafi responds by presenting two interpretations from the commentators (ahl al-ta'wil). First, the verse means 'except to be servants' (li-yakunu `ibadan), which applies to both believer and disbeliever in the sense of being subservient, not restricted to their voluntary act of worship. This gives the verse a general sense, and general application is preferable to specific. Second, some say it means 'except for My commanding them to worship'. If so, the verse gives no support to the opponent, because it commands worship universally. Even if we say worship is intended, children and insane are excluded due to lack of taklif (legal obligation), so the opponents (disbelievers) are still included among those commanded, and the verse remains applicable to those whom Allah knows will have faith. Thus the verse does not prove that Allah only wills worship from every individual.

Key Terms
أهل التأويل
عموم
خصوص
فعل اختياري
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse 'except to worship Me' necessarily implies that Allah's will is limited to worship from every person, ignoring the broader interpretation.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that the verse can be interpreted generally (as 'servants') or as a command, and even if taken literally, it is subject to exceptions like children and the insane.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is one interpretation of 'except to worship Me' that allows the verse to apply to both believers and disbelievers?
A) It means 'except to be servants' (عبادا)
B) It means 'except to pray'
C) It means 'except to obey'
D) It means 'except to believe'

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi demonstrates that a single Qur'anic verse can bear multiple valid interpretations, and the opponent's reading is not forced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me', do you instinctively assume you know exactly what it means, or do you allow space for multiple valid interpretations as al-Nasafi does? How does your humility before the Qur'an affect your theology?
?
Al-Nasafi shows that even children and the insane are excluded from the verse's obligation. Consider someone you know who has never truly worshipped Allah—are you quick to judge them, or do you remember that the ultimate knowledge of faith belongs to Allah alone? How does this reframe your attitude toward others?
?
The opponent here clung to a single reading of a verse to support their position. Is there a verse you personally have used to support a view without considering alternative explanations? Reflect on your willingness to investigate other interpretations, even if they challenge your comfort. What is at stake for you if you are wrong?

Nasafi's Quranic Refutation of Mu'tazila on Action
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila using Quran 6:125

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses Quran 6:125 to refute the Mu'tazila claim that humans create their own actions.

Core Idea
The Quran's distinction between guided and misguided disproves that guidance is mere clarification.

This lesson examines a key Quranic argument against the Mu'tazila doctrine of human self-creation of actions. Imam Nasafi uses Surah al-An'am 6:125 to show that if guidance were only calling, the verse would be self-contradictory. The lesson will walk through this logical refutation, grounding it in the source text.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila argue that humans create their own actions, so divine guidance and misguidance are reinterpreted as mere clarification or provision of means. Grasp this before moving to the refutation.
2
Read the Quranic argument
Read the Arabic and translation of the passage. Notice how Nasafi quotes verse 6:125 and then draws a logical implication from the Mu'tazila interpretation.
The verse contrasts expanded and constricted breasts.
3
Grasp the logical implication
If guidance is only calling, then everyone called should respond, making the verse about constricted hearts false. Nasafi calls this disbelief.
This shows the Mu'tazila position leads to contradicting the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنهم يقولون : إن العبد يخلق فعل نفسه، وعليه فإن فى رأيهم ما أضيف إلى الله من الهداية أي بيان طريق الدين، ومن الإضلال أي خلق الآلات التي بها يرتكب العبد الضلال. ثم يدلل على بطلان كلامهم بعدد من الأدلة منها قول تعالى: ﴿ فَمَن يُرِدِ اللَّهُ أَن يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلإِسْلَامِ ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٢٥] فلو كان المراد الدعوة وبيان الطريق فينبغي أن يقال كل من دعاه الله للإيمان ويبين له الطريق فهو مشروح الصدر، فيصير قوله تعالى: ﴿ يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَجًا ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٢٥] باطلا كذبا. وهو كفر.
Because they say: The servant creates his own action, and accordingly in their view what is attributed to Allah of guidance, i.e., clarifying the path of religion, and of misguidance, i.e., creating the instruments by which the servant commits misguidance. Then he provides proof of the invalidity of their statement with a number of proofs, among them the saying of the Most High: 'So whoever Allah wants to guide, He expands his breast to Islam' (al-An'am 6:125). So if the intended meaning were the call and clarification of the path, then it should be said that everyone whom Allah calls to faith and clarifies the path to him is expanded in breast, making the saying of the Most High: 'He makes his breast tight and constricted' (al-An'am 6:125) false and a lie. And that is disbelief.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that man creates his own actions. The author first states the Mu'tazila claim: that the servant creates his own actions, leading to a reinterpretation of divine guidance and misguidance as mere clarification or provision of means. Then he presents a proof against this view using Surah al-An'am 6:125. If guidance meant only calling and clarifying the path, then every person who is called and clarified should have an expanded breast, making the verse's distinction between the guided and the misguided meaningless and contradictory. Calling one who is called but not guided a liar is disbelief. Thus the Mu'tazila interpretation leads to blasphemy.

Key Terms
الهداية
الإضلال
شرح الصدر
خلق الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think that by denying that Allah creates evil actions, they protect divine justice.
Correction: Nasafi shows that their reinterpretation leads to a contradiction in the Quran, which is worse—it implies the Quran is false.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would be the consequence if 'guidance' (al-hidaya) meant only calling and clarifying the path?
A) Everyone called would become a believer.
B) The verse about constricted breasts would be false and a lie.
C) People would no longer need to seek guidance.
D) Allah would be unjust to those who are misguided.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that divine guidance must involve more than mere calling; otherwise, the Quran's statement about constricted hearts would be false, leading to disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your faith is merely intellectual assent—a dry 'yes I believe'—without the soul-expanding sweetness that comes from divine guidance? How does this verse (6:125) confront you with the reality that true hidayah is a gift you must beg for, not a conclusion you reach?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to protect Allah from being the source of misguidance, but in doing so they ended up calling the Quran false. Where in your own heart do you subtly resist the full weight of Allah's absolute will—perhaps by limiting His power over your life because it feels uncomfortable? What would it mean for you to surrender even that resistance?
?
If Allah can expand your breast to Islam, He can also leave it constricted. Does that truth make you fear Him—or seek Him more urgently? Today, what one concrete prayer can you utter that acknowledges your utter dependence on Him to open your heart?

Refuting the Maskhiyya on Transmutation of the Divine
Nasafi refutes the Maskhiyya doctrine of transmutation using two arguments and a reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maskhiyya doctrine of transmutation (inmisākh) is rationally impossible and how it leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
Transmutation of God implies partition and change—both impossible for the Eternal.

Nasafi turns to refute a specific sect, the Maskhiyya, who believed that a part of God could transmute. This belief contradicts the fundamental attributes of God's eternity and simplicity. In this passage, Nasafi demonstrates that even if transmutation were possible for a part, it would have to be possible for the whole, leading to absurdities no sound intellect can accept.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then its translation. Note the two main reasons for impossibility.
The Arabic uses technical terms like تبعض and تجزؤ.
2
Identify the first argument
The first reason: transmutation implies partition and division, which are impossible for the Ancient (al-qadīm).
Nasafi promises to elaborate later in the refutation of anthropomorphism.
3
Identify the second argument
The second reason: change (taghayyur) itself is impossible for the Eternal.
This will be explained later as well.
4
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
If transmutation were possible for a part, it must be possible for the whole. This leads to absurd consequences like all parts turning into devils or ugly bodies becoming gods.
This shows the contradiction of their claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة المسخية ويقال للمسخية منهم: إن ما تزعمون من انمساخ بعضه محال من وجوه أحدها إثبات التبعض والتجزؤ وهو على القديم محال على ما نبين في إبطال قول المجسمة إذا انتهينا إليه إن شاء الله تعالى والثاني أن القول بتغير القديم محال على ما نبين أيضا بعد هذا إن شاء الله ثم نقول لهم: لما جاز الانمساخ على بعضه جاز على كله، لثبوت الموافقة من جميع الوجوه بين جميع أجزائه فما جاز على البعض جاز على الكل، لا أمان بكم من أن يتقلب جميع أجزاء القديم شياطين وأبالسة وهذا ظاهرا لفساده، إذ لو جاز هذا لجاز انقلاب جميع الشياطين والأجسام القبيحة آلهة قديمة، وكل ذلك خارج عن قضية العقول وما قالوا من استحالة إيجاد الحكيم الشرور والقبايح لكون ذلك كله سفها فهو ممنوع، فيقال لهم: لم قلتم ذلك؟ ومن أين قلتم؟ أبدليل سمعي قلتم أم بدليل عقلي؟ فإن ادعوا الدليل السمعي طلب منهم الدليل على كون قول من أسندوا القول إليه حجة، وإن ادعوا الدليل العقلي طولبوا بإيراده، ثم نقول لهم: أعرفتم بعقولكم أن إيجاد هذه الأشياء سفه أم حكمتم بكونه سفها لجهلكم بوجه الحكمة في إيجاد هذه الأشياء؟ فإن زعموا أنهم عرفوا بعقولهم كونه سفها قيل لهم: أببديهة العقل عرفتم ذلك أم بالاستدلال؟ فإن قالوا عرفنا بالبديهة ظهرت مكابرتهم، لأن أرباب العقول السليمة لا يختلفون
Refutation of the Maskhiyya. And it is said to the Maskhiyya among them: Indeed what you claim of the transmutation (inmisākh) of some part of Him is impossible for several aspects: first, the affirmation of partition (tabaʿuḍ) and division (tajazzuʾ), which is impossible for the Ancient (al-qadīm), as we will explain in refuting the saying of the anthropomorphists (al-mujassima) when we reach it, God willing. Second, that the statement of change (taghayyur) in the Ancient is impossible, as we will also explain later, God willing. Then we say to them: Since transmutation is possible for some part of Him, it is possible for all of Him, due to the establishment of agreement in all respects among all His parts. So what is possible for a part is possible for the whole. There is no security for you that all parts of the Ancient would turn into devils (shayāṭīn) and demons (abālisa), and this is obviously corrupt, for if this were possible, then the transformation of all devils and ugly bodies into ancient gods would be possible, and all that is outside the judgment of intellects. And what they said of the impossibility of the Wise bringing about evils and ugly things, because all that is foolishness (safah), it is rejected. So it is said to them: Why did you say that? From where did you say it? Did you say it by a scriptural evidence (dalīl samʿī) or by a rational evidence (dalīl ʿaqlī)? If they claim scriptural evidence, proof is demanded from them that the saying of the one to whom they attribute the saying is authoritative. If they claim rational evidence, they are challenged to present it. Then we say to them: Did you know by your intellects that bringing about these things is foolishness, or did you judge it to be foolishness because of your ignorance of the aspect of wisdom in bringing about these things? If they claim that they knew by their intellects that it is foolishness, it is said to them: Did you know that by intellectual intuition (badīha) or by inference (istidlāl)? If they say we knew by intuition, their obstinacy (mukābara) becomes apparent, because people of sound intellects do not differ [on such matters].
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage contains two refutations. First, it refutes the Maskhiyya sect who believed in transmigration or transmutation of some part of God. The impossibility is argued from two points: (1) transmutation implies partition and division, which are impossible for the Eternal; (2) change itself in the Eternal is impossible. Additionally, if transmutation were possible for a part, it would be possible for the whole, leading to absurdities like all parts turning into devils or ugly bodies becoming gods—which sound intellects reject. Second, the passage refutes the more general objection that it is impossible for a wise being to create evil because that would be foolishness. The author demands proof: is this based on scripture or reason? If scripture, they must prove the authority of the one they quote. If reason, they must present the argument. Further, the author questions whether they know this by intuition or inference. If by intuition, they are obstinate because sound intellects do not universally agree that creating evil is foolish. The implication is that the judgment is not self-evident, and therefore the objection fails.

Key Terms
مسخية (Maskhiyya)
انمساخ (transmutation)
تبعض (partition)
تجزؤ (division)
قديم (Ancient)
تغير (change)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maskhiyya believe that a part of God can transmute or change.
Correction: Nasafi argues that transmutation implies partition and change, both impossible for the Eternal, and leads to absurdities.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Nasafi gives for the impossibility of transmutation?
A) Change is impossible for the Eternal.
B) Transmutation implies partition and division.
C) All parts must agree.
D) It leads to devils becoming gods.

Key Takeaway
Transmutation of God is impossible because it implies partition and change, and leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him as subject to change or affected by your actions? How does Nasafi's argument about the impossibility of change in the Eternal challenge you to refine your concept of God? What specific habit of thought must you abandon so that you never attribute imperfection to Him, even in your mind?
?
The reductio shows that allowing even a small change in God leads to absurd consequences. In your own life, are there 'small' compromises in your beliefs about God that you tolerate? How can you guard your heart against any conception that diminishes His absolute perfection and transcendence?
?
If you truly internalize that God is beyond all partition and change, how would that transform your worship and reliance upon Him? When you face trials, do you ever doubt His eternal power or attribute weakness to Him? Confess this to yourself and commit to remembering His immutable attributes in your next moment of difficulty.

Refuting the Claim that Creating Evil is Foolishness
Nasafi refutes the claim that creating evil is foolish by challenging its proof and the basis of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's method of refuting the objection that a wise God cannot create evil because that would be foolishness.

Core Idea
The claim that creating evil is foolish lacks both scriptural and rational proof.

Having refuted the Maskhiyya, Nasafi now addresses a more fundamental objection: How can a wise God create evil and ugly things? The objectors argue that doing so would be foolish (safah). Nasafi challenges them to provide evidence—either scriptural or rational—and then questions whether they know this by intuition or inference. He shows that intuition would be invalid because sound intellects disagree, so the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'وما قالوا' to the end. Notice how Nasafi demands proof and then questions the basis of knowledge.
2
Identify the objection
The objectors claim it is impossible for the Wise (al-ḥakīm) to create evils because that would be foolishness (safah).
Nasafi calls this claim 'rejected' (mamnūʿ).
3
Follow the demand for proof
Nasafi asks: Did you say this by scriptural evidence or rational evidence? If scriptural, you must prove the authority; if rational, you must present the argument.
This shifts the burden of proof.
4
Examine the intuition vs. inference dilemma
If they claim intuitive knowledge, Nasafi argues that sound intellects do not universally agree that creating evil is foolish, so their claim of intuition is obstinate.
This reveals the weakness of their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة المسخية ويقال للمسخية منهم: إن ما تزعمون من انمساخ بعضه محال من وجوه أحدها إثبات التبعض والتجزؤ وهو على القديم محال على ما نبين في إبطال قول المجسمة إذا انتهينا إليه إن شاء الله تعالى والثاني أن القول بتغير القديم محال على ما نبين أيضا بعد هذا إن شاء الله ثم نقول لهم: لما جاز الانمساخ على بعضه جاز على كله، لثبوت الموافقة من جميع الوجوه بين جميع أجزائه فما جاز على البعض جاز على الكل، لا أمان بكم من أن يتقلب جميع أجزاء القديم شياطين وأبالسة وهذا ظاهرا لفساده، إذ لو جاز هذا لجاز انقلاب جميع الشياطين والأجسام القبيحة آلهة قديمة، وكل ذلك خارج عن قضية العقول وما قالوا من استحالة إيجاد الحكيم الشرور والقبايح لكون ذلك كله سفها فهو ممنوع، فيقال لهم: لم قلتم ذلك؟ ومن أين قلتم؟ أبدليل سمعي قلتم أم بدليل عقلي؟ فإن ادعوا الدليل السمعي طلب منهم الدليل على كون قول من أسندوا القول إليه حجة، وإن ادعوا الدليل العقلي طولبوا بإيراده، ثم نقول لهم: أعرفتم بعقولكم أن إيجاد هذه الأشياء سفه أم حكمتم بكونه سفها لجهلكم بوجه الحكمة في إيجاد هذه الأشياء؟ فإن زعموا أنهم عرفوا بعقولهم كونه سفها قيل لهم: أببديهة العقل عرفتم ذلك أم بالاستدلال؟ فإن قالوا عرفنا بالبديهة ظهرت مكابرتهم، لأن أرباب العقول السليمة لا يختلفون
Refutation of the Maskhiyya. And it is said to the Maskhiyya among them: Indeed what you claim of the transmutation (inmisākh) of some part of Him is impossible for several aspects: first, the affirmation of partition (tabaʿuḍ) and division (tajazzuʾ), which is impossible for the Ancient (al-qadīm), as we will explain in refuting the saying of the anthropomorphists (al-mujassima) when we reach it, God willing. Second, that the statement of change (taghayyur) in the Ancient is impossible, as we will also explain later, God willing. Then we say to them: Since transmutation is possible for some part of Him, it is possible for all of Him, due to the establishment of agreement in all respects among all His parts. So what is possible for a part is possible for the whole. There is no security for you that all parts of the Ancient would turn into devils (shayāṭīn) and demons (abālisa), and this is obviously corrupt, for if this were possible, then the transformation of all devils and ugly bodies into ancient gods would be possible, and all that is outside the judgment of intellects. And what they said of the impossibility of the Wise bringing about evils and ugly things, because all that is foolishness (safah), it is rejected. So it is said to them: Why did you say that? From where did you say it? Did you say it by a scriptural evidence (dalīl samʿī) or by a rational evidence (dalīl ʿaqlī)? If they claim scriptural evidence, proof is demanded from them that the saying of the one to whom they attribute the saying is authoritative. If they claim rational evidence, they are challenged to present it. Then we say to them: Did you know by your intellects that bringing about these things is foolishness, or did you judge it to be foolishness because of your ignorance of the aspect of wisdom in bringing about these things? If they claim that they knew by their intellects that it is foolishness, it is said to them: Did you know that by intellectual intuition (badīha) or by inference (istidlāl)? If they say we knew by intuition, their obstinacy (mukābara) becomes apparent, because people of sound intellects do not differ [on such matters].

Commentary
This passage contains two refutations. First, it refutes the Maskhiyya sect who believed in transmigration or transmutation of some part of God. The impossibility is argued from two points: (1) transmutation implies partition and division, which are impossible for the Eternal; (2) change itself in the Eternal is impossible. Misconception: Some think it is self-evident that creating evil is foolish, so God cannot do it.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, if someone claims intuitive knowledge that creating evil is foolish, what follows?
A) They must provide scriptural evidence.
B) They are asked to present rational argument.
C) Their obstinacy becomes apparent because sound intellects do not differ on such matters.
D) They are correct because intuition is reliable.

Key Takeaway
The claim that creating evil is foolish lacks proof and fails the test of intuition, as sound intellects differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly felt that God's actions in your life—or in the world—are foolish or unfair? What evidence do you have for that judgment, and how does Nasafi's challenge to the objectors apply to your own heart?
?
When you face a tragedy or evil you cannot explain, do you humbly admit your ignorance of God's wisdom, or do you assume it must be wrong? This lesson forces you to examine which response is true of you, starting today.
?
Nasafi demands that every claim about God be backed by proof—scriptural or rational—not by mere intuition. Are you willing to subject your own beliefs about God to the same standard, even if it means abandoning comfortable assumptions? What will you do with this challenge?

Divine Transcendence

Refuting Transformation in God: Three Arguments
Imam Nasafi refutes the Musabbiha by presenting three arguments against transformation in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God can be divided or transformed is rationally impossible according to Imam Nasafi.

Core Idea
If God could change, devils could become gods — absurdity proves His transcendence.

Imam Nasafi here refutes a specific anthropomorphic sect that claimed God could undergo transformation. He uses three clear arguments, each showing that such a claim leads to logical contradictions. The first two arguments are previews of later discussions, while the third is a direct reductio ad absurdum. This lesson walks through these arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three arguments
Read the Arabic and English carefully. Look for the three distinct reasons Nasafi gives. Note that he mentions they will be explained further later.
The first two are brief, the third is developed more fully.
2
Understand the first argument: division and partition
Nasafi says transformation implies division (taba'ud) and partition (tajazzu'), which is impossible for the Eternal. Reflect on why a simple, non-composite being cannot be divided.
This connects to later refutation of Mujassima.
3
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum argument
If a part could change, by parity the whole could change, leading to the absurdity that all parts could become devils, or devils could become deities. This is clearly against reason. Consider how this argument uses the principle of uniformity.
It shows the logical consequence of the opponent's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للمسخية منهم: إن ما تزعمون من انمساخ بعضه محال من وجوه أحدها إثبات التبعض والتجزؤ وهو على القديم محال على ما نبين في إبطال قول المجسمة إذا انتهينا إليه إن شاء الله تعالى والثاني: أن القول بتغير القديم محال على ما نبين أيضا بعد هذا إن شاء الله ثم نقول لهم: لما جاز الانمساخ على بعضه جاز على كله، لثبوت الموافقة من جميع الوجوه بين جميع أجزائه فما جاز على البعض جاز على الكل، لا أمان بكم من أن يتقلب جميع أجزاء القديم شياطين وأبالسة وهذا ظاهرا لفساده، إذ لو جاز هذا، لجاز انقلاب جميع الشياطين والأجسام القبيحة آلهة قديمة، وكل ذلك خارج عن قضية العقول
And it is said to the Musabbiha among them: What you claim of a part of Him being transformed is impossible from several aspects. One of them is affirming division and partition, and that is impossible for the Eternal, as we will explain in refuting the saying of the Mujassima when we reach it, God willing. The second is that the saying of change in the Eternal is impossible, as we will also explain after this, God willing. Then we say to them: Since transformation of a part of Him is possible, it is possible for the whole of Him, due to the sameness in all respects among all His parts. So what is possible for a part is possible for the whole. There is no safety for you from the possibility that all parts of the Eternal might turn into devils and demons. This is clearly corrupt, because if this were possible, it would be possible for all devils and ugly bodies to turn into ancient deities, and all of that is outside the ruling of intellects.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Musabbiha (anthropomorphists) who claim that a part of God can undergo transformation (tamassukh). The author presents three arguments. First, transformation would require affirming division (taba'ud) and partition (tajazzu'), which is impossible for the Eternal (al-qadim). Second, change (taghayyur) in the Eternal is impossible. Third, if a part could change, then by parity of reasoning the whole could change, leading to the absurd consequence that all parts could become devils and demons, or even that devils could become deities. Each step relies on the impossibility of composition and change in God, which will be explained in later sections on refuting the Mujassima (anthropomorphists). The passage concludes that such possibilities are outside the judgment of sound reason.

Key Terms
المسخية
التبعض
التجزؤ
القديم
الانمساخ
المجسمة
الشياطين
الأبالسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that denying change in God contradicts His attribute of will.
Correction: The focus here is on essential change (taghayyur dhati), not voluntary action. God's will does not imply change in His essence.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason given for the impossibility of transformation (tamassukh) in God?
A) It would imply that God has parts and is divisible.
B) It would imply that God is composed of atoms.
C) It would contradict God's omnipotence.
D) It would mean God can change His essence.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God can be transformed is rationally impossible because it implies division, change, and leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God could change, would you still be able to rely on His promises with absolute certainty? How does the impossibility of change in God anchor your trust in Him today?
?
When you think about God, do you ever catch yourself picturing Him in human terms? What specific thought or image will you replace with the understanding that He is beyond all change and composition?
?
The absurdity of devils becoming deities is obvious to reason. Yet, we sometimes speak of God in ways that imply change or need. What is one phrase you will consciously avoid to guard His transcendence in your speech?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
Refuting the Objection: Act vs. Effect and Praiseworthy Consequence
Nasafi refutes the analogy between witnessed and unseen, distinguishing act from effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the objection that God's creation of evil implies evil in God by distinguishing between an act and its effect, and by introducing the concept of a praiseworthy consequence.

Core Idea
Evil in creation does not reflect on the Creator when it has a higher purpose.

Before Nasafi presents the four wisdoms behind evil, he first dismantles the logical analogy that evil in the world makes God evil. He shows that the objection relies on a false equivalence between human acts and divine creation, and that even human acts are only judged evil if they lack a praiseworthy outcome. This lesson walks through that initial refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the opponents begin by claiming self-evident knowledge, then pivot to analogy from the witnessed world.
Pay attention to the dialectical style.
2
Identify the key distinctions
Nasafi makes two crucial distinctions: (1) between the act (fi'l) and the effect (maf'ul), and (2) between acts with and without a praiseworthy consequence ('aqiba hamida). Find these in the text.
3
Analyze the refutation of ignorance
The opponents claim they see no wisdom; Nasafi replies that ignorance does not prove non-existence. Understand why this is a powerful rebuttal.
This sets up the need for the positive wisdoms that follow.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[الحكمة في إيجاد الشرور]
أحدها: أن الله تعالى لما خلق الخلق وأمرهم ونهاهم، ووعد لهم وأوعد، والوعد والترغيب بما لا يعلم لذته، والإيعاد بما لا يعلم ألمه غير مفيد، فخلق الضار والنافع ليصح الوعد والإيعاد والترغيب والترهيب والله الموفق. والثاني: أنه خلق العالم مختلف الأجزاء متفاوت الأبعاض، متباين الأفعال والآثار من نافع وضار ومستحسن ومستقبح حار وبارد وخشن ولين وغير ذلك، ليستدل بذلك على كمال قدرته ونفاذ مشيئته وسلطانه، إذ من فعله على جهة واحدة فهو كالمطبوع المسخر على ذلك كالنار على التسخين والثلج على التبريد، فيكون في خلقه المختلفات (۱) إقامة دلالة كمال القدرة ونفاذ المشيئة وهذه عاقبة حميدة، فكان خلق الكل حكمة وكذا لما اجتمعت هذه الأجسام المختلفة على الدلالة على أن لها صانعا حكيما لا يسفه، عالما لا يجهل قادرا (۲) لا يعجز (۳) كانت دليل كمال القدرة(4) وتمام التدبير ونفاذ المشيئة، حيث جعل المتضادات والمختلفات بالجوهر متجانسا في حق الشهادة على ربوبيته وحكمته وكمال قدرته.
والثالث : أنه خلق الأجسام الضارة والأوجاع والآلام() ليذل(1) بها الجبابرة والملوك، ليعلموا بذلك ضعفهم، فلا يفتروا بكثرة العدة والعتاد، واجتماع الجنود والعساكر، والأتباع والحواشي، فيصير ذلك مبعثة لهم على المسارعة إلى طاعته، ومزجرة عن الانهماك في معاصيه وداعيا إلى إفاضة العدل فيمن تحت أيديهم من الرعايا، وصادًا لهم عن الجور والظلم على من في ممالكهم من أصناف البرايا.
والرابع: أنه خلق هذه الأشياء الضارة المستقذرة ليعرف من رآها أنه تعالى منشئ العالم غني أن ينتفع بشيء أو يحتاج إلى شيء ) (۷) إذ من ذلك وصفه لا
Knowledge of self-evident truths: we and all who support us in the oneness of the Creator do not know this. If they say: 'You know that, but you deny it,' it is said to them: 'How are you distinguished from one who says to you: You know the falsity of your statement and the correctness of your opponents' statement by self-evidence, but you obstinately resist?' If they say: 'We know that by inference,' they are asked to produce the proof. If they say: 'In the witnessed world, everyone who brings into existence evil is evil (1) and whoever brings into existence an ugly act is foolish,' we say: 'Why, if it is so in the witnessed world, should it be so in the unseen? And in what sense do you analogize between the witnessed and the unseen? Moreover, this is denied: that in the witnessed world it is as they claim, for in the witnessed world, whoever's act is evil and ugly is evil and foolish. But as for one whose effect (produced thing) is so, it is not; then he is evil and foolish. In the disputed case, what is evil and ugly is an effect (maf'ul) of God, not His act (fi'l) and bringing into existence. Then in the witnessed world, an act is only foolish, ugly, evil if it has no praiseworthy consequence. But what has a praiseworthy consequence (3) is not evil, foolish, or ugly. So why do you say that God's bringing these things into existence has no praiseworthy consequence?' If they say: 'We judged these things to be foolish because we do not know any wisdom in them, and we have not come to know the aspect of wisdom,' it is said to them: 'Is everything you do not know non-existent?' If they say: 'Yes,' their contumacy appears and their obstinacy becomes clear. If they say: 'It may be that which we do not know,' it is said to them: 'What prevents you from acknowledging that God, in bringing that into existence, has a wisdom that your intellects cannot grasp, because you are incapable of comprehending all human wisdoms, so how do you claim to comprehend all divine wisdoms?' Then we say to them: 'In bringing these things into existence there is wisdom from several aspects: [The wisdom in the existence of evils] One: that when God created creatures, commanded and forbade them, promised them and threatened them, and the promise and encouragement by what is not known to be pleasurable, and the threat and deterrence by what is not known to be painful, are ineffective. So He created the harmful and the beneficial to make the promise, threat, encouragement, and deterrence valid. And God is the granter of success. Second: that He created the world with different parts, varying in components, different in actions and effects — beneficial and harmful, good and bad, hot and cold, rough and smooth, etc. — so that by that one may infer the perfection of His power, the effectiveness of His will, and His dominion. For one whose action is uniform is like one compelled and subjugated to that, like fire for heating and snow for cooling. So in creating diverse things (1) there is an establishment of evidence of perfect power and effective will, and this is a praiseworthy consequence. Thus creation of all is wisdom. Also, when these diverse bodies unite in indicating that they have a wise Maker who is not foolish, knowing who is not ignorant, powerful (2) who is not incapable (3), they become proof of perfect power (4), complete management, and effective will, since He made opposites and diversities in substance homogeneous in witnessing His Lordship, Wisdom, and Perfect Power. Third: that He created harmful bodies, pains, and sufferings ( ) to humble (1) tyrants and kings by them, so that they may know thereby their weakness, and not exult in abundance of numbers, equipment, soldiers, armies, followers, and attendants. Thus that becomes a stimulus for them to hasten to His obedience, a deterrent from indulging in His disobedience, a call to administer justice among those under their rule, and a restraint from oppression and injustice toward the various kinds of people in their kingdoms. Fourth: that He created these harmful, disgusting things so that whoever sees them may know that He, exalted, is the originator of the world, independent of benefiting from anything or needing anything (7). For from that His description is not...
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that the existence of evil in the world proves that God is evil or foolish. The author first addresses the claim that knowledge of self-evident truths (badihiyyat) does not include the thesis defended. He then presents a dialogue: the opponents argue from analogy with the witnessed world (shahid) where one who causes evil is evil. The author replies by making several distinctions: (1) in the witnessed world, the evil attributed to a person is based on the agent’s act (fi‘l), not the effect (maf‘ul); here the evil is God’s effect, not His act. (2) An act is only considered foolish or evil if it has no praiseworthy consequence (‘aqiba hamida); since God’s acts have such consequences, they are not evil. (3) The opponents’ claim that they see no wisdom in these evils is refuted: ignorance of wisdom does not prove its absence, for human intellects are limited. Then the author provides four specific wisdoms (hikam) behind the existence of evil:

1. To make divine promises and threats effective: reward and punishment require experiential knowledge of pleasure and pain, so God created beneficial and harmful things to make Paradise and Hell meaningful.
2. To demonstrate perfect power and will: the diversity in creation (opposites, different properties) shows that the Creator is not constrained like natural forces, but acts freely with full power and wisdom. This diversity proves perfection of power, will, and wisdom.
3. To humble tyrants and kings: by inflicting pain and harm, God reminds the mighty of their weakness, deterring them from arrogance and injustice, and encouraging justice and obedience.
4. To show God’s independence: by creating disgusting and harmful things, God demonstrates that He is in need of nothing and does not act for personal benefit, unlike creatures who seek benefit or avoid harm.

The passage ends mid-sentence with the fourth wisdom, indicating a continuation on the next page.

Key Terms
بديهيات
شاهد
غائب
فعل
مفعول
عاقبة حميدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the existence of evil in the world necessarily implies that God is evil or foolish.
Correction: Evil in the world is an effect of God, not an act; and it serves a praiseworthy consequence, so the analogy from human acts fails.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is the analogy from the witnessed world invalid?
A) Because the witnessed world is completely different from the unseen
B) Because in the witnessed world, evil is attributed to the effect, not the act
C) Because God gave humans free will
D) Because evil is an illusion

Key Takeaway
Evil in creation is not evil in the Creator, because it has a praiseworthy consequence and is an effect, not an act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you suffer a trial that you cannot understand, do you genuinely trust that God's wisdom is hidden, or does your heart accuse Him of injustice? How would your response change if you truly believed that every evil has a praiseworthy consequence?
?
Think of a time you hesitated to obey God because the command seemed 'ugly' or 'foolish' in your eyes. What does this lesson say about your limited perspective, and how can you surrender your judgment to His wisdom starting today?
?
If you are honest, do you sometimes use your ignorance of God's purposes as an excuse to doubt His goodness? What would it cost you to say, 'I do not know the wisdom, but I know the All-Wise' — and then live accordingly?

Four Divine Wisdoms Behind the Creation of Evils
Nasafi enumerates four wisdoms for the creation of evils.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four specific wisdoms Nasafi gives for why God created evil and harmful things.

Core Idea
Every apparent evil in creation serves a higher divine purpose.

Having refuted the logical objection, Nasafi now provides positive reasons for the existence of evil. These four wisdoms show that evil is not arbitrary but integral to God's plan, serving purposes from making reward and punishment meaningful to demonstrating God's perfect power and independence.

Guided Path
1
Read the first wisdom
Read the first wisdom about making promises and threats effective. Notice how the creation of harmful and beneficial things enables meaningful reward and punishment.
This wisdom relates to moral accountability.
2
Read the second wisdom
Read the second wisdom about demonstrating perfect power through diversity. Understand how opposites and variations in creation prove that the Creator is free and powerful, not constrained like natural forces.
3
Read the third wisdom
Read the third wisdom about humbling tyrants. Reflect on how pain and suffering can serve a social and moral purpose.
4
Read the fourth wisdom
Read the fourth wisdom about showing God's independence. Note that the passage is incomplete but the point is clear: creation of harmful things shows God needs nothing.
The text cuts off, but the lesson is clear.

Then the author provides four specific wisdoms (hikam) behind the existence of evil:

1. To make divine promises and threats effective: reward and punishment require experiential knowledge of pleasure and pain, so God created beneficial and harmful things to make Paradise and Hell meaningful.
2. To demonstrate perfect power and will: the diversity in creation (opposites, different properties) shows that the Creator is not constrained like natural forces, but acts freely with full power and wisdom. This diversity proves perfection of power, will, and wisdom.
3. To humble tyrants and kings: by inflicting pain and harm, God reminds the mighty of their weakness, deterring them from arrogance and injustice, and encouraging justice and obedience.
4. To show God’s independence: by creating disgusting and harmful things, God demonstrates that He is in need of nothing and does not act for personal benefit, unlike creatures who seek benefit or avoid harm.

The passage ends mid-sentence with the fourth wisdom, indicating a continuation on the next page.

Key Terms
حكمة
وعد
إيعاد
قدرة
مشيئة
جبابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That evil exists without any purpose.
Correction: Nasafi provides four specific wisdoms showing that every evil serves a meaningful divine purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the third wisdom, why did God create harmful bodies and pains?
A) To punish sinners directly
B) To humble tyrants and kings, reminding them of their weakness
C) To prove that God is not all-powerful
D) To show that evil has no purpose

Key Takeaway
God creates evil for wise purposes, such as enabling moral accountability, demonstrating power, humbling tyrants, and showing divine independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful trial, do you truly believe it serves a higher wisdom from God, or do you secretly resent Him for allowing it? How can you cultivate that trust today?
?
The passage says harmful things humble tyrants. What 'tyrant' inside you—pride, arrogance, or desire for control—needs to be humbled by God's creation of difficulty? Will you let this lesson break your pride?
?
If you saw a disgusting or harmful creature, would you immediately remember that God is independent of all needs, or would you judge it as pointless? How can you change your instant reaction to see divine wisdom in everything?
