Defining Accident: Linguistic and Technical Meanings
Al-Nasafi defines accident linguistically and technically, emphasizing impossibility of persistence as the essential meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of an accident (arad) in Islamic theology, and why impossibility of persistence is the essential characteristic.

Core Idea
An accident is primarily defined by its inability to persist, not by its dependence on a substrate.

Before investigating whether God is an accident, al-Nasafi carefully defines the term. He draws from language and then refines it for technical use, showing that the core meaning is impermanence, not merely being dependent.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage where al-Nasafi defines accident. Notice how he starts with language, then moves to technical definition.
Pay attention to the words 'arad' versus 'arid'.
2
Identify key characteristics
The passage mentions two characteristics: not self-subsistent and not persisting. Determine which is the defining one.
Al-Nasafi explicitly says 'al-arad is what cannot persist'.
3
Understand the reasoning
Why does al-Nasafi emphasize impossibility of persistence over dependence? Think about the linguistic origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - الكلام في نفي كون الباري جل وعلا عرضا
وإذا ثبت أن العالم له صانع، وثبت أنه واحد، وأنه قديم، فبعد ذلك ينبغي أن يبحث ويتأمل أن ذاته هل هو عرض أم ليس بعرض؟ فبحثنا وتأملنا في العرض، فوجدناه استحق اسم العرض لاستحالة بقائه إذ العارض في اللغة هو ما لا يدوم، ويقال: عرض لفلان أمر، أي معنى لإقرار له ويقال: هذه الحالة ليست بأصلية في كذا، بل هي عارضة له أي هي أمر لا دوام له. أمر لا دوام له. ولهذا سمي عارضا على ما بينا. ثم رأينا أشياء لا بقاء لها فسميناها أعراضا، فإذا العرض ما يستحيل بقاؤه في عرف المتكلمين، وهذا الاسم له، مأخوذ من الدلالة اللغوية، ثم وجدنا كل عرض له وصف لازم لا يزايله ويستحيل وجوده غير موصوف به، وهو كونه مما يستحيل قيامه بذاته، ويفتقر إلى قائم بذات يقوم به، ثم هو ما كان عرضا لكونه غیر قائم بذاته إذ ليس فيه ما ينبئ عن معنى كونه عرضا في اللغة بل كان عرضا لاستحالة بقائه.

وظنت المعتزلة والكرامية حيث رأوا كل عرض غير قائم بذاته في الشاهد أنه كان عرضا لهذا الوصف، ثم تفرع لهما عن هذا الاتفاق المبني على الخيال مذهبان باطلان إذ الباطل لا ينتج إلا الباطل، فأما المعتزلة فإنهم يزعمون أن الله تعالى لو كانت له صفات أزلية كالعلم والقدرة والحياة، لكانت أعراضا، لاستحالة قيام هذه الصفات بنفسها، وقيام العرض بذات الله تعالى محال. وكذا وجود الأعراض في الأزل ممتنع، فإذا لا صفة الله تعالى، إذ لو كانت لكانت عرضا، وأما الكرامية فإنهم لما عرفوا ثبوت هذه الصفات الله تعالى بالدليل الضروري، وعلموا
2 - Discussion on negating that the Creator, exalted and majestic, is an accident. And when it is established that the world has a Maker, and it is established that He is one and that He is eternal, then after that it is fitting to investigate and consider: is His essence an accident or not an accident? So we investigated and considered the accident (al-'arad), and we found that it deserves the name 'accident' due to the impossibility of its persistence, because al-'arid in language is that which does not endure. And it is said: 'an accident occurred to so-and-so,' i.e., a meaning that has no permanence, and it is said: 'this state is not original in such-and-such, but rather it is accidental to it,' i.e., it is something that has no duration. And for this reason it is called 'arid as we explained. Then we observed things that have no permanence and we called them a'rad (accidents). So al-'arad is what cannot persist in the terminology of the mutakallimun, and this name is derived from the linguistic indication. Then we found that every accident has a necessary attribute that does not leave it and it is impossible for it to exist without being described by it, and that is that it is something that cannot subsist by itself (qa'im bi-dhatihi) and is in need of a self-subsistent entity in which it subsists. Then it is an accident because it is not self-subsistent, since there is nothing in it that indicates the meaning of being an accident in language, rather it is an accident due to the impossibility of its persistence.

And the Mu'tazila and the Karramiyya thought, when they saw that every accident is not self-subsistent in the witnessed world, that it was an accident due to this description. Then there branched from this agreement based on imagination two false doctrines, for falsehood produces only falsehood. As for the Mu'tazila, they claim that if God had eternal attributes like knowledge, power, and life, they would be accidents, because it is impossible for these attributes to subsist by themselves, and the subsistence of an accident in the essence of God is impossible. Similarly, the existence of accidents in pre-eternity is impossible. So they say there is no attribute of God, for if there were, it would be an accident. As for the Karramiyya, when they recognized the establishment of these attributes of God by necessary proof, and they knew
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on negating that God is an accident (arad). The author first establishes that after proving God's existence, oneness, and eternity, one must investigate whether His essence is an accident. He then defines accident linguistically and technically. Linguistically, an accident (arid) is something that does not endure (la yadum). Technically, in the terminology of the mutakallimun, an accident (arad) is that which cannot persist (mustahil al-baqa'). Additionally, every accident has two necessary characteristics: it cannot subsist by itself (laysa qa'iman bi-dhatihi) and it is in need of a self-subsistent substrate in which to subsist. The reason something is an accident is ultimately the impossibility of its persistence, not just its dependence on a substrate.

Having defined accident, the author then refutes two sects, the Mu'tazila and the Karramiyya, who mistakenly applied the concept of accident to God's attributes. The Mu'tazila argued that if God had eternal attributes, those attributes would be accidents because they cannot subsist by themselves, and since accidents cannot subsist in God nor exist in pre-eternity, God has no attributes. The Karramiyya accepted the existence of God's attributes by necessary proof but then considered them accidents because they are not self-subsistent. The author dismisses both positions as based on imagination (khayal) and false, because the definition of accident includes impossibility of persistence, and God's attributes are eternal (azali), not temporal. The passage is incomplete but strongly establishes the falsehood of both views.

Key Terms
عرض
عارض
استحالة البقاء
قائم بذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accident is defined by not being self-subsistent.
Correction: The primary definition is impossibility of persistence; not being self-subsistent is a necessary characteristic but not the defining one.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what is the primary defining characteristic of an accident (arad) in the terminology of the mutakallimun?
A) It is not self-subsistent.
B) It cannot persist.
C) It is created in time.
D) It does not have an essence.

Key Takeaway
An accident is fundamentally defined by its impossibility of persistence, a meaning rooted in language and refined by theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says an accident is something that does not persist. Think about one thing in your life that you rely on heavily—a relationship, a possession, a skill. If that thing is truly an accident, it cannot persist. How does that truth sit with you now, and what will you do to anchor your hope in something that does persist, namely Allah?
?
Al-Nasafi emphasizes that the essence of an accident is its impermanence, not its dependence. Where in your life have you mistaken dependence as the main issue, when really the deeper problem is that you are clinging to something fleeting? Name that thing and decide today to shift your attachment from the fleeting to the Eternal.
?
If everything that is impermanent is an accident, and Allah is not an accident, then every good thing you experience that is temporary is a gift from the Self-Subsistent. When you enjoy a moment of peace or happiness, do you truly see it as a loan that will return to its Owner, or do you grasp it as if it is yours to keep? What one change can you make today to receive blessings with open hands, knowing they will not last?

The False Doctrines of Mu'tazila and Karramiyya on Divine Attributes
Al-Nasafi presents and refutes the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya positions on divine attributes as accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila and Karramiyya mistakenly applied the definition of accident to God's attributes, and why their positions are false.

Core Idea
Both sects started from a flawed premise about accidents and built contradictory errors.

Having defined accident, al-Nasafi now shows how two major sects misused this definition. They agreed that accident = not self-subsistent, but then drew opposite conclusions about God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the sectarian claims
Read the passage where al-Nasafi describes the positions of Mu'tazila and Karramiyya. Identify the common premise they share.
Both base their argument on the concept of 'not self-subsistent'.
2
Compare the two positions
Mu'tazila denied attributes, Karramiyya affirmed them as accidents. Note how the same premise leads to different outcomes.
Al-Nasafi calls them both 'false'.
3
Understand the refutation
Although the passage is cut off, al-Nasafi declares both doctrines false because they are built on a mistaken understanding of accident. The key is that accidents are defined by impermanence, not just dependence.
Think: God's attributes are eternal, so they cannot be accidents.

Why Allah Cannot Be Described as a Jawhar
Refutes the claim that Allah is a jawhar and mentions Ibn Karram's agreement with Christians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological argument against describing Allah as a jawhar (substance/atom) and why this claim contradicts Muslim consensus.

Core Idea
Allah cannot be a jawhar because that would imply createdness and composition.

The claim that Allah is a jawhar (substance) was made by some groups, including Christians and, remarkably, Ibn Karram. In this passage, the author systematically refutes this notion by first establishing that Allah is not an 'arad (accident), then showing that the only remaining category for created entities is the 'ayn (entity), which is further divided into jawhar and jism (body). Since jawhar in the witnessed realm is defined as self-subsisting but contingent, it cannot apply to the eternal Creator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification of existents
Read the passage and identify the two categories of existents in the witnessed realm: 'arad and 'ayn. Note the definitions given for each.
The author uses the witnessed realm (shahid) as a basis for analogy.
2
Follow the negation of 'arad for Allah
See how the author argues that Allah cannot be an 'arad because an 'arad cannot persist and depends on a substrate, whereas Allah is eternal and self-subsisting.
3
Examine the division of 'ayn into jawhar and jism
Find the two subdivisions: non-composite (jawhar) and composite (jism). Understand that both are contingent in the witnessed realm.
4
Conclude the impossibility of jawhar for Allah
The author states that describing Allah as jawhar is impossible and prohibited. Ask yourself why this is, based on the definitions provided.
Consider that jawhar in the shahid implies being a substrate for accidents and being finite, which contradict divine attributes.
5
Note the critique of Ibn Karram
Read the mention of Ibn Karram's view and the author's condemnation. Recognize that this is presented as a departure from Muslim consensus.
This historical note reinforces the gravity of the theological error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال قول من يقول : إن الباري جل وعز جوهر: وإذا ثبت استحالة كون الباري عرضا ، فبعد ذلك تأملنا فوجدنا الموجود في الشاهد، ينقسم إلى ما يستحيل بقاؤه ويمتنع قيامه بذاته وهو العرض، وإلى ما يستحيل بقاؤه، ولا يمتنع قيامه بذاته وهو العين واستحال كونه عرضا لما مر ، فعلمنا ضرورة استحالة اتصافه بكونه عرضا أنه باق، غير مفتقر إلى مكان، ثم رأينا أن ما وراء العرض في الشاهد هو العين، وهو ينقسم إلى غير متركب، وهو المسمى عند المتكلمين جوهر، وإلى متركب وهو المسمى جسما فتأملنا أنه هل يوصف بكونه جوهرا أم لا، فوجدنا اتصافه بكونه جوهرا محالا ممتنعا. وزعمت النصارى أنه تعالى جوهر. ولم يساعدهم أحد من أهل قبلتنا إلا ابن كرام، فإن الأسفراييني ذكر في باب فضائح الكرامية من كتابه المسمى بتعجيز المعتزلة، أن ابن كرام ذكر في كتابه الملقب بعذاب القبر، في وصف ذات الله تعالى، أنه أحدى الذات إحدى الجوهر، وهذا من ابن كرام خروج عن إجماع المسلمين، والتحاق بالنصارى
The discussion on invalidating the statement of those who say that the Creator, may He be glorified and exalted, is a jawhar (substance/atom): And when the impossibility of the Creator being an 'arad (accident) is established, then after that we considered and found that the existent in the witnessed realm (shahid) is divided into that whose permanence is impossible and whose subsistence by itself is impossible, which is the 'arad, and into that whose permanence is impossible but whose subsistence by itself is not impossible, which is the 'ayn (entity). And it is impossible for Him to be an 'arad for what has passed, so we necessarily know the impossibility of His being described as an 'arad, that He is eternal, not in need of place. Then we saw that what is beyond the 'arad in the witnessed realm is the 'ayn, and it is divided into what is not composed, which is called (by the theologians) jawhar, and into what is composed, which is called a body (jism). So we considered whether He is described as being a jawhar or not, and we found that His being described as being a jawhar is impossible and prohibited. And the Christians claimed that He, exalted, is a jawhar. And no one from our people (ahl qiblatina) assisted them except Ibn Karram. For al-Isfara'ini mentioned in the chapter on the scandals of the Karramiyya from his book called Ta'jiz al-Mu'tazila, that Ibn Karram mentioned in his book named 'Adhab al-Qabr, in describing the essence of Allah, that He is one in essence, one in jawhar. And this from Ibn Karram is a departure from the consensus of the Muslims, and an alignment with the Christians.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that Allah is a jawhar (substance/atom). The author begins by stating the purpose: to invalidate the statement of those who say the Creator is a jawhar. He builds the argument by first establishing that Allah cannot be an 'arad (accident), then dividing the existents in the witnessed realm (shahid) into two categories: 'arad and 'ayn. The 'arad is that which cannot persist and cannot subsist by itself, while the 'ayn is that which cannot persist but can subsist by itself. Since Allah is eternal and not in need of place, He cannot be an 'arad. The author then looks beyond the 'arad to the 'ayn, which is further divided into the non-composite, called jawhar by the theologians, and the composite, called jism (body). He then asks whether Allah can be described as jawhar and finds it impossible and prohibited. He then mentions that the Christians claimed Allah is a jawhar, and no one from the Muslims (ahl qiblatina) supported them except Ibn Karram, citing al-Isfara'ini's work to show that Ibn Karram described Allah as 'one in essence, one in jawhar', which is a departure from Muslim consensus and an alignment with Christians.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
عرض ('arad)
عين ('ayn)
جسم (jism)
ابن كرام (Ibn Karram)
شاهد (shahid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that describing Allah as a 'jawhar' is a neutral or even reverent term, similar to saying 'substance' in philosophy.
Correction: The author shows that 'jawhar' in the Islamic theological tradition implies contingency, composition, and being a substrate for accidents, all of which are incompatible with the divine nature.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for Allah to be described as a jawhar (substance)?
A) Because the jawhar is composed, unlike Allah.
B) Because the jawhar is defined as qaim bidhat (self-subsisting) and in the shahid this implies contingency.
C) Because the jawhar is a type of 'arad (accident).
D) Because the jawhar is confined to the witnessed realm and Allah is transcendent.

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be described as a jawhar because the term entails contingency and composition, which are incompatible with His eternal and transcendent nature.

How seriously do you take the obligation to safeguard Allah's transcendence in your daily conversations? In what situation this week can you consciously guard your tongue from saying anything that implies deficiency in Allah?

Refuting the Claim that God is a Jawhar (Substance)
Nasafi presents and refutes arguments that God is a jawhar by clarifying the term's linguistic sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'jawhar' (substance) cannot be applied to God, based on its linguistic meaning and the impossibility of God being an origin for composition.

Core Idea
The term 'jawhar' means 'origin' for composites, and God is not composed.

Opponents used logical arguments to claim that God is a jawhar (substance). The author refutes them not by rejecting the logic but by clarifying that the term 'jawhar' has a specific linguistic meaning related to material origins. This lesson examines their arguments and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two arguments presented by the opponents.
Look for the phrases 'موجود' and 'فاعل' as key premises.
2
Identify the opponents' syllogisms
Find the two arguments: one from the division of existents into substance/accident, and the other from the property of being an agent.
The first argument: everything existent is either jawhar or 'arad; God not 'arad → God is jawhar. The second: only jawhar can act in the witnessed world; God acts → God is jawhar.
3
Understand the refutation via linguistic meaning
The author explains that 'jawhar' in Arabic means 'origin' (asl). He gives examples: a noble person has a noble jawhar; a high-quality garment is called 'jawhari'. This shows the term is used for what serves as an origin, not for God.
Notice the examples: 'فلان يجري في الإحسان على شاكلة جوهره الشريف' and 'ثوب جوهري'.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since 'jawhar' is a term for indivisible parts that are origins of composites, and God cannot be composed, He cannot be called a jawhar in this sense.
The refutation does not deny the logical form of the opponents' arguments but shows their misuse of the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتعلقون به أن الله تعالى موجود والموجود إما أن يكون جوهرا وإما أن يكون عرضا والله تعالى ليس بعرض، فيكون جوهرا إذ لو لم يكن جوهرا مع أنه ليس بعرض لما كان موجودا لأن الموجود ينقسم إلى قسمين، وما انقسم إلى قسمين فما خرج عن قسميه جميعا لا يكون من جملته، فلو خرج الباري جل وعلا عن القسمين جميعا لما كان موجودا، ويقولون أيضا: إن الله تعالى فاعل، وما يجوز منه الفعل في الشاهد فهو جوهر، وما يستحيل منه الفعل فهو عرض، وقد تحقق من الله تعالى الفعل فكان جوهرا.
الرد على النصارى وعلى الكرامية في قولهم: إنه تعالى جوهر
وحجتنا أن الجوهر في اللغة عبارة عن الأصل، يقال لمن اشتهر بالإحسان من أبناء الشرف والسيادة فلان يجري في الإحسان على شاكلة جوهره الشريف ومقتضى حسبه العالي المنيف، ويقال للثوب إذا كان محكم الصنعة جيد الأصل: إنه ثوب جوهري، فعلى هذا سموا ما لا يتجزأ من أجزاء الجسم جوهرا لكون البسائط التي يتركب منها المتركبات جارية مجرى الأصول لها، لا يعنون به أنه أصله القديم كما يذهب إليه أهل الدهر، بل يريدون أن كل متركب يقدر في الوهم أنه من الأفراد تركب، فيكون الأفراد أصولا وأركانا في المركبات، ولتصوير الأفراد بدون التركب، واستحالة المتركبات بدون الأفراد التي تركب هي منها. والله سبحانه وتعالى يستحيل تركبه إلى غيره ليصير أجساما فلم يكن أصلا يتركب
And among what they adduce is that God most high is existent, and the existent is either a substance (jawhar) or an accident ('arad), and God most high is not an accident, so He is a substance; for if He were not a substance while He is not an accident, He would not be existent, because the existent is divided into two divisions, and what is divided into two divisions, whatever falls outside both of its divisions is not among its entirety. So if the Glorious and Exalted fell outside both divisions, He would not be existent. And they also say: God most high is an agent (fa'il), and whatever from which action is permissible in the witnessed world is a substance, and whatever from which action is impossible is an accident, and the action from God most high is realized, so He is a substance.
The refutation of the Christians and the Karramiyya regarding their statement: He most high is a substance.
And our proof is that 'jawhar' in language is an expression for the origin (asl). It is said of one who is famous for beneficence among the sons of nobility and lordship: 'So-and-so proceeds in beneficence according to his noble essence (jawhar) and the requirement of his exalted and lofty lineage.' And it is said of a garment, if it is firmly made, good in origin: 'It is a substantial (jawhari) garment.' On this basis, they called that which is indivisible from the parts of a body 'jawhar' because the simple elements (basa'it) from which composites are composed run in the course of origins for them. They do not mean by it that it is its eternal origin as the materialists (ahl al-dahr) hold, but rather they intend that every composite is imagined in the mind to be composed of individuals (afrad), so the individuals are origins and pillars in the composites, and [they intend] the conceivability of individuals without composition, and the impossibility of composites without the individuals from which they are composed. And God, glorified and exalted, is impossible to be composed into another to become bodies, so He was not an origin for composition.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments used by opponents (Christians and Karramiyya) to claim that God is a jawhar (substance), followed by the author's refutation based on the linguistic meaning of 'jawhar'. The first argument is from the division of existence: everything existent is either a substance or an accident; God is not an accident, therefore He must be a substance. The second argument is from action: in the witnessed world, whatever can perform action is a substance; God performs actions, so He is a substance. Then the author begins the refutation by clarifying that 'jawhar' in the Arabic language originally means 'origin' (asl). He gives examples: a noble person is said to have a noble jawhar, and a high-quality garment is called 'jawhari'. On this basis, the term was used for indivisible particles of a body because they serve as origins for composites. The author emphasizes that this does not imply an eternal origin as materialists claim; rather, it simply means that composites are imagined to be composed of individuals, and those individuals are origins and pillars. Since God cannot be composed into bodies, He cannot be an origin for composition, and therefore the term 'jawhar' does not apply to Him in this sense. The refutation thus denies that the technical term 'jawhar' can be predicated of God, even if the opponents' syllogisms seem valid, because the term's meaning is restricted to created material origins.

Key Terms
جوهر (jawhar)
عرض ('arad)
فاعل (fa'il)
أصل (asl)
أفراد (afrad)
بسائط (basa'it)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'jawhar' is a universal category that includes God, just as it includes created substances.
Correction: The term 'jawhar' is a technical term for material origins; God is not material and not composed, so He cannot be described as jawhar.

Checkpoint
On what basis does the author refute the claim that God is a jawhar?
A) By denying that God is existent.
B) By arguing that 'jawhar' in language means 'origin' and God cannot be an origin for composition.
C) By accepting that God is an accident.
D) By proving that the division of existents into substance and accident is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The term 'jawhar' refers to material origins, and since God is not composed, He cannot be described as a jawhar.

This lesson shows that even well-intentioned scholars can misapply language about God. How careful are you with the words you use when speaking of Him? Will you commit to guarding your tongue and heart from any description that implies composition or limitation?

Refuting the Divine Substance Argument
Nasafi refutes the substance argument by redefining substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God is a substance (jawhar) based on the shahid analogy is corrupt, and how the definitions of substance and accident invalidate the argument.

Core Idea
Substance is defined by being a principle of composition, not merely by not being an accident.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi responds to a common theological argument that because God exists and is not an accident, He must be a substance (jawhar). The argument seems simple, but Nasafi shows it is based on a misunderstanding of what 'substance' means in the created world. By carefully analyzing the definitions of substance and accident, he reveals the logical flaws and upholds divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense but follows a clear logical flow.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent argues: Everything existent is either substance or accident; God exists and is not an accident; therefore God is a substance. Note this premise and conclusion.
This is a classic qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid (analogical inference).
3
Understand the correction for 'substance'
Nasafi says substance is defined as 'a principle from which the body is composed' (asl yurakkabu minhu al-jism). Since God cannot be a principle of composition, He cannot be a substance. Existence alone does not make something a substance.
Contrast with the opponent's simplistic definition of substance as 'not an accident'.
4
Understand the correction for 'accident'
Accident is defined by the impossibility of permanence (istihalat al-dawam). God is necessarily everlasting, so He is not an accident. This also shows why substance is not accident: it persists.
Both definitions rely on properties of the witnessed world that cannot apply to God.
5
Grasp the final refutation and conclusion
Nasafi declares the entire division of existent into substance and accident corrupt because it is a 'division of the divided' (taqsim muqassam) – it fails to be comprehensive. He notes this answer only works for Ahl al-Haqq, not the Mu'tazila who permit such faulty definitions.
This shows the importance of sound definition in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجوهر، والله الموفق. وقولهم : إن الموجود فى الشاهد، إما أن يكون عرضا وإما أن يكون جوهرا والله تعالى موجود وليس بعرض فيكون جوهرا كلام فاسد، لأن الجوهر في الشاهد ما كان جوهرا لأنه ليس بعرض، بل كان جوهرا لأنه أصل يتركب منه الجسم، ولهذا لم يكن العرض جوهرا لأنه ليس بأصل يتركب منه الجسم، والله تعالى يستحيل عليه كونه أصلا يتركب منه الجسم فلم يكن جوهرا، وليس من ضرورة كونه موجودا كونه جوهرا، لأن العرض موجود وليس بجوهر، وكذا العرض لم يكن عرضا، لأنه موجود لأن الجوهر موجود وليس بعرض، بل كان عرضا لاستحالة دوامه والله تعالى لا يستحيل عليه الدوام، بل هو واجب البقاء فلم يكن عرضا ولهذا لم يكن الجوهر عرضا لأنه ليس بمستحيل البقاء وتحديد الموجود بأنه جوهر أو عرض فاسد، لأنه تحديد مقسم وهو فاسد، لأن جمع الوصفين جميعا في محل واحد محال، إذ يستحيل موجود جوهر وعرض، وإثبات كل وصف في محل على حده ليس بجامع، ومن شرط صحة الحد أن يكون جامعا على ما مر في إفساد كلام الجبائي في تحديده العلم أنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به عن ضرورة أو دليل، وهذا الجواب مما لا يستقيم إلا على أصول أهل الحق فأما المعتزلة المجوزون التحديد المقسم فلا يمكنهم الخروج عن إلزام النصارى، والله الموفق.
al-Jawhar, and God is the grantor of success. And their saying: That the existent in the witnessed [world] is either an accident or a substance, and God exists and is not an accident, therefore He is a substance — [this is] corrupt speech, because the substance in the witnessed [world] is a substance not because it is not an accident, but rather it is a substance because it is a principle from which the body is composed, and for this reason the accident is not a substance because it is not a principle from which the body is composed, and it is impossible for God to be a principle from which the body is composed, so He is not a substance. And it is not necessary from His existence that He be a substance, because the accident exists and is not a substance, and likewise the accident is an accident not because it exists (since the substance exists and is not an accident), but rather it is an accident due to the impossibility of its permanence, and permanence is not impossible for God, rather He is necessarily everlasting, so He is not an accident, and for this reason the substance is not an accident because it is not impossible to persist. And defining the existent as substance or accident is corrupt, because it is a division of the divided [term] and that is corrupt, because joining both descriptions in one locus is impossible, since an existent that is both substance and accident is impossible, and positing each description in a separate locus is not comprehensive, and from the conditions of a valid definition is that it be comprehensive, as mentioned in the refutation of al-Jubba'i's definition of knowledge as 'belief of a thing as it is from necessity or evidence'. And this answer is only valid according to the principles of the People of Truth; as for the Mu'tazila who permit division of the divided [term], they cannot escape the Christians' objection. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that God is a substance (jawhar) based on the division of existents into substance and accident in the witnessed world. The opponent argues: God exists, and He is not an accident, therefore He is a substance. Imam Nasafi begins by stating that this claim is corrupt. He explains that the definition of substance in the witnessed world is not merely 'not an accident'; rather, it is defined as a principle (asl) from which the body is composed. An accident is not a substance because it is not such a principle. Since it is impossible for God to be a principle of composition, He cannot be a substance. Existence does not necessitate substance, because accidents exist and are not substances. Similarly, an accident is not defined by existence but by the impossibility of its permanence (istihalat dawamihi). God is necessarily everlasting, so He is not an accident. Thus, the division of existent into substance and accident is faulty because it is a 'division of the divided' (taqsim muqassam), meaning the term 'existent' is being divided into substance and accident, but this division is not valid because it is not comprehensive (jami'). You cannot have an existent that is both substance and accident, nor can you have an existent that is neither? Actually the criticism is that the definition does not cover all cases properly. The author then refers to his earlier refutation of al-Jubba'i's definition of knowledge. He concludes that this answer only holds for the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq); the Mu'tazila who permit division of the divided cannot refute the Christians' objection.

عرض ('arad)
تحديد مقسم (taqsīm muqassam)
أصل (aṣl)
تركيب (tarkīb)
دوام (dawām)
أهل الحق (Ahl al-Ḥaqq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'not being an accident' automatically makes something a substance.
Correction: In classical kalam, substance is defined as a principle of composition (asl yurakkabu minhu al-jism), not merely by negation of accident.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi, why is it incorrect to say that God is a substance (jawhar) simply because He is existent and not an accident?
A) Because substance is defined as something that does not depend on a locus for existence.
B) Because substance in the witnessed world is defined as a principle from which bodies are composed, which cannot apply to God.
C) Because God is not subject to the division of substance and accident.
D) Because accident can also be a substance.

Key Takeaway
Defining theological terms using only shahid accidents misrepresents God; proper definitions must avoid applying composition or impermanence to the divine.

?

Refutation of the Claim that God is a Substance Because He is a Doer
Imam Nasafi refutes the analogical argument for God being a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogical argument from 'doer' to 'substance' fails, and how Imam Nasafi uses counter-examples and definitional analysis to refute it.

Core Idea
Being a doer does not prove something is a substance; inanimate objects are substances but not doers.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi responds to an argument that since God is a doer (fa'il) and every doer we see is a substance, God must be a substance. He dismantles this analogy by showing it leads to absurdities and by clarifying the true definition of substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the two distinct parts of the refutation: the counter-analogy and the direct definitional argument.
The passage is concise but logically dense.
2
Analyze the counter-analogy
Nasafi points out that every doer in the witnessed world is also a body (jism), flesh and blood, and finite. If the opponent follows the analogy, they must admit God is a body, which they deny. This shows the flaw in their reasoning.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Understand the definitional argument
Nasafi then explains that substance (jawhar) is not defined by being a doer, but as a principle (aṣl) from which bodies are composed. Many substances are not doers. This meaning does not extend to God, so He is not a substance.
This directly refutes the opponent's premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : إن الله تعالى فاعل وكل فاعل في الشاهد جوهر . قلنا : وكل فاعل في الشاهد أيضا جسم، فإنا ما عاينا جوهرا غير متركب يفعل فإن التزموا جعلوا الله تعالى جسما، وهو خلاف مذهبهم وإن لم يلتزموا ، أبطلوا دليلهم، وكذا كل فاعل في الشاهد لحم ودم ، وكل فاعل محدود متناه، فإن أثبتوا ذلك في الغائب لكونه فاعلا تركوا ،مذهبهم، وإن لم يثبتوا بطل دليلهم، ثم نقول لهم: لم يكن الجوهر في الشاهد جوهرا لأنه فاعل، بدليل أن الجماد والموات جوهر وليس بفاعل، بل كان جوهرا لكونه أصلا يتركب منه الأجسام، وهذا المعنى لا يتعدى إلى الغائب فلم يكن جوهرا، وبالله التوفيق.
And their saying: God is a doer (fa'il), and every doer in the witnessed [world] is a substance. We say: And every doer in the witnessed [world] is also a body, for we have not observed a non-composite substance performing an action. If they accept [that], they make God a body, which contradicts their doctrine; and if they do not accept [that], their proof is invalidated. Likewise, every doer in the witnessed [world] is flesh and blood, and every doer is limited and finite. If they affirm that for the absent [God] because He is a doer, they abandon their doctrine; and if they do not affirm [that], their proof is invalid. Then we say to them: The substance in the witnessed [world] is not a substance because it is a doer; the proof is that inanimate objects and dead things are substances and are not doers. Rather, it is a substance because it is a principle from which bodies are composed, and this meaning does not extend to the absent [God], so He is not a substance. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage refutes another analogical argument: the opponent claims that since God is a doer (fa'il) and every doer in the witnessed world is a substance, then God is a substance. Nasafi responds by pointing out that every doer we observe is also a body (jism), not just a substance. If the opponent accepts this, they must admit God is a body, which they deny. If they reject the analogy, their own argument collapses. He then gives further examples: every doer is flesh and blood, limited, etc., all of which cannot be applied to God. He then provides a direct refutation: substance in the witnessed world is not defined by being a doer, because many substances (like inanimate objects) are not doers. Rather, substance is defined as a principle of composition for bodies, and this does not apply to God.

جسم (jism)
جماد (jamād)

أصل (aṣl)
يتعدى (yata'addā)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that being a doer (fa'il) implies being a substance, because most doers in daily experience are objects that occupy space.
Correction: Nasafi shows that doership is not the defining quality of substance; inanimate substances are not doers, and substance is defined as a principle of composition.

Checkpoint

B) He argues that every doer in the witnessed world is also a body, so the analogy would imply God is a body.
C) He denies that God is a doer.
D) He says substances are not doers.

Key Takeaway
God's attribute of being a doer does not entail that He is a substance, because substance is defined by composition, not by action.

Refuting the Body Argument by Analogy
Nasafi presents a reductio against the analogy argument for God's bodiliness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the analogy from the witnessed realm fails to prove that God must be a body.

Core Idea
Analogy from the seen to the unseen is invalid when the attributes differ.

Nasafi begins by examining the claim that since every doer in the witnessed realm is a body, God, as a doer, must also be a body. He exposes the flaw in this reasoning by showing that the opponents either must contradict their own school or abandon their proof entirely. This lesson walks through that reductio argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: if X then Y, if not X then Z.
The passage uses a rhetorical style common in kalam.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents argue that because all doers in the witnessed realm are bodies, God must be a body. Find where Nasafi states this.
3
Trace the reductio
Nasafi gives two examples: bodies are composite, and all doers are limited/finite. For each, if the opponents affirm these for God, they contradict their own school; if not, their proof collapses.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Summarize the conclusion
The analogy from the witnessed realm is invalid because the attributes of doing do not necessarily imply bodiliness in the unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلا الشاهد أيضا جسم، فإنا ما عاينا جوهرا غير متركب يفعل فإن التزموا جعلوا الله تعالى جسما، وهو خلاف مذهبهم وإن لم يلتزموا، أبطلوا دليلهم، وكذا كل فاعل في الشاهد لحم ودم، وكل فاعل محدود متناه، فإن أثبتوا ذلك في الغائب لكونه فاعلا تركوا مذهبهم، وإن لم يثبتوا بطل دليلهم، ثم نقول لهم: لم يكن الجوهر في الشاهد جوهرا لأنه فاعل، بدليل أن الجماد والموات جوهر وليس بفاعل، بل كان جوهرا لكونه أصلا يتركب منه الأجسام، وهذا المعنى لا يتعدى إلى الغائب فلم يكن جوهرا، وبالله التوفيق.
The doer in the witnessed [realm] is also a body, for we have not observed a substance that is not composite acting. If they commit [to that], they make God a body, which is contrary to their school; and if they do not commit, their proof is invalidated. Likewise, every doer in the witnessed [realm] is flesh and blood, and every doer is limited and finite. If they affirm that for the unseen because it is a doer, they abandon their school; and if they do not affirm, their proof is invalidated. Then we say to them: The substance in the witnessed [realm] was not a substance because it is a doer, as evidenced by the fact that inanimate and dead things are substances but not doers. Rather, it was a substance because it is a principle from which bodies are composed, and this meaning does not extend to the unseen, so it is not a substance. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that attempts to prove that God must be a body because He acts, using analogy from the witnessed realm. The author begins by stating that in the witnessed realm, the doer is a body, but then argues that not every doer in the witnessed realm is a substance? Actually the argument here is a reductio: the opponents claim that if God is a doer, He must be a body. But then the author points out that in the witnessed realm, all doers are bodies, so by their logic, they must affirm that God is a body, which contradicts their own school. If they refuse to affirm that, their proof fails. Then the author makes a deeper point: the reason a substance is called a substance is not because it acts but because it serves as a substrate for composition. Inanimate objects are substances but do not act. Therefore, the attribute of being a doer is not essential to being a substance, so the analogy breaks down. The conclusion: God is not a substance.

Key Terms
شاهد (witnessed realm)
غائب (unseen realm)
فاعل (doer/agent)
جسم (body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That analogy from the witnessed realm to the unseen is always valid.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the analogy fails because the attributes of bodiliness cannot be transferred without contradiction.

Checkpoint
What happens if the opponents affirm that God is limited and finite based on the analogy?
A) They have proven their point.
B) They contradict their own school.
C) They must accept that God is a body but not limited.
D) Nasafi agrees with them.

Key Takeaway
The analogy from the witnessed realm cannot prove God is a body because it would force contradictions or invalidate the proof itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used an analogy from your own life to understand God, only to realize later that it limited your perception of Him? In what specific way does this passage challenge you to be more careful about how you think about God’s attributes? Commit to one practical step to guard against reducing God to the familiar.
?
Nasafi shows how inconsistency in applying a logical argument destroys it. Are you consistent in how you apply your beliefs about God, or do you sometimes selectively hold to one attribute while ignoring another to avoid discomfort? What is one area where you need to realign your understanding of God with what revelation and sound reason demand, even if it is difficult?

Why God is Not a Substance
Nasafi argues that substance is defined by composition, not by action, so God is not a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that being a substance is defined by composition, not by action, so God cannot be called a substance.

Core Idea
Substance is defined by composition, not by acting.

After refuting the body analogy, Nasafi makes a deeper point: even if the opponents retreat from calling God a body and instead call Him a substance, that also fails. He explains that the term 'substance' in the witnessed realm is based on being a principle of composition, not on being a doer, so it cannot be transferred to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the shift from body to substance.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents might say God is a substance (جوهر) because He is a doer. Nasafi addresses this.
3
Understand the counter-evidence
Nasafi points out that inanimate objects are substances but do not act, so action is not the defining feature of substance.
The example 'جماد وموات' (inanimate and dead things) is key.
4
Learn the true definition
Substance is defined as a principle from which bodies are composed. This meaning cannot apply to God because He is not composed of parts.
The term 'أصل يتركب منه الأجسام' is the definition.
5
Apply the conclusion
Since the meaning of substance does not apply to the unseen realm, God cannot be called a substance.
Thus, the opponents' argument fails on both levels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage is a refutation of an argument that attempts to prove that God must be a body because He acts, using analogy from the witnessed realm. Refuting Anthropomorphism: The Impossibility of a Bodily Creator
Nasafi states the impossibility of God being a body and identifies the first group of anthropomorphists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot be a body according to the Maturidi proof, and which groups historically held anthropomorphic views.

Core Idea
A body is defined by composition and dimensions; Allah is beyond both.

Imam Nasafi builds on earlier arguments that the Creator is neither substance nor accident. Now he turns to the claim that Allah could be a body, showing it is impossible. He also identifies the groups who fell into anthropomorphism, setting the stage for refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi connects this argument to previous proof that Allah is neither substance nor accident.
The passage is divided into two parts: the impossibility argument and the identification of opponents.
2
Understand the definition of 'body'
Nasafi defines a body as something composed or possessing three dimensions. Ask yourself: why does this definition lead to the impossibility of Allah being a body?
This definition is key to the rational proof.
3
Learn about the opponent groups
List the groups Nasafi mentions: Jews, extremist Rafidah, Mushabbihah, and Karramiyyah. Note how he further distinguishes between opponents in name and meaning vs. opponents in name only.
This prepares for detailed refutations later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال قول المجسمة: وإذا ثبت بما مر ذكره أن صانع العالم ليس بعرض ولا بجوهر، فيجب بعد ذلك أن نصرف العناية إلى أن نعرف أنه هل يوصف بأنه جسم، فصرفنا العناية وتأملنا في ذلك فعرفنا استحالة اتصافه تعالى بكون جسما، وذلك لأن المؤتلف أو ما له الأبعاد الثلاثة على ما تقدم ذكره هو الجسم، وكل ذلك مستحيل على الله تعالى، وخالفنا في ذلك طوائف كثيرة من اليهود وغلاة الروافض والمشبهة والكرامية. المخالفون في الاسم والمعنى: وحاصل الاختلاف واقع بيننا وبين طائفتين طائفة يخالفوننا في الاسم والمعنى، ويزعمون أنه تعالى متركب متبعض متجزئ وهم أكثر أصناف اليهود، فإنهم يزعمون أنه تعالى جسم متركب على صورة الآدمي، وهو على صورة شيخ أبيض الرأس واللحية، وكثير من الروافض كهشام بن الحكم وهشام بن سالم الجواليقي وداود الجواربي فإن بعض هؤلاء كانوا يقولون : إنه على صورة إنسان، وأن له شعرا قططا.
The discourse on refuting the statement of the Anthropomorphists: Since it has been established by what has passed that the Maker of the world is not an accident nor a substance, it is necessary after that that we turn our attention to knowing whether He can be described as a body. So we turned our attention and reflected on that, and we came to know the impossibility of His being described as a body. That is because that which is composed or that which has the three dimensions—as previously mentioned—is the body, and all that is impossible for Allah Almighty. And opposed us in that many groups from the Jews, the extremist Rafidah, the Anthropomorphists (al-mushabbihah), and the Karramiyyah. Those who oppose in name and meaning: The sum of the difference lies between us and two groups: a group who oppose us in name and meaning, and they claim that He Almighty is composite, divisible, and partitionable. And they are most kinds of Jews, for they claim that He Almighty is a composite body in the form of a human being, and that He is in the form of an old man with white head and beard. And many of the Rafidah, such as Hisham ibn al-Hakam, Hisham ibn Salim al-Jawaliqi, and Dawud al-Jawaribi, for some of these used to say that He is in the form of a human being and that He has curly hair.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the refutation of anthropomorphism (tajsīm). Nasafi first establishes that since it has been proven earlier that the Creator is neither an accident (ʿaraḍ) nor a substance (jawhar), it follows that He cannot be described as a body (jism), because a body is defined as something composed or possessing three dimensions. This is impossible for Allah. He then notes that many groups opposed this view: Jews, extremist Rafidah (Shi'a), Mushabbihah (those who liken God to creation), and Karramiyyah. The difference is of two types: those who differ in both name and meaning—i.e., they affirm that Allah is a composite, divisible body. Among them are most Jews, who liken God to an old man with white hair and beard. Also many Rafidah like Hisham ibn al-Hakam, Hisham ibn Salim, and Dawud al-Jawaribi, who said He is in human form with curly hair. This sets up the discussion to refute these claims later.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
عرض
مجسمة
مشبهة
اليهود
الروافض
الكرامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume anthropomorphism is a fringe or insignificant theological deviation.
Correction: Nasafi shows it was held by multiple groups (Jews, Rafidah, Mushabbihah, Karramiyyah) and must be refuted systematically.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is it impossible for Allah to be a body?
A) Because the Quran says so.
B) Because a body is composed or has three dimensions, and Allah is neither a substance nor an accident.
C) Because prophets never saw Allah.
D) Because the Jews already believe He is a body.

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be a body because a body is defined by composition and dimensions, and multiple groups historically erred by attributing bodily form to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously imagined Allah in a form, like a bright light or a throne, even though you know He is beyond physical description? How does this passage challenge you to purify your mental image of Allah?

When you hear or read translations of the Qur'an that use anthropomorphic language (e.g., 'hand of Allah'), do your thoughts tend to take them literally? What concrete steps can you take to affirm tanzīh while still believing in the verses?
?
This passage lists groups who deviated into anthropomorphism. Before studying this, were you aware that this belief existed among Muslims? Does knowing this increase your gratitude for sound theology, and how will it affect your attitude towards those who hold such beliefs?

The Rational Refutation of God's Bodiliness
The author presents the rational argument that God cannot be a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that God cannot be a body based on the definitions of substance, accident, and body.

Core Idea
If God is neither accident nor substance, He cannot be a body.

In this passage, the author builds on the earlier proof that God is neither an accident nor a substance. He now argues that since a body is defined as either a composite or something with three dimensions, and both are impossible for God, the claim of anthropomorphists is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof that God is not a body
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the logical flow.
Notice that the author uses the previous conclusion as a premise.
2
Understand the definition of body
The passage defines body as either composite (muta'allif) or having three dimensions. See that both entail limitations.
3
Connect to anthropomorphism
Since God is transcendent, He cannot be described by these categories, thus anthropomorphism is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال قول المجسمة: وإذا ثبت بما مر ذكره أن صانع العالم ليس بعرض ولا بجوهر، فيجب بعد ذلك أن نصرف العناية إلى أن نعرف أنه هل يوصف بأنه جسم، فصرفنا العناية وتأملنا في ذلك فعرفنا استحالة اتصافه تعالى بكون جسما، وذلك لأن المؤتلف أو ما له الأبعاد الثلاثة على ما تقدم ذكره هو الجسم، وكل ذلك مستحيل على الله تعالى، وخالفنا في ذلك طوائف كثيرة من اليهود وغلاة الروافض والمشبهة والكرامية. المخالفون في الاسم والمعنى: وحاصل الاختلاف واقع بيننا وبين طائفتين طائفة يخالفوننا في الاسم والمعنى، ويزعمون أنه تعالى متركب متبعض متجزئ وهم أكثر أصناف اليهود، فإنهم يزعمون أنه تعالى جسم متركب على صورة الآدمي، وهو على صورة شيخ أبيض الرأس واللحية، وكثير من الروافض كهشام بن الحكم وهشام بن سالم الجواليقي وداود الجواربي فإن بعض هؤلاء كانوا يقولون : إنه على صورة الآدمي، حتى قال بعضهم: إن له شعرا أسود من نور أسود، وبعضهم قالوا: هو على صورة غلام أمرد له شعر جعد قطط. فأما هشام بن الحكم فالمحكي عنه أنه قال: إنه كالسبيكة الصافية يتلألأ ، وروي عنه أنه قال : هو سبعة أشبار بشبر نفسه، وحكي أنه قيل له : هو أكبر أم هذا الجبل ؟ وأشير إلى جبل كان بقربه، فقال: لا بل الجبل أعظم منه. وحكي الجاحظ عنه في كتابه الرد على المشبهة: أنه انتقل في سنة واحدة في الله تعالى إلى خمسة أقوال وذهب إلى مثل مذهب هؤلاء في التجسيم والتشبيه مقاتل بن سليمان صاحب التفسير هؤلاء كلهم يخالفوننا في المعنى واللفظ.
The discourse on invalidating the statement of the anthropomorphists: And when it has been established by what has been mentioned previously that the Maker of the world is neither an accident nor a substance, it becomes necessary after that to direct our attention to knowing whether He is described as being a body. So we directed our attention and reflected on that, and we came to know the impossibility of His being described as a body. That is because the composite or that which has the three dimensions, as previously mentioned, is the body, and all of that is impossible for God, may He be exalted. And many groups have opposed us in that: from the Jews, the extremist Rafidah, the Mushabbihah, and the Karramiyya. Those who oppose in name and meaning: The sum of the disagreement occurs between us and two groups: a group that opposes us in name and meaning, and they claim that He, may He be exalted, is composite, divisible, partitionable. They are most of the classes of the Jews, for they claim that He, may He be exalted, is a composite body in the form of a human being, and He is in the form of an old man with a white head and beard. And many of the Rafidah, such as Hisham ibn al-Hakam, Hisham ibn Salim al-Jawaliqi, and Dawud al-Jawaribi, for some of these used to say: He is in the form of a human being, until some of them said: He has black hair from black light, and some said: He is in the form of a beardless youth with curly black hair. As for Hisham ibn al-Hakam, it is reported from him that he said: He is like a pure ingot that glitters, and it is narrated from him that he said: He is seven spans of His own span, and it is mentioned that it was said to him: Is He larger or this mountain? And he pointed to a mountain that was near him, and he said: No, the mountain is greater than Him. And al-Jahiz reported from him in his book 'The Refutation of the Anthropomorphists' that he changed in one year concerning God, may He be exalted, to five opinions. And one who went to the likes of the school of these in anthropomorphism and comparison was Muqatil ibn Sulayman, the author of the commentary. All of these oppose us in meaning and wording.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of anthropomorphism (tajsim). It begins by restating the earlier proof that God is neither an accident nor a substance. Since a body is defined as a composite or something with three dimensions, God cannot be a body. The author then identifies opposing groups, focusing first on those who disagree both in name and meaning. These include most Jews, who claim God is a composite human-shaped body with specific features like a white beard. Among the Rafidah, figures like Hisham ibn al-Hakam held various extreme views: some said God has black hair, others said He is a beardless youth. Hisham ibn al-Hakam himself is reported to have said God is like a pure ingot, seven spans tall, and even smaller than a mountain. The passage also mentions Muqatil ibn Sulayman as sharing these views. All these groups are said to oppose the author's position in both terminology and underlying meaning, as they affirm composition and division in God.

Key Terms
جسم
عرض
جوهر
مؤتلف
الأبعاد الثلاثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'body' is a neutral term not implying imperfection.
Correction: The author shows that by definition, body involves composition or dimensions, both are impossible for God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of body?
A) That which is composed of parts
B) That which has three dimensions
C) Both A and B (composite or has three dimensions)
D) That which is divisible

Key Takeaway
God cannot be a body because a body is either composite or has dimensions, both incompatible with His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your mind may be tempted to form a mental image of God during worship. After learning that God cannot be a body, what specific images or thoughts must you now actively reject? How will you train yourself to think of God without any spatial or physical form?
?
If God is not composed of parts or limited by dimensions, He is present everywhere without being contained. Does your prayer reflect this? Do you sometimes act as if God is 'up there' or 'far away'? What will you do today to remind yourself that God is closer than your own jugular vein, yet not limited by space?
?
The anthropomorphists insisted on a literal reading of certain verses, but this rational proof shows their error. Is there any belief you hold about God that is comfortable for you but might not be correct? Are you willing to surrender that comfort for the truth, even if it feels unsettling?

Opponents Who Affirm God's Bodiliness in Name and Meaning
The author enumerates the anthropomorphic beliefs of Jews and certain Rafidah sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the views of various groups that affirmed God's bodiliness in both name and meaning, and how they differed from the orthodox position.

Core Idea
Some opponents not only called God a body but also believed He is composite and human-like.

Having established that God cannot be a body, the author now turns to the groups that explicitly affirm God's bodiliness. These include many Jews and some Extremist Shiites who held that God has a human form, with detailed descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups opposing in name and meaning
Read the classification: the author mentions two groups, but focuses on the first which opposes in both name and meaning.
This group includes most Jews and many of the Rafidah.
2
Examine the Jewish view
The Jews claim God is a composite body in the form of a human, specifically an old man with white head and beard.
3
Examine the Rafidah views
Figures like Hisham ibn al-Hakam, Hisham ibn Salim, and Dawud al-Jawaribi held various anthropomorphic descriptions, such as God having black hair or being a beardless youth.
Note Hisham ibn al-Hakam's extreme statements: God as a pure ingot, seven spans tall, smaller than a mountain.
4
Note the mention of Muqatil
Muqatil ibn Sulayman is cited as holding similar anthropomorphic views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال قول المجسمة: وإذا ثبت بما مر ذكره أن صانع العالم ليس بعرض ولا بجوهر، فيجب بعد ذلك أن نصرف العناية إلى أن نعرف أنه هل يوصف بأنه جسم، فصرفنا العناية وتأملنا في ذلك فعرفنا استحالة اتصافه تعالى بكون جسما، وذلك لأن المؤتلف أو ما له الأبعاد الثلاثة على ما تقدم ذكره هو الجسم، وكل ذلك مستحيل على الله تعالى، وخالفنا في ذلك طوائف كثيرة من اليهود وغلاة الروافض والمشبهة والكرامية. المخالفون في الاسم والمعنى: وحاصل الاختلاف واقع بيننا وبين طائفتين طائفة يخالفوننا في الاسم والمعنى، ويزعمون أنه تعالى متركب متبعض متجزئ وهم أكثر أصناف اليهود، فإنهم يزعمون أنه تعالى جسم متركب على صورة الآدمي، وهو على صورة شيخ أبيض الرأس واللحية، وكثير من الروافض كهشام بن الحكم وهشام بن سالم الجواليقي وداود الجواربي فإن بعض هؤلاء كانوا يقولون : إنه على صورة الآدمي، حتى قال بعضهم: إن له شعرا أسود من نور أسود، وبعضهم قالوا: هو على صورة غلام أمرد له شعر جعد قطط. فأما هشام بن الحكم فالمحكي عنه أنه قال: إنه كالسبيكة الصافية يتلألأ ، وروي عنه أنه قال : هو سبعة أشبار بشبر نفسه، وحكي أنه قيل له : هو أكبر أم هذا الجبل ؟ وأشير إلى جبل كان بقربه، فقال: لا بل الجبل أعظم منه. وحكي الجاحظ عنه في كتابه الرد على المشبهة: أنه انتقل في سنة واحدة في الله تعالى إلى خمسة أقوال وذهب إلى مثل مذهب هؤلاء في التجسيم والتشبيه مقاتل بن سليمان صاحب التفسير هؤلاء كلهم يخالفوننا في المعنى واللفظ.
The discourse on invalidating the statement of the anthropomorphists: And when it has been established by what has been mentioned previously that the Maker of the world is neither an accident nor a substance, it becomes necessary after that to direct our attention to knowing whether He is described as being a body. So we directed our attention and reflected on that, and we came to know the impossibility of His being described as a body. That is because the composite or that which has the three dimensions, as previously mentioned, is the body, and all of that is impossible for God, may He be exalted. And many groups have opposed us in that: from the Jews, the extremist Rafidah, the Mushabbihah, and the Karramiyya. Those who oppose in name and meaning: The sum of the disagreement occurs between us and two groups: a group that opposes us in name and meaning, and they claim that He, may He be exalted, is composite, divisible, partitionable. They are most of the classes of the Jews, for they claim that He, may He be exalted, is a composite body in the form of a human being, and He is in the form of an old man with a white head and beard. And many of the Rafidah, such as Hisham ibn al-Hakam, Hisham ibn Salim al-Jawaliqi, and Dawud al-Jawaribi, for some of these used to say: He is in the form of a human being, until some of them said: He has black hair from black light, and some said: He is in the form of a beardless youth with curly black hair. As for Hisham ibn al-Hakam, it is reported from him that he said: He is like a pure ingot that glitters, and it is narrated from him that he said: He is seven spans of His own span, and it is mentioned that it was said to him: Is He larger or this mountain? And he pointed to a mountain that was near him, and he said: No, the mountain is greater than Him. And al-Jahiz reported from him in his book 'The Refutation of the Anthropomorphists' that he changed in one year concerning God, may He be exalted, to five opinions. And one who went to the likes of the school of these in anthropomorphism and comparison was Muqatil ibn Sulayman, the author of the commentary. All of these oppose us in meaning and wording.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of anthropomorphism (tajsim). It begins by restating the earlier proof that God is neither an accident nor a substance. Since a body is defined as a composite or something with three dimensions, God cannot be a body. The author then identifies opposing groups, focusing first on those who disagree both in name and meaning. These include most Jews, who claim God is a composite human-shaped body with specific features like a white beard. Among the Rafidah, figures like Hisham ibn al-Hakam held various extreme views: some said God has black hair, others said He is a beardless youth. Hisham ibn al-Hakam himself is reported to have said God is like a pure ingot, seven spans tall, and even smaller than a mountain. The passage also mentions Muqatil ibn Sulayman as sharing these views. All these groups are said to oppose the author's position in both terminology and underlying meaning, as they affirm composition and division in God.

Correction: The passage shows diversity: Jews see God as old, some Rafidah see Him as a youth, Hisham as an ingot, etc.

?

The Karramiyya: Opponents Who Differ Only in Name
The Karramiyya's verbal disagreement on calling God a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Karramiyya differed from mainstream Islam only in terminology regarding God's nature, not in essential meaning.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya said 'body' but meant 'self-subsistent' — a verbal distinction, not a real one.

This passage introduces a second group of opponents, the Karramiyya, who initially appear to disagree with orthodox Islamic theology by calling God a body. However, a closer look reveals that their disagreement is merely verbal: they use the term 'body' but explicitly deny any composition, affirming instead that God is self-subsistent. The author also notes a historical shift within the sect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation side by side. Pay attention to the terms 'body', 'divisible', 'self-subsistent'.
2
Understand the Karramiyya position
Note that the Karramiyya affirm God is a body but immediately clarify that they do not mean composition. They interpret 'body' as 'self-subsistent'.
This matches Hisham ibn al-Hakam's definition of body as 'existent'.
3
Consider the historical development
The author suggests that later Karramiyya distanced themselves from affirming composition, indicating an evolution in the sect's theology.
The passage ends abruptly; the full argument may continue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكرامية مخالفون في الاسم فقط: والطائفة الثانية هم الكرامية، وهم يخالفوننا في الاسم فيقولون: إنه جسم وساعدونا في الظاهر في المعنى فيقولون: لا نعني بقولنا إنه جسم أنه متبعض متجزئ متركب بل نريد أنه القائم بالذات. وحكي عن هشام بن الحكم أنه كان يقول الجسم عبارة عن الموجود. فكان الخلاف بين هؤلاء وبيننا في العبارة، وظني أن المتأخرين من الكرامية يتبرؤن عن إثبات التركيب لما ظهر لهم من فساد ذلك، فأما أوائلهم فقد كانوا
And the Karramiyya are those who oppose in name only: The second group are the Karramiyya, and they oppose us in name, for they say: He is a body, and they agree with us in apparent meaning, for they say: We do not mean by our saying that He is a body that He is divisible, partitionable, or composite; rather we mean that He is the Self-Subsistent. And it is narrated from Hisham ibn al-Hakam that he used to say: The body is an expression for the existent. So the disagreement between these and us was in expression. And I think that the later Karramiyya disavow the affirmation of composition because the corruption of that has become clear to them. As for their early ones, they were...
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage discusses the Karramiyya, the second group of opponents. Unlike the first group, the Karramiyya oppose only in name (al-ism), not in meaning (al-ma'na). While they affirm that God is a body, they explicitly deny that this entails composition, division, or partition. Instead, they interpret 'body' as meaning 'self-subsistent' (al-qa'im bi al-dhat). The author notes that Hisham ibn al-Hakam similarly defined body as 'the existent.' Thus the disagreement is merely verbal. The author adds his opinion that later Karramiyya distanced themselves from affirming composition because its corruption became evident to them, implying that earlier Karramiyya did affirm composition, though the sentence is cut off.

Three Absurd Consequences of Anthropomorphism
Nasafi states the three absurd consequences of tajsim and begins the first argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational consequences that make anthropomorphism (tajsim) false according to Ahl al-Haqq.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphism is false because it leads to impossible consequences.

This passage from Imam Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla offers a decisive rational refutation of anthropomorphism. The People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq) argue that claiming Allah is a body forces absurd conclusions: either the world is pre-eternal or Allah is temporal, it denies the existence of a Creator, and it destroys the proof of divine unity. Since these are impossible, anthropomorphism is false. The author then begins unpacking the first consequence with a dilemma of finitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify three consequences
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. List the three absurd consequences that follow from saying Allah is a body.
The consequences are given in the first few lines.
2
Understand why each consequence is impossible
Reflect on why each consequence is considered false: pre-eternity of the world contradicts temporality of creation, temporality of God contradicts His necessity, denial of a Maker is atheism, and invalidating tawhid undermines monotheism.
These reasons are implicit in Kalam; the passage states they are false.
3
Examine the structure of the first consequence
Notice that the first consequence is explained through two aspects. The passage begins the first aspect: if God is a composite body, He must be either finite or infinite. The argument cuts off, but the direction is clear.
The full argument continues on later pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأي أهل الحق في التجسيم: فأما أهل الحق فإنهم يقولون بأن القول بأن الله تعالى جسم مؤتلف متبعض متجزئ يؤدي إلى القول بقدم العالم أو القول بحدوث الباري جل وعلا، والقول بعدم الصانع للعالم، ويؤدي أيضا إلى إبطال دليل التوحيد، وكل ذلك باطل؛ فكان القول بالتجسيم باطلا. أما الأول وهو أنه يؤدي إلى القول بقدم العالم أو حدوث الصانع، فتقريره من وجهين أحدهما أنه تعالى لو كان جسما مؤتلفا له أبعاض وأجزاء، إما أن يكون متناهيا، وإما أن لا يكون متناهيا، ولا وجه إلى القول بعدم التناهي لأن
The view of the People of Truth concerning anthropomorphism: As for the People of Truth, they say that the statement that God, Exalted is He, is a composite, subdivided, divisible body leads to the statement of the pre-eternity of the world or the statement of the temporal origination of the Creator, Mighty and Majestic, and the statement of the non-existence of the Maker of the world, and it also leads to nullifying the proof of tawhid. All of that is false; so the statement of anthropomorphism is false. As for the first, namely that it leads to the statement of the pre-eternity of the world or the temporal origination of the Creator, its explanation is from two aspects: one of them is that if He, Exalted is He, were a composite body having parts and divisions, either He would be finite or He would not be finite, and there is no point to saying He is not finite because
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents the decisive refutation of anthropomorphism (tajsim) by the People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq), i.e., the Maturidi and Ash'ari schools. The author states that claiming God is a composite, subdivided, divisible body leads to three absurd consequences. First, it forces one to accept either the pre-eternity (qidam) of the world or the temporal origination (huduth) of God—both are impossible: if God is a body and the world is also a body, and if bodies are temporally originated, then God would be temporal; if bodies are pre-eternal, the world would be pre-eternal. Second, it leads to denying the existence of the Maker of the world (since if God is a body, He would need a maker). Third, it invalidates the proof of tawhid (divine unity) because a body is composite and thus requires a composer. Since all these consequences are false, the statement of anthropomorphism is false. The author then begins explaining the first absurdity—namely, the dilemma of pre-eternity or temporality—and announces that it has two aspects. He starts the first aspect: if God is a composite body, He must be either finite (mutanahi) or infinite (ghayr mutanahi). The passage cuts off while rejecting the possibility of infinity (because an infinite body leads to further absurdities, to be elaborated later). This is a classic Kalam argument known as the 'dilemma of the body' against tajsim.

Key Terms
أهل الحق (ahl al-haqq)
تجسيم (tajsim)
قدم العالم (qidam al-'alam)
حدوث (huduth)
دليل التوحيد (dalil al-tawhid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think anthropomorphism (tajsim) is a valid theological position derived from scripture without requiring rational refutation.
Correction: Ahl al-Haqq show that tajsim leads to impossible rational consequences, proving it false regardless of scriptural appearances.

Checkpoint
What is the first consequence that Ahl al-Haqq attribute to the statement that God is a body?
A) It leads to denial of the Hereafter
B) It leads to either the pre-eternity of the world or the temporality of God
C) It leads to denial of prophethood
D) It leads to belief in multiple gods

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Haqq reject anthropomorphism because it necessarily leads to impossible conclusions, thus proving it false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that anthropomorphism forces absurd consequences like the pre-eternity of the world or the origination of Allah, do you see how your own thoughts about Allah can quietly drift toward imagining Him in human terms? What will you do today to guard your heart from such thoughts?
?
If the claim that Allah is a body leads to denying the existence of the Maker of the world, what does that say about the seriousness of even a casual anthropomorphic image in your mind? Will you commit to actively purifying your understanding of Allah's transcendence, starting with one attribute you often picture?

Divine Transcendence
