Why 'Thing' Does Not Imply a Genus
Refutation of the claim that 'thing' implies genus and definition of 'thing' as existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'thing' (shay') does not imply that God belongs to a genus or is similar to creatures.

Core Idea
'Thing' indicates only existence, not a shared genus.

This passage continues the debate over whether God can be called a 'thing.' The opponents argue that if God is a thing, He must be like other things. Imam al-Nasafi refutes this by showing that 'thing' is a name for existence alone, and existence does not necessitate similarity. He then gives the true answer: 'thing' is not a genus name.

Guided Path
1
Follow the dialectical exchange
Read the passage carefully, noting the back-and-forth between the opponent and the author. The opponent presents an objection, and the author responds.
Notice how the author first dismantles a weaker objection before addressing the stronger one.
2
Understand the refutation
Focus on the argument that 'thing' indicates only absolute existence (mujarrad al-wujud) and that similarity depends on specific realities, not on words.
The example of blackness and whiteness helps illustrate that two things can share the label 'thing' without being similar.
3
Grasp the true answer
The 'true answer' (haqiqat al-jawab) defines a genus name as one that indicates beyond mere existence. Since 'thing' indicates only existence, it is not a genus name.
This conclusively shows that calling God a 'thing' does not place Him in a genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل: إذا كان ثابت الذات هل كانت ذات شبيها بغيره، وهل هو مثل لغيره فإن قالوا: نعم. شبهوه، وهم امتنعوا عن إطلاق هذا الاسم عليه فرارا من التشبيه وإن قالوا: لا. قيل: إذا لم يكن ذاته مثلا لغيره وهذا الاسم لا ينبئ عما وراء مطلق الوجود والثبوت ولا شبه تقع لذلك، فلم امتنعتم عن إطلاقه؟ فإن قالوا: امتنعنا مخافة التشبيه. قيل: قد بينا أنه لا يدل إلا على مطلق الثبوت والوجود ولا شبهة فيه فالامتناع عنه إما جهل محض، وإما إظهار ما هو حقيقة الاعتقاد من نفيه وتعطيله، والقول بأنه موجود تلبيس لدفعة السيف. فإن قيل: لو كان الله تعالى شيئا وغيره شيئا لكان من جنس الأشياء، والمماثلة بين أفراد الجنس وأنواعه ثابتة في حق المجانسة، فلو كان هو شيئا لكان مثلا من حيث المجانسة لكل ما ينطلق عليه اسم الجنس، فحينئذ يصير لكل شخص مستقبح وعين مستقذر. قيل: قد مر ما يوجب بطلان هذا فإنا قد بينا أنه اسم للموجود لما أن لا شيء عبارة عن العدم والله تعالى موجود، وبالوجود لم تثبت المجانسة ولا المماثلة بينه وبين سائر الموجودات، والشبه تحقق بحقائق المعاني دون الألفاظ المستعملة فإذا لم يثبت ذلك بالثبوت والوجود لم يثبت باللفظ الذي لا ينبئ إلا عن ذلك. ثم نقول لهم: إن السواد شيء، والبياض شيء، ولا مماثلة بينهما، وقد قررنا هذا. وحقيقة الجواب: أن اسم الشيء ليس باسم جنس لأن اسم الجنس ما ينبئ عما وراء مطلق الوجود كاسم الجوهر ليس باسم مطلق الوجود، بل هو اسم الموجود يتركب منه ومن غير الجسم، أو لما هو قابل للأعراض حتى إنه لا يتناول موجودا ليس يتركب منه الأجسام، ولا موجودا لا يقبل العرض، وكذا العرض ليس باسم لمطلق الوجود إذ موجودات كثيرة ليست بأعراض، بل هو اسم لما يعرض في الجوهر مما يستحيل بقاؤه، فما لم يوجد في هذا
It is said: if He is fixed in essence, is His essence similar to other than Him, and is He like other than Him? If they say: yes, then they have likened Him, and they refrained from applying this name out of fleeing from anthropomorphism. And if they say: no, it is said: if His essence is not like other than Him, and this name does not indicate beyond the absolute existence and subsistence, and no resemblance occurs because of that, so why did you refrain from applying it? If they say: we refrained out of fear of anthropomorphism, it is said: we have shown that it only indicates absolute subsistence and existence, and no ambiguity in it, so refraining from it is either pure ignorance, or an expression of what is the true belief of negation and denial of Him, and the statement that He is existent is a covering to ward off the sword. If it is said: if God Most High is a thing and other than Him is a thing, then He would be of the genus of things, and similarity between members of the genus and its types is established in terms of conspecificity. So if He is a thing, He would be similar in terms of conspecificity to everything to which the name of the genus applies, then He would become for each person repugnant and an object of abomination. It is said: what has passed has necessitated the falsehood of this, for we have shown that it is a name for the existent because 'no thing' is an expression for non-existence, and God Most High is existent. And by existence, conspecificity nor similarity is established between Him and the rest of existents. And resemblance is realized by the realities of meanings apart from the used words. So if that is not established by subsistence and existence, it is not established by the word which indicates only that. Then we say to them: blackness is a thing, and whiteness is a thing, and no similarity between them, and we have established this. And the true answer: that the name 'thing' is not a name of a genus because the name of a genus is that which indicates beyond absolute existence, like the name 'substance' is not a name for absolute existence; rather it is a name for the existent that is composed of it and other than body, or for what is receptive of accidents, to the extent that it does not apply to an existent that is not composed of bodies, nor to an existent that does not accept accidents. And likewise, 'accident' is not a name for absolute existence, since many existents are not accidents; rather it is a name for what inheres in the substance that is impossible of remaining. So what has not existed in this...
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectical argument against those who deny God is a 'thing'. It first takes up the scenario in which the opponent affirms that God is fixed in essence (thabit al-dhat). The author then asks whether that essence is similar to others or like them. If the opponent says yes, they contradict themselves because they refrained from the term 'thing' to avoid likening, yet now they affirm likeness. If they say no, then they must concede that the term 'thing' does not imply similarity, since it indicates only absolute existence and subsistence (thubut). Hence their objection fails. The author then presents a stronger objection from the opponent: if God is a 'thing', He must belong to the genus of 'things' and thus be similar to all other things in terms of generic conspecificity. The author refutes this: 'thing' is a name for existent (mawjud), and existence does not imply a common genus; similarity depends on specific realities (haqa'iq al-ma'ani), not on words. To illustrate, he notes that blackness and whiteness are both 'things' but have no similarity. Finally, he gives the 'true answer' (haqiqat al-jawab): the term 'thing' is not a genus name (ism jins) because genus names indicate beyond mere existence (e.g., 'substance' implies composition or receptivity to accidents). 'Thing' indicates only existence, and thus does not place God in a genus. The passage ends mid-argument, but the main point is clear.

Misconception: That calling God a 'thing' implies He belongs to a genus alongside creatures, leading to similarity.
Correction: The term 'thing' indicates only absolute existence, which does not establish a genus or similarity. Similarity depends on specific realities, not the word.

Checkpoint
According to the 'true answer' (haqiqat al-jawab), why is 'thing' not a genus name?

B) Because it is used for both God and creatures.

D) Because it is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The term 'thing' indicates only absolute existence and does not imply genus or similarity.

Why Calling Allah 'Thing' Does Not Imply Anthropomorphism
Nasafi defends applying 'thing' to God, refuting anthropomorphism claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the name 'thing' (shay') applied to Allah denotes only absolute existence and does not imply similarity to creation.

Core Idea
'Thing' simply means 'existent' — it does not create a genus.

Nasafi addresses a key theological objection: if we call Allah a 'thing', doesn't that make Him like other things? He argues that 'thing' indicates only absolute existence (muṭlaq al-wujūd) and affirmation (thubūt), not genus or similarity. This lesson walks through his dialectical argument and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure of objections and responses.
The argument is dialectical: the opponent presents two objections, and Nasafi answers each.
2
Identify the first objection and response
The opponent says they refrain from calling God 'thing' to avoid tashbīh (anthropomorphism). Nasafi calls this ignorance or a cover for actual denial.
Nasafi insists 'thing' only means existence, so no fear is warranted.
3
Understand the second objection and its refutation
The opponent claims that if God is a 'thing', He belongs to the same genus as other things. Nasafi responds by distinguishing 'thing' from genus names, using examples like blackness and whiteness.
The key point: 'thing' is a name for the existent (mawjūd), not a genus name.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
Nasafi concludes that 'thing' denotes absolute existence alone, which does not necessitate homogeneity or similarity. The name is safe to apply to Allah.
This preserves divine transcendence while affirming real existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الاسم لا ينبئ عما وراء مطلق الوجود والثبوت ولا شبه تقع لذلك، فلم امتنعتم عن إطلاقه؟ فإن قالوا: امتنعنا مخافة التشبيه. قيل: قد بينا أنه لا يدل إلا على مطلق الثبوت والوجود ولا شبهة فيه فالامتناع عنه إما جهل محض، وإما إظهار ما هو حقيقة الاعتقاد من نفيه وتعطيله، والقول بأنه موجود تلبيس لدفع [؟] السيف. فإن قيل: لو كان الله تعالى شيئا وغيره شيئا لكان من جنس الأشياء، والمماثلة بين أفراد الجنس وأنواعه ثابتة في حق المجانسة، فلو كان هو شيئا لكان مثلا من حيث المجانسة لكل ما ينطلق عليه اسم الجنس، فحينئذ يصير لكل شخص مستقبح وعين مستقذر. قيل: قد مر ما يوجب بطلان هذا فإنا قد بينا أنه اسم للموجود لما أن لا شيء عبارة عن العدم والله تعالى موجود، وبالوجود لم تثبت المجانسة ولا المماثلة بينه وبين سائر الموجودات، والشبه تحقق بحقائق المعاني دون الألفاظ المستعملة فإذا لم يثبت [؟] ذلك بالثبوت والوجود لم يثبت باللفظ الذي لا ينبئ إلا عن ذلك. ثم نقول لهم: إن السواد شيء، والبياض شيء، ولا مماثلة بينهما، وقد قررنا هذا، وحقيقة الجواب: أن اسم الشيء ليس باسم جنس لأن اسم الجنس ما ينبئ عما وراء مطلق الوجود كاسم الجوهر ليس باسم مطلق الوجود، بل هو اسم الموجود يتركب منه ومن غير الجسم، أو لما هو قابل للأعراض حتى إنه لا يتناول موجودا ليس يتركب منه الأجسام، ولا موجودا لا يقبل العرض، وكذا العرض ليس باسم لمطلق الوجود إذ موجودات كثيرة ليست بأعراض، بل هو اسم لما يعرض فيه مما يستحيل بقاؤه، فما لم يوجد في هذا المعنى لم يكن عرضا، وكذا كل اسم جنس كالحيوان والنبات، وغير ذلك، فدل أن ما دل على مطلق الوجود لا يكون اسم جنس، ألا ترى أنا علمنا أن هذا الاسم لا يدل إلا على مطلق الوجود ولا مماثلة تثبت بمطلق الوجود، والمجانسة تثبت المماثلة من حيث المجانسة، فعرف بهذه المقدمات أن الشيء ليس باسم جنس، ولا يوجب المجانسة ولا المماثلة، والله ولي التوفيق.
And this name does not signify anything beyond absolute existence and affirmation, and no doubt occurs because of it. So why do you refrain from applying it? If they say: We refrain out of fear of anthropomorphism. It is said: We have already explained that it indicates only absolute affirmation and existence, and there is no doubt in it; so refraining from it is either pure ignorance, or a display of the true belief in negating and nullifying Him, and the statement that He is existent is a disguise to ward off [؟] the sword. If it is said: If God Almighty is a thing and other things are things, then He would be of the genus of things, and similarity between individuals of a genus and its types is established in terms of homogeneity. So if He were a thing, He would be similar in terms of homogeneity to everything to which the name of genus applies; then He would become for every person abominable and an object of disgust. It is said: What has passed requires the invalidity of this; for we have already explained that it is a name for the existent because nothing (lā shay') is an expression for non-existence, and God Almighty is existent, and by existence neither homogeneity nor similarity is established between Him and other existents. Likeness is realized by the realities of meanings, not by the words used, so if [؟] that is not established by affirmation and existence, it is not established by the word which signifies only that. Then we say to them: Blackness is a thing, and whiteness is a thing, yet there is no similarity between them; and we have established this. The essence of the answer: The name 'thing' is not a genus name, because a genus name is what signifies beyond absolute existence, like the name 'substance' is not a name for absolute existence, but rather it is a name for an existent composed of it and of something other than body, or for that which is receptive to accidents, such that it does not apply to an existent that is not composed of bodies, nor to an existent that does not accept accidents. Similarly, accident is not a name for absolute existence, since many existents are not accidents, but rather it is a name for what inheres in [substance] and cannot endure. So whatever does not exist in this meaning is not an accident. Likewise every genus name like animal, plant, etc. This shows that what indicates absolute existence is not a genus name. Do you not see that we know that this name indicates only absolute existence, and no similarity is established by absolute existence, while homogeneity establishes similarity in terms of homogeneity. So through these premises it is known that 'thing' is not a genus name, nor does it necessitate homogeneity or similarity. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a defense of applying the name 'thing' (shay') to God, addressing objections that it implies anthropomorphism (tashbīh). The argument proceeds as follows: First, the opponent claims they refrain from calling God 'thing' for fear of similarity. The author responds that 'thing' denotes only absolute existence (muṭlaq al-wujūd) and affirmation (thubūt), so the refusal is either ignorance or a cover for true denial. Then a second objection: if God is a 'thing' like other things, He would belong to the same genus and thus be similar to all things in genus. The author answers that 'thing' is simply a name for the existent (al-mawjūd) because 'nothing' (lā shay') means non-existence, and God is existent. Existence alone does not entail homogeneity (mujānasa) or similarity (mumāthala); those require specific realities, not the mere word. The author then gives the definitive answer: 'thing' is not a genus name, because a genus name (ism jins) signifies beyond absolute existence, e.g., 'substance' (jawhar) specifies composition or receptivity to accidents. 'Thing' indicates only absolute existence, and existence alone does not create similarity, as seen in blackness and whiteness both being 'things' yet dissimilar. Thus the premises prove that 'thing' does not necessitate homogeneity or similarity.

اسم جنس (ism jins)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name 'thing' implies that God belongs to the same genus as created things, leading to anthropomorphism.
Correction: Nasafi argues that 'thing' indicates only absolute existence, not genus. Genus names specify additional properties, which 'thing' does not. Hence, no similarity follows.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does the name 'thing' not necessitate homogeneity (mujānasa) between God and creation?
A) Because 'thing' is a genus name that includes God, but He is exempt from similarity.
B) Because 'thing' indicates only absolute existence, and existence alone does not establish homogeneity or similarity.
C) Because God is not a thing at all; the name is metaphorical.
D) Because similarity is determined by verbal usage, not realities.

Key Takeaway
The name 'thing' applied to Allah affirms His existence without implying any similarity to creation, because it denotes only absolute existence, not a genus or shared attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is a thing,' do you feel a discomfort that reveals a residual anthropomorphism in your heart? How can you train yourself to accept His absolute existence without imagining a form?
?
The opponents avoided the term 'thing' out of fear of tashbih. Is there any statement about Allah that you silently reject or water down because of fear of misunderstanding? Are you willing to stand firm in affirming what the text affirms?
?

Defending Abu Hanifa from the Accusation of Tashbīh
The author argues the report from Abu Hanifa is not reliably transmitted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argumentative steps taken to defend Abu Hanifa from the charge of anthropomorphism (tashbīh) by questioning the authenticity of the report and citing al-Maturidi's authority.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's supposed statement on mā'iyya is either unauthentic or misunderstood.

This passage addresses a controversial report attributing a statement to Abu Hanifa that could imply anthropomorphism. The author first examines the chain of transmission and the absence of the report in authoritative sources, then invokes al-Maturidi's expertise. Read on to see how the defense is built.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main arguments
Read the passage and note how the author first challenges the report's authenticity by pointing out that it was not transmitted by trusted students and is absent from Abu Hanifa's books.
2
Understand the role of al-Maturidi
The author uses al-Maturidi's authority as the foremost expert on Abu Hanifa's school, noting that he never attributed this statement to Abu Hanifa in his works.
This strengthens the defense.
3
Reflect on the defense strategy
Consider why attacking the report's transmission is a powerful argument against tashbīh accusations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول وبالله التوفيق : إن هذه الرواية عن أبي حنيفة صحيحة لم يروها عنه أحد من أصحابه الناقلين لعلمه العارفين بحقائق مذهبه، ولا ذكر في كتاب من كتبه، والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله كونه أعرف الناس بمذاهب أبي حنيفة - رحمه الله - لم ينسب هذا القول إليه لا في كتاب التوحيد، ولا في كتاب المقالات، وإن كان اشتغل ببيان مراد من قال بهذه المقالة على ما نبين. ثم ولئن ثبت ذلك عنه فلم يرد به المجانسة، فإن الشيخ أبا منصور الماتريدي ذكر في كتاب المقالات فقال بعد كلام ذكره: ثم لزم تعاليه عن جميع معاني غيره وسبحانيته عن أن يكون له مثال في الحوادث، فذلك هو القول بالمائية عند من يقول لا غير ، وهو أن ينفي عنه معنى هسيته غيره إذ لم يكن المائية عند القول إلا حسيته خلاف هسيته غيره، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك دل أن القائل بالمائية لا يريد به إثبات المجانسة بل يريد به نفيها، ومن دأب الشيخ – رحمه الله – أنه يذكر لفظ الهسية، وإن كان فارسية لما أن لفظة الوجود مشتركة بين فعل الواحد، وبين ثبوت الذات، ولفظة الآنية مما يستعمله الفلاسفة دون المتكلمين مع أنه لا أصل له يوجد في العربية الفصيحة، والله أعلم.
We say, and with God is success: Indeed, this report from Abu Hanifa is authentic; none of his companions who transmitted his knowledge and were aware of the realities of his school narrated it from him, nor is it mentioned in any of his books. And the Shaykh, Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi – may God have mercy on him, who was the most knowledgeable of people about the schools of Abu Hanifa – did not attribute this statement to him, neither in Kitab al-Tawhid nor in Kitab al-Maqalat, even though he occupied himself with explaining the intent of those who said this article as we will clarify. Then, even if it is established from him, he did not mean by it al-mujānasa (homogeneity). For Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned in Kitab al-Maqalat, saying after some speech: 'Then He is necessarily exalted above all meanings of others and transcendent above having any likeness in contingent things. That is the statement of mā'iyya according to those who say it, and it is to negate from Him the meaning of the hāsiyya (existence in external reality) of other than Him. For mā'iyya in their view is nothing but 'hāsiyya' contrary to the hāsiyya of other than Him.' And when the matter is thus, it indicates that the one who says mā'iyya does not intend to affirm al-mujānasa but rather to negate it. And it is the practice of the Shaykh – may God have mercy on him – that he uses the word 'al-hāsiyya' even though it is Persian, because the word 'wujūd' (existence) is shared between the act of the One and the subsistence of the essence, and the word 'al-āniyya' (betweenness?) is used by the philosophers rather than the theologians, while it has no basis in eloquent Arabic. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage defends Abu Hanifa from the accusation of tashbīh. It first argues that the report attributing the statement of mā'iyya to Abu Hanifa is not authentic (though the text says 'authentic', the context indicates it is meant as 'not authentic'), as none of his trusted students transmitted it and it is not found in his books. Moreover, Imam al-Maturidi, the foremost authority on Abu Hanifa's school, never attributed it to him in his works. Then, even if the report were authentic, al-Maturidi's interpretation in Kitab al-Maqalat shows that the term mā'iyya was meant to negate any likeness to creation, not to affirm a genus. Al-Maturidi explains that mā'iyya refers to the negation of the hāsiyya (contingent existence) of others from God, thus affirming His transcendence. The author also notes that al-Maturidi used the Persian term 'hāsiyya' to avoid ambiguity of the Arabic word 'wujūd' which can mean both the act of causing existence and the subsistence of essence. The term 'āniyya' is rejected as philosophical jargon without basis in eloquent Arabic.

Key Terms
مائية (mā'iyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The defense of Abu Hanifa begins by questioning the authenticity of the report, citing lack of reliable transmission and the silence of al-Maturidi.

?

Al-Maturidi's Reinterpretation of Mā'iyya as Negation of Likeness
Al-Maturidi reinterprets mā'iyya to mean negation of likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi interprets the term mā'iyya to negate any similarity between Allah and creation, and why he uses the Persian term hāsiyya.

Core Idea
Mā'iyya means negating the hāsiyya of others from Allah, not affirming a genus.

Even if the report were authentic, al-Maturidi provides an interpretation that clears Abu Hanifa from anthropomorphism. In this passage, he explains that mā'iyya actually negates any likeness by contrasting it with the contingent existence (hāsiyya) of others. He also discusses terminology preferences.

Guided Path
1
Examine al-Maturidi's definition of mā'iyya
Read the embedded quote from Kitab al-Maqalat where al-Maturidi explicitly states that mā'iyya negates the hāsiyya of others from Allah.
2
Understand the function of hāsiyya
Note that al-Maturidi uses the Persian word hāsiyya to avoid the ambiguity of the Arabic wujūd, which can mean either the act of causing existence or the subsistence of essence.
This clarifies why a foreign term is preferred.
3
Reflect on the rejection of āniyya
The passage rejects the philosophical term āniyya as lacking basis in eloquent Arabic, showing a concern for linguistic authenticity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول وبالله التوفيق : إن هذه الرواية عن أبي حنيفة صحيحة لم يروها عنه أحد من أصحابه الناقلين لعلمه العارفين بحقائق مذهبه، ولا ذكر في كتاب من كتبه، والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله كونه أعرف الناس بمذاهب أبي حنيفة - رحمه الله - لم ينسب هذا القول إليه لا في كتاب التوحيد، ولا في كتاب المقالات، وإن كان اشتغل ببيان مراد من قال بهذه المقالة على ما نبين. ثم ولئن ثبت ذلك عنه فلم يرد به المجانسة، فإن الشيخ أبا منصور الماتريدي ذكر في كتاب المقالات فقال بعد كلام ذكره: ثم لزم تعاليه عن جميع معاني غيره وسبحانيته عن أن يكون له مثال في الحوادث، فذلك هو القول بالمائية عند من يقول لا غير ، وهو أن ينفي عنه معنى هسيته غيره إذ لم يكن المائية عند القول إلا حسيته خلاف هسيته غيره، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك دل أن القائل بالمائية لا يريد به إثبات المجانسة بل يريد به نفيها، ومن دأب الشيخ – رحمه الله – أنه يذكر لفظ الهسية، وإن كان فارسية لما أن لفظة الوجود مشتركة بين فعل الواحد، وبين ثبوت الذات، ولفظة الآنية مما يستعمله الفلاسفة دون المتكلمين مع أنه لا أصل له يوجد في العربية الفصيحة، والله أعلم.
We say, and with God is success: Indeed, this report from Abu Hanifa is authentic; none of his companions who transmitted his knowledge and were aware of the realities of his school narrated it from him, nor is it mentioned in any of his books. And the Shaykh, Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi – may God have mercy on him, who was the most knowledgeable of people about the schools of Abu Hanifa – did not attribute this statement to him, neither in Kitab al-Tawhid nor in Kitab al-Maqalat, even though he occupied himself with explaining the intent of those who said this article as we will clarify. Then, even if it is established from him, he did not mean by it al-mujānasa (homogeneity). For Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned in Kitab al-Maqalat, saying after some speech: 'Then He is necessarily exalted above all meanings of others and transcendent above having any likeness in contingent things. That is the statement of mā'iyya according to those who say it, and it is to negate from Him the meaning of the hāsiyya (existence in external reality) of other than Him. For mā'iyya in their view is nothing but 'hāsiyya' contrary to the hāsiyya of other than Him.' And when the matter is thus, it indicates that the one who says mā'iyya does not intend to affirm al-mujānasa but rather to negate it. And it is the practice of the Shaykh – may God have mercy on him – that he uses the word 'al-hāsiyya' even though it is Persian, because the word 'wujūd' (existence) is shared between the act of the One and the subsistence of the essence, and the word 'al-āniyya' (betweenness?) is used by the philosophers rather than the theologians, while it has no basis in eloquent Arabic. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage defends Abu Hanifa from the accusation of tashbīh. It first argues that the report attributing the statement of mā'iyya to Abu Hanifa is not authentic (though the text says 'authentic', the context indicates it is meant as 'not authentic'), as none of his trusted students transmitted it and it is not found in his books. Moreover, Imam al-Maturidi, the foremost authority on Abu Hanifa's school, never attributed it to him in his works. Then, even if the report were authentic, al-Maturidi's interpretation in Kitab al-Maqalat shows that the term mā'iyya was meant to negate any likeness to creation, not to affirm a genus. Al-Maturidi explains that mā'iyya refers to the negation of the hāsiyya (contingent existence) of others from God, thus affirming His transcendence. The author also notes that al-Maturidi used the Persian term 'hāsiyya' to avoid ambiguity of the Arabic word 'wujūd' which can mean both the act of causing existence and the subsistence of essence. The term 'āniyya' is rejected as philosophical jargon without basis in eloquent Arabic.

Key Terms
مائية (mā'iyya)
مجانسة (mujānasa)
هسية (hāsiyya)
آنية (āniyya)
وجود (wujūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mā'iyya implies Allah has a genus or similarity to creation.
Correction: Al-Maturidi explains that mā'iyya negates any likeness by denying the hāsiyya of others from Allah.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, what does mā'iyya mean when applied to Allah?
A) Affirming a genus
B) Negating contingent existence from Allah
C) Describing Allah's knowledge
D) Denying existence

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi interprets mā'iyya as a negation of any likeness to creation, using the term hāsiyya to avoid ambiguity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah, do you unconsciously imagine a likeness to something you know? How does al-Maturidi's insistence on negating any hāsiyya force you to purify that mental image—and what specific aspect of your concept of God needs to change today?
?
You've just read that mā'iyya is about negation, not affirmation. In your own life, are you more comfortable affirming what Allah is 'like' (even if unintentionally) than accepting the difficult task of negating all resemblance? What habit or assumption about God will you actively strip away starting now?

The Ambiguity of Asking 'What is God?'
Al-Nasafi enumerates five possible meanings of the question 'What is God?' and provides answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the question 'What is God?' can be interpreted in five distinct ways, each requiring a different answer, and that affirming mā'iyyah does not imply anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
The question 'What is God?' is ambiguous — it depends on what you mean.

The great Maturidi scholar al-Nasafi addresses a common yet problematic question: 'What is God?' (mā huwa?). In this passage, he unpacks the ambiguity inherent in this question and shows how each interpretation leads to a different theological answer. This clarification protects against misunderstandings about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow of the five interpretations.
Notice how al-Nasafi begins by setting up the questioner's query.
2
Identify the five interpretations
Scan the passage for each occurrence of 'وإن عنيت بقولك: ما هو؟' and note the five categories: name, attribute, mā'iyyah, action, origin.
Each category is preceded by a conditional phrase.
3
Understand each answer
For each interpretation, see what al-Nasafi gives as the appropriate answer. For example, for name, he lists divine names; for attribute, He is hearing, seeing; etc.
The answers reflect different aspects of God's nature.
4
Focus on the mā'iyyah meaning
Particularly note the third interpretation: asking about mā'iyyah (what-ness). Al-Nasafi's answer emphasizes transcendence above likeness and genus.
This directly addresses the common misunderstanding.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the final lines where al-Nasafi states that affirming mā'iyyah does not entail homogeneity or likeness. Think about how this protects tanzih (divine transcendence).
This is the key doctrinal takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: إن سائلا لو سألنا عن الله تعالى فقال: ما هو؟ قيل له: سؤالك هذا محتمل إن عنيت بقولك ما هو؟ ما اسمه على ما قال فرعون: {وَمَا رَبُّ الْعَالَمِينَ} وقال عز وجل: {وَمَا تِلكَ بِيَمِينكَ} أي: ما اسمها؟ حتى قال موسى عليه السلام: هي عصاي. فجوابك: أنه الله الرحمن الرحيم، الرب الخالق، البارئ العالم. وإن عنيت بقولك: ما هو؟ ما صفته؟ فجوابك: إنه سميع بصير. وإن عنيت بقولك: ما هو؟ ما مائيته؟ فجوابك: إنه متعال عن المثال والجنس، وإن عنيت به ما فعله؟ فجوابك: أنه تعالى خلق المخلوقات، ووضع كل شيء موضوعه، وإن عنيت بقولك: ما هو؟ أي ممن هو؟ فجوابك: إنه تعالى متعال عن أن يكون من شيء بل هو يكون الأشياء كذا ذكره الشيخ أبو منصور في جواب السؤال: إن الله تعالى ما هو؟ وعرف بهذا أن من أثبت المائية لم يثبت المجانسة بينه وبين المخلوقات، ولا المماثلة، والله الموفق.
Then we say: If a questioner were to ask us about God Almighty, saying: 'What is He?' It would be said to him: This question of yours is ambiguous. If by your saying 'What is He?' you mean what is His name, as Pharaoh said: 'And what is the Lord of the worlds?' and He, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'And what is that in your right hand?' meaning what is its name? until Moses, peace be upon him, said: 'It is my staff.' Then your answer is: He is Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, the Lord, the Creator, the Maker, the Knower. And if by your saying 'What is He?' you mean what is His attribute? Then your answer is: He is Hearing, Seeing. And if by your saying 'What is He?' you mean what is His mā'iyyah? Then your answer is: He is exalted above likeness and genus. And if by it you mean what did He do? Then your answer is: He, exalted, created the creatures and placed everything in its proper place. And if by your saying 'What is He?' you mean from whom is He? Then your answer is: He, exalted, is exalted from being from something, rather He is the creator of things. Thus mentioned Shaykh Abu Mansur in answer to the question: 'What is God Almighty?' And by this it is known that whoever affirmed mā'iyyah did not affirm homogeneity between Him and creatures, nor likeness. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is an extensive theological answer to the question 'What is God?' (mā huwa?). The author explains that the question is ambiguous and can be interpreted in five different ways: asking about His name, His attributes, His mā'iyyah (what-ness), His actions, or His origin. For each, a correct answer is provided, culminating in the affirmation that God is beyond any likeness, genus, or composition. The passage then clarifies that affirming mā'iyyah for God does not imply any homogeneity or similarity with creation, as some might mistakenly think. The author cites Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's response to this question to reinforce the point. The conclusion emphasizes that the proper understanding of mā'iyyah is not anthropomorphic.

المائية (al-mā'iyyah)
المجانسة (al-mujānasah)
المماثلة (al-mumāthalah)
التشبيه (al-tashbīh)
الاسم (al-ism)
الصفة (al-sifah)
المثال (al-mithāl)
الجنس (al-jins)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming a 'what-ness' (mā'iyyah) for God necessarily implies that He is like created things or has a genus.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explains that affirming mā'iyyah for God does not affirm homogeneity (mujānasah) or likeness (mumāthalah) with creation because God's mā'iyyah is unique and transcends any example or genus.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The question 'What is God?' must be clarified to avoid false attributions; affirming mā'iyyah does not entail similarity, preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Have you ever felt frustrated when someone answers a question about God with 'Allah knows best' or a reference to transcendence? Reflect: is your frustration rooted in wanting to know God's essence (mā'iyyah) in a way that He cannot be known? How does this lesson recalibrate your expectation?
?
If a non-Muslim or a person of doubt asks you 'What is God?' tomorrow, will you be able to clarify their intent and respond with the wisdom of Al-Nasafi? What concrete step will you take to prepare yourself for that moment?

Refutation of an Unknowable Quality (Kayfiyyah)
Al-Nasafī refutes the claim of an unknowable divine quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing an unknowable quality to Allah leads to rational contradiction.

Core Idea
You cannot affirm what you cannot describe without falling into absurdity.

Al-Nasafī addresses a view that attributes an unknowable quality to Allah. He first notes its absence from Sunni tradition, then exposes its logical flaw. This lesson walks through his concise refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of argument.
Notice the bracketed heading marks a new section.
2
Identify the two prongs of refutation
The refutation has a historical part (not from Ahl al-Sunna) and a logical part (dilemma about knowing the quality). Locate both.
The logical part begins with 'ثم يقال'.
3
Follow the dilemma
Trace the two possible answers from the opponent and Al-Nasafī's response to each. Understand why both lead to contradiction.
The first response leads to affirming something unknown; the second leads to an infinite regress.
4
Understand the conclusion
The final statement 'وهو محال' declares the impossibility. Reflect on why this refutation is effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما القول بكيفية لا يعرفها إلا هو مما لم يرو عن أحد من أهل السنة البتة، وإنما هو شيء روي عن الكرامية الأولى روى عنهم أبو بكر بن اليمان أحد متكلمى سمرقند وكان من القدماء، ذكر ذلك في كتابه الذي رد فيه على الكرامية مذاهبهم، وهو قول فاسد، لما أن الكيفية عبارة عن البيئات والألوان والأحوال، وقد مر القول ببطلان ذلك كله. [إبطال القول بالكيفية] ثم يقال: إن هذه الكيفية التي لا يعرفها إلا الله تعالى هل تعرفونها أنتم؟ فإن قالوا: لا. قيل لهم: إذا كنتم لم تعرفونها فلم تقولون: إنها ثابتة. فإن قالوا: نعرف أنها موجودة، ولا نعرف كيفيتها قيل لهم هذا منكم إقرار أنكم تعرفونها، ولا تعرفون كيفيتها، وهذا منكم إثبات الكيفية للكيفية وهو محال، والله الموفق.
As for the statement of a kayfiyyah (quality) that no one knows but Him, it has not been narrated from anyone of Ahl al-Sunna at all. Rather, it is something narrated from the early Karramiyya, narrated from them by Abu Bakr ibn al-Yaman, one of the theologians of Samarkand and among the early ones. He mentioned that in his book in which he refuted the doctrines of the Karramiyya. And it is a corrupt statement, because kayfiyyah refers to conditions, colors, and states, and the statement of the nullity of all that has already passed. [Refutation of the statement of kayfiyyah] Then it is said: 'This kayfiyyah which no one knows but Allah, exalted is He, do you know it?' If they say: 'No,' it is said to them: 'Since you do not know it, why do you say it is established?' If they say: 'We know that it exists, but we do not know its how-ness (kayfiyyah),' it is said to them: 'This from you is an admission that you know it, but you do not know its how-ness, and this from you is an affirmation of a kayfiyyah for the kayfiyyah, which is impossible.' And Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the doctrine that God possesses an unknowable quality (kayfiyyah). The author first states that this view is not reported from any Sunni authority; it is only attributed to the early Karramiyya, as recorded by Abu Bakr ibn al-Yaman. The author declares it corrupt because kayfiyyah, as a concept, refers to modalities like states, colors, and conditions, which have already been shown to be inapplicable to God. Then the author presents an argument against those who hold this view: He asks whether they themselves know this kayfiyyah. If they answer 'no,' he points out the contradiction of affirming something unknown. If they answer that they know it exists but not its modality (kayfiyyah), he retorts that this is actually an admission that they know it, and by saying they don't know its kayfiyyah, they are positing a kayfiyyah for the kayfiyyah, which leads to an infinite regress and is impossible. Thus the concept is rationally invalid.

Key Terms
كيفية
الكرامية
أهل السنة
إثبات الكيفية للكيفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can affirm that God has a quality without specifying its nature, and that is acceptable.
Correction: Affirming a quality without any specification leads to a logical contradiction: either you know it (then it's not unknowable) or you don't (then why affirm it?). Claiming to know its existence but not its how-ness implies a further quality, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Nasafī's argument, what is the problem with saying 'we know the quality exists but not its how-ness'?
A) It contradicts the Qur'an.
B) It implies an infinite regress of qualities.
C) It is only reported from the Karramiyya.
D) It makes God composite.

Key Takeaway
The existence of a quality cannot be affirmed without describing it in meaningful terms; otherwise, it leads to rational absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself affirming something about Allah without being able to describe what you mean—like 'He has a quality only He knows'? This lesson exposes that as a contradiction. Will you now examine every belief you hold about Allah and discard those you cannot substantiate from revelation?
?
If you were asked to describe Allah's essence, would you be content to say 'I don't know' and stop, or would you feel the need to add a qualification that you cannot justify? This lesson demands that you check your tendency to assert unknowable qualities. Are you willing to hold your tongue and rely only on what Allah has revealed?
?
The Karramiyya claimed a quality that only Allah knows. Have you ever made a claim about Allah that you cannot support from the Qur'an and Sunnah? This lesson calls you to repentance for any such presumption. Will you search your heart and abandon any belief about Allah that is built on speculation rather than revelation?

Refuting the Claim of an Unknown Divine Quality
Imam Nasafi refutes the claim that God has a quality unknown to anyone except Him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming an unknown quality in God leads to logical contradiction and is rejected by Ahl al-Sunnah.

Core Idea
An unknown quality is either meaningless or self-contradictory.

Imam Nasafi confronts a claim attributed to the early Karramiyyah: that God has a quality (kayfiyyah) known only to Him. He first identifies its source, then refutes it by defining what 'quality' means and exposing the logical incoherence of the claim. This lesson walks through his argument, showing how the claim collapses under its own weight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Imam Nasafi structures his refutation: first historical, then definitional, then dialectical.
2
Understand the definition of kayfiyyah
Imam Nasafi explains that kayfiyyah refers to states, colors, and conditions—all of which have been shown impossible for God. Why does this definition matter?
This is the core of the theological refutation.
3
Follow the dialectical argument
The argument presents two options: either the proponents know the quality or they do not. Both lead to absurdity. Trace the logic step by step.
Use the guided breakdown to see the structure.
4
Apply the lesson
Ask yourself: How does this argument protect divine transcendence (tanzih)? What does it teach us about speaking about Allah's attributes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما القول بكيفية لا يعرفها إلا هو مما لم يرو عن أحد من أهل السنة البتة، وإنما هو شيء روي عن الكرامية الأولى روى عنهم أبو بكر بن اليمان أحد متكلمى سمرقند وكان من القدماء، ذكر ذلك في كتابه الذي رد فيه على الكرامية مذاهبم، وهو قول فاسد، لما أن الكيفية عبارة عن البيئات والألوان والأحوال، وقد مر القول ببطلان ذلك كله. ثم يقال: إن هذه الكيفية التي لا يعرفها إلا الله تعالى هل تعرفونها أنتم؟ فإن قالوا: لا. قيل لهم: إذا كنتم لم تعرفونها فلم تقولون إنها ثابتة. فإن قالوا: نعرف أنها موجودة، ولا نعرف كيفيتها قيل لهم هذا منكم إقرار أنكم تعرفونها، ولا تعرفون كيفيتها، وهذا منكم إثبات الكيفية للكيفية وهو محال، والله الموفق.
As for the statement of a quality (kayfiyyah) that none knows except Him, it has not been reported from any of the Ahl al-Sunnah at all. Rather, it is something reported from the early Karramiyyah, reported from them by Abu Bakr ibn al-Yaman, one of the theologians of Samarqand, who was among the early ones. He mentioned that in his book in which he refuted the Karramiyyah regarding their doctrines. And it is a corrupt statement, because quality (kayfiyyah) refers to states (hay'at), colors (alwan), and conditions (ahwal), and the statement of the invalidity of all that has already passed. Then it is said: This quality that none knows except Allah Almighty, do you know it yourselves? If they say: No, it is said to them: If you do not know it, why do you say that it is established? If they say: We know that it exists, but we do not know its modality (kayfiyyah), it is said to them: This from you is an admission that you know it and you do not know its modality, and this from you is an affirmation of a quality for the quality, which is impossible. And Allah is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that God has a quality (kayfiyyah) that is unknown to anyone except Him. Imam Nasafi first states that this view is not reported from any Ahl al-Sunnah; rather it is attributed to the early Karramiyyah, reported by Abu Bakr ibn al-Yaman in his refutation of them. The author declares this view corrupt because 'kayfiyyah' properly refers to states, colors, and conditions—all of which have been previously shown to be impossible for God. Then he presents a dialectical argument: if the proponents say they do not know this quality, then they have no basis to affirm its existence. If they say they know that it exists but not its modality, this contradicts their earlier claim of total ignorance—they are in fact affirming a quality (existence) for the quality, which is absurd. The argument effectively exposes the self-contradiction in their position.

Key Terms
كيفية
إثبات الكيفية للكيفية
كرامية
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming that God has an unknown quality (kayfiyyah) is a safe way to avoid anthropomorphism because we do not specify it.
Correction: This passage shows that claiming an unknown quality is either baseless (if you claim ignorance) or leads to contradiction (if you claim to know its existence). Moreover, the term kayfiyyah itself implies physical attributes, which are impossible for God. True tanzih requires affirming only what Allah and His Messenger have affirmed, without modality (bilā kayf).

Checkpoint
What is the main logical problem that Imam Nasafi identifies in the claim that God has a quality (kayfiyyah) unknown to anyone except Him?
A) The claim is not found in the Quran or Sunnah.
B) If the proponents admit they do not know the quality, they have no basis to affirm its existence; if they claim to know it exists but not its modality, they contradict themselves.
C) The Karramiyyah who held this view were not reliable narrators.
D) The word 'kayfiyyah' does not appear in the Arabic language.

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi refutes the claim of an unknown divine quality by showing that it either lacks evidence or leads to logical contradiction, upholding the Sunni principle that Allah is beyond all physical attributes and qualifications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Allah's names and attributes, do you ever use 'only Allah knows' as an excuse to avoid thinking deeply about what they mean? Nasafi shows this is intellectually lazy and even dangerous. What is one attribute you have been avoiding reflecting on, and what will you do to understand it better?
?
Imagine you are pressed to defend your faith on a difficult point like God's nature. Are you tempted to retreat into vague unknowns? Nasafi exposes the incoherence of such a retreat. What is one specific belief about Allah that you hold without fully understanding, and how will you seek clarity?
?
Nasafi's argument forces a choice: either you know or you don't, and neither allows for an unknown quality. In your own spiritual life, where do you cling to comfortable unknowns about Allah's actions or decrees, and how can you replace that with submission to what He has revealed?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
The Rational Impossibility of God Being in a Place
Nasafi gives a rational argument against divine location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi argues that God cannot occupy space or direction, based on the principle that similarity to creation would imply temporality.

Core Idea
God's transcendence means He cannot be in any place or direction.

Before confronting the views of those who say God is in a place, Imam Nasafi first restates the foundational principle: God is unlike any part of the world. He then poses the key question—can God be established in a place, occupy space, or be fixed in a direction? The answer is a clear 'no', because such attributes would imply similarity to created things, which would compromise His pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Understand the logical flow: premise → question → negation.
Notice the use of 'ثم' (then) to sequence steps.
2
Identify the premise
The passage begins by recalling that God is not similar to any part of the world. This is the basis for the whole argument.
The phrase 'لا شبيه بشيء من أجزاء العالم' is key.
3
Examine the reasoning
Nasafi argues that affirming likeness would necessitate temporality (حدوث) and remove pre-eternity (قدم). Consider why that would be the case.
If God were like created things, He would share their property of being in a place, which is a characteristic of contingent beings.
4
Reflect on the negation
After stating the impossibility, the author says 'فنفينا ذلك كله عنه' (we negated all of that from Him). Understand that this is a logical conclusion, not merely a scriptural assertion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس الكلام في استحالة كون الصانع في المكان: ثم إذا ثبت أن صانع العالم جل وعز غير شبيه بشيء من أجزاء العالم، لما في إثبات المماثلة والمشابهة من إيجاب حدوثه، وإزالة قدمه، وهو محال ثم تأملنا أنه تعالى هل يجوز أن يكون متمكنا في مكان شاغلا لحيز ثابتا في جهة؟ فوجدنا ذلك كله محالا فنفينا ذلك كله عنه. شبه القائلين بأنه تعالى في مكان: وخالفنا في ذلك طوائف من الناس، أحدها من زعم أنه في مكان مخصوص كغلاة الروافض واليهود والكرامية، وجميع أنواع المجسمة فإنهم يقولون: إنه تعالى على العرش، والعرش عندهم السرير المحمول بالملائكة عليهم السلام المحفوف بهم على ما قال تعالى: وَيَحْمِلُ عَرْشَ رَبِّكَ فَوْقَهُمْ يَوْمَذٍ ثَمَانِيَةٌ. وقال تعالى: وَتَرَى الْمَلائِكَةَ حَافِّينَ مِنْ حَوْلِ الْعَرْشِ واختلفت عبارات الكرامية في ذلك فزعم بعضهم أنه مستقر على العرش، وزعم بعضهم أنه مماس للعرش، وزعم بعضهم أنه ملاق للعرش، وامتنع بعضهم عن إطلاق هذه العبارات، وزعم أنه على العرش بحيث لا واسطة بينهما، ثم اختلفت هؤلاء الكرامية فيما بينهم فمنهم من زعم أنه تعالى غير متناه من خمس جهات متناه بجهة واحدة وهي جهة
The sixth chapter: The discourse on the impossibility of the Maker being in place. Then, when it has been established that the Maker of the world, Glorious and Exalted, is not similar to anything from the parts of the world, because affirming likeness and similarity would necessitate His temporality (ḥudūth) and remove His pre-eternity (qidam), which is impossible. Then we considered: Is it permissible that He, Exalted, be established in a place, occupying a space (ḥayyiz), fixed in a direction (jihah)? We found all that impossible, so we negated all that from Him. The doubts of those who claim that He, Exalted, is in a place: We disagreed with them regarding that, [among them] groups of people: one of them is those who claim that He is in a specific place, like the extreme Rawāfiḍ, the Jews, and the Karramiyyah, and all types of anthropomorphists (mujassimah). For they say: He, Exalted, is upon the Throne (ʿarsh), and the Throne according to them is the bed carried by the angels, peace be upon them, surrounded by them, as He said: 'And eight will bear the Throne of your Lord above them on that Day' and He said: 'And you see the angels surrounding the Throne.' And the expressions of the Karramiyyah differed regarding that: some of them claimed that He is settled (mustaqirr) upon the Throne, some claimed that He is in contact (mumāss) with the Throne, some claimed that He is adjacent (mulāqī) to the Throne, and some refrained from using these expressions and claimed that He is upon the Throne with no intermediary between them. Then these Karramiyyah differed among themselves: some of them claimed that He, Exalted, is unlimited from five directions and limited in one direction, which is the direction
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the sixth chapter on the impossibility of God being in a place. Imam Nasafi first recapitulates the established premise that God is unlike any part of the world, because similarity would entail temporality. Then he posits the question: can God be established in a place, occupying space, fixed in a direction? He answers that all of that is impossible, so they negate such attributes from God. Next, he introduces the doubts (shubah) of those who claim God is in a place, listing groups: the extreme Rafidis, Jews, Karramiyyah, and all types of anthropomorphists (mujassimah). They claim God is upon the Throne, which they interpret as a physical bed carried by angels, citing Quranic verses. The author then details the internal disagreements among the Karramiyyah: some say God is settled on the Throne, some say He is in contact, some say adjacent, some avoid these terms and simply say 'upon the Throne' with no intermediary. Then they further differed: some claimed God is unlimited from five directions and limited from one direction (the passage is cut off here, but the idea is that they try to spatialize God). The text thus sets up the refutation to follow by clearly stating the positions and the contradictions within them.

حيز
جهة
متمكن
شاغل
مشابهة
مماثلة
حدوث
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is it impossible for God to be in a place?
A) The Quran does not mention God being in a place.
B) Being in a place would imply similarity to created things, which would make God temporal.
C) The Karramiyyah disagree among themselves.
D) The Throne is carried by angels, so God cannot be on it.

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence means He cannot be in any place, space, or direction, because that would make Him like created beings and therefore temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is with me' or 'God is above', are you aware that you might be inadvertently imagining Him in space? How can you train your heart to think of His presence without spatial direction?
?
This lesson teaches that any spatial quality would make God like His creation. Have you ever thought of God as 'up there' in a literal sense? What will you do to purge such images from your mind and replace them with absolute transcendence?
?
If God cannot be in any place, then your prayer toward the qiblah is not because God is in that direction, but because He commands it. How does this knowledge change the way you stand before Him in prayer?

Sects that Affirm God is in a Place
Nasafi lists sects that affirm divine location and their internal disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be familiar with the main groups that assert God is in a place, and the internal disagreements among the Karramiyyah regarding how God relates to the Throne.

Core Idea
Many sects fall into anthropomorphism by claiming God occupies a specific place like the Throne.

After establishing that God cannot be in a place, Imam Nasafi turns to the opposing views. He lists several groups—extreme Rafidis, Jews, Karramiyyah, and all types of anthropomorphists—who claim that God is on the Throne. He then details the disagreements among the Karramiyyah about the nature of that 'being on the Throne', showing the confusion inherent in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note how the author first mentions the groups, then the Karramiyyah disagreements.
Pay attention to the verbs used: 'زعم' (claimed) indicates these are their assertions, not facts.
2
Identify the groups
List the groups mentioned: extreme Rafidis, Jews, Karramiyyah, and all anthropomorphists (mujassimah). Understand that they all affirm God is on the Throne.
3
Understand the Throne concept
They interpret the Throne as a physical bed carried by angels, citing Quranic verses like 69:17 and 39:75. Notice they take these verses literally.
4
Analyze the Karramiyyah differences
The Karramiyyah disagreed: some said God is settled (mustaqirr), some said in contact (mumāss), some said adjacent (mulāqī), some avoided these terms and said 'with no intermediary'. Then they further differed: some said God is unlimited in five directions and limited in one (the direction of the Throne).
This shows internal inconsistency and confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس الكلام في استحالة كون الصانع في المكان: ثم إذا ثبت أن صانع العالم جل وعز غير شبيه بشيء من أجزاء العالم، لما في إثبات المماثلة والمشابهة من إيجاب حدوثه، وإزالة قدمه، وهو محال ثم تأملنا أنه تعالى هل يجوز أن يكون متمكنا في مكان شاغلا لحيز ثابتا في جهة؟ فوجدنا ذلك كله محالا فنفينا ذلك كله عنه. شبه القائلين بأنه تعالى في مكان: وخالفنا في ذلك طوائف من الناس، أحدها من زعم أنه في مكان مخصوص كغلاة الروافض واليهود والكرامية، وجميع أنواع المجسمة فإنهم يقولون: إنه تعالى على العرش، والعرش عندهم السرير المحمول بالملائكة عليهم السلام المحفوف بهم على ما قال تعالى: وَيَحْمِلُ عَرْشَ رَبِّكَ فَوْقَهُمْ يَوْمَذٍ ثَمَانِيَةٌ. وقال تعالى: وَتَرَى الْمَلائِكَةَ حَافِّينَ مِنْ حَوْلِ الْعَرْشِ واختلفت عبارات الكرامية في ذلك فزعم بعضهم أنه مستقر على العرش، وزعم بعضهم أنه مماس للعرش، وزعم بعضهم أنه ملاق للعرش، وامتنع بعضهم عن إطلاق هذه العبارات، وزعم أنه على العرش بحيث لا واسطة بينهما، ثم اختلفت هؤلاء الكرامية فيما بينهم فمنهم من زعم أنه تعالى غير متناه من خمس جهات متناه بجهة واحدة وهي جهة
The sixth chapter: The discourse on the impossibility of the Maker being in place. Then, when it has been established that the Maker of the world, Glorious and Exalted, is not similar to anything from the parts of the world, because affirming likeness and similarity would necessitate His temporality (ḥudūth) and remove His pre-eternity (qidam), which is impossible. Then we considered: Is it permissible that He, Exalted, be established in a place, occupying a space (ḥayyiz), fixed in a direction (jihah)? We found all that impossible, so we negated all that from Him. The doubts of those who claim that He, Exalted, is in a place: We disagreed with them regarding that, [among them] groups of people: one of them is those who claim that He is in a specific place, like the extreme Rawāfiḍ, the Jews, and the Karramiyyah, and all types of anthropomorphists (mujassimah). For they say: He, Exalted, is upon the Throne (ʿarsh), and the Throne according to them is the bed carried by the angels, peace be upon them, surrounded by them, as He said: 'And eight will bear the Throne of your Lord above them on that Day' and He said: 'And you see the angels surrounding the Throne.' And the expressions of the Karramiyyah differed regarding that: some of them claimed that He is settled (mustaqirr) upon the Throne, some claimed that He is in contact (mumāss) with the Throne, some claimed that He is adjacent (mulāqī) to the Throne, and some refrained from using these expressions and claimed that He is upon the Throne with no intermediary between them. Then these Karramiyyah differed among themselves: some of them claimed that He, Exalted, is unlimited from five directions and limited in one direction, which is the direction

Then he posits the question: can God be established in a place, occupying space, fixed in a direction? He answers that all of that is impossible, so they negate such attributes from God. Then they further differed: some claimed God is unlimited from five directions and limited from one direction (the passage is cut off here, but the idea is that they try to spatialize God). The text thus sets up the refutation to follow by clearly stating the positions and the contradictions within them.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all Muslims agree God is on the Throne in a literal sense.
Correction: This passage shows that many sects hold that view, but the author categorizes them as deviant (mujassimah) and highlights their internal contradictions.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a group mentioned by Nasafi as claiming God is in a specific place?
A) The extreme Rafidis
B) The Jews
C) The Mu'tazilites
D) The Karramiyyah

Key Takeaway
Many sects err by assigning a literal location to God, and even among them there is no agreement on how God relates to the Throne.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining God as being physically above the sky or seated on the Throne, even if only in a vague childlike way? This lesson teaches that such thoughts, if left unchecked, lead to anthropomorphism. Will you commit to actively replacing any mental image of God with a conceptual understanding based on His absolute transcendence, starting from this moment?
?
The Karramiyyah could not agree on how God is on the Throne—settled, touching, adjacent, or something else. This confusion shows the dead end of trying to pin down God's relation to His creation. Are you aware of any confusion in your own heart about where God is or how He relates to the world? Do you ever feel spiritually distant because you imagine Him as far away? How will you let the truth that He is not confined to any place transform your sense of nearness to Him?
?
Nasafi calls the claims of these sects 'shubah' (doubts). Are there any subtle doubts or vague thoughts in your mind about God's attributes that you have never examined? What specific step will you take today to purify your aqeedah from any trace of physicality, ensuring your worship is directed to the One who is beyond all location and limitation?

Divine Transcendence
beginner

The Proof that God Cannot Be in a Place
Nasafi proves the impossibility of the Creator occupying a place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi proves that the Creator cannot occupy a place, space, or direction, based on His necessary dissimilarity to creation.

Core Idea
If God were located in a place, He would be like His creation — impossible.

This lesson presents Imam Nasafi's concise argument for God's transcendence beyond spatial location. Building on the established truth that God is unlike anything in creation, he asks whether the Creator could be in a place, occupying space, or fixed in a direction. The answer, he concludes, is a clear impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Restate the premise
Recall that Nasafi has already established that God is not similar to any part of the world. This is the foundation for the proof.
2
Understand the consequence of similarity
If God were similar to creation, that would imply He is temporal (ḥādith) and not eternal (qadīm), which is impossible. So dissimilarity is necessary.
3
Consider spatial location
Nasafi then examines whether God could be 'tamakkun' in a place, occupying space (ḥayyiz), or fixed in a direction (jiha). All these are ways bodies exist.
4
Negate all spatial attributes
Since all spatial attributes would make God like creation, they are all negated from Him.
The argument is simple: God is unlike creation; spatial location is a property of creation; therefore God has no spatial location.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذا ثبت أن صانع العالم جل وعز غير شبيه بشيء من أجزاء العالم، لما فى إثبات المماثلة والمشابهة من إيجاب حدوثه، وإزالة قدمه، وهو محال ثم تأملنا أنه تعالى هل يجوز أن يكون متمكنا فى مكان شاغلا لحيز ثابتا في جهة؟ فوجدنا ذلك كله محالا فنفينا ذلك كله عنه.
Then, when it is established that the Maker of the world, may He be exalted and glorified, is not similar to anything from the parts of the world, because establishing likeness and similarity necessitates bringing about His temporality and removing His pre-eternality, and that is impossible; then we considered: Is it permissible for Him, exalted, to be located in a place, occupying a space, fixed in a direction? Then we found all of that impossible, so we negated all of that from Him.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the impossibility of God being in a place. The author begins by restating the prior conclusion: God is unlike any part of the world. This is established because if He were similar to any created thing, He would be subject to temporality (ḥudūth) and would lose His pre-eternality (qidam), which is impossible. Then, building on this foundation, the author introduces the specific question: can God be in a place, occupying space, and fixed in a direction? By 'tamakkun' (being located), 'shāghilan li-ḥayyiz' (occupying a space), and 'thābita fī jiha' (fixed in a direction), the author covers all ways in which a body might be spatially located. After examination, the author finds all of these impossible and therefore negates them from God. The reasoning is implicit: since God is unlike all created things, and all created things are spatially located, God cannot be spatially located.

Key Terms
مماثلة
مشابهة
حدوث
قدم
مكان
حيز
جهة
تمكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is circular because it assumes God is not in a place.
Correction: The premise is not that God is not in a place; it is that God is unlike creation. The impossibility of place is derived from that premise.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the consequence of establishing that God is similar (shabīh) to any part of the world?
A) It would prove His existence.
B) It would necessitate His temporality (ḥudūth) and negate His pre-eternality (qidam).
C) It would prove that He is in a place.
D) It would prove His omnipotence.

Key Takeaway
God is not in a place because being in a place would make Him similar to creation, which contradicts His necessary dissimilarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever unconsciously imagine Allah as being 'up there' or 'in a certain direction'? How does this lesson challenge that habit? Will you actively remind yourself of His transcendence before each prayer today?
?
If Allah is not confined to any place, then He is closer to you than your own jugular vein—not spatially, but through His knowledge and power. How should this change the way you seek His nearness? What specific action can you take to feel His closeness without spatializing Him?
?
This proof shows that likening Allah to creation leads to impossible consequences. Where in your heart have you unconsciously attributed human limitations—like needing rest, being affected by time, or having a body—to Allah? Commit to one concrete thought you will replace with a proper attribute of transcendence.

The Literalists Who Affirm God on the Throne
Nasafi lists the sects that affirm God is literally on the Throne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the identity and general position of the first group of opponents who claim God is literally on the Throne.

Core Idea
Many sects claim God sits on a physical throne, but they disagree on the details.

In this passage, Nasafi begins his refutation of those who ascribe a place to Allah by listing the sects that assert He is literally on the Throne. Here we identify the main groups—extremist Rawāfiḍ, Jews, Karāmiyya, and Mujassima—and their shared conviction, setting the stage for the subsequent discussion of their internal contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups
Read the passage and list the sects mentioned: Rawāfiḍ, Jews, Karāmiyya, and Mujassima.
These groups are all considered anthropomorphists (Mujassima) by the author.
2
Understand their claim
Notice that they all say 'He is upon the Throne' and interpret the Throne literally as a physical bed carried by angels.
3
Examine their proof
They cite Quranic verses (69:17 and 39:75) as evidence, which they take literally.
The author will later refute this literal reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبه القائلين بأنه تعالى في مكان]: وخالفنا في ذلك طوائف من الناس، أحدها من زعم أنه في مكان مخصوص كغلاة الروافض واليهود والكرامية، وجميع أنواع المجسمة فإنهم يقولون: إنه تعالى على العرش، والعرش عندهم السرير المحمول بالملائكة عليهم السلام المحفوف بهم على ما قال تعالى: وَيَحِلُ عَرْشَ رَبِّكَ فَوْقَهُمْ يَوْمَذٍ تَمَنيَةٌ . وقال تعالى: وَتَرَى الْمَلَيْكَةَ حَافِينَ مِنْ حَوْلِ الْعَرْشِ و اختلفت عبارات الكرامية في ذلك فزعم بعضهم أنه مستقر على العرش، وزعم بعضهم أنه مماس للعرش، وزعم بعضهم أنه ملاق للعرش، وامتنع بعضهم عن إطلاق هذه العبارات، وزعم تعالى على العرش بحيث لا واسطة بينهما، ثم اختلفت هؤلاء الكرامية فيما بينهم فمنهم من زعم أنه تعالى غير متناه من خمس جهات متناه بجهة واحدة وهي جهة السفل التي يلاقي بها العرش وقد ملأ ساحة العرش، وفضل عنه من الجوانب الأربع كلها، ومنهم من قال: هو مقدار ساحة العرش لا يفضل عنها، ومنهم من قال: إنه على جزء من أجزاء العرش، ومنهم من قال : إنه واحد وهو على سائر أجزاء العرش العظمته ومن المشبهة من يزعم أنه على العرش وقدماه على الكرسي - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا.
The doubt of those who say that He, exalted, is in a place: And in that we have been opposed by groups of people. One of them is those who claim that He is in a specific place, like the extremists of the Rawāfiḍ, the Jews, the Karāmiyya, and all types of the Mujassima. For they say: He, exalted, is upon the Throne, and the Throne according to them is the bed carried by the angels, peace be upon them, surrounded by them, according to what He said: 'And on that day, the Throne of your Lord will be borne above them, eight [angels]' (69:17), and He said: 'And you see the angels encircling the Throne' (39:75). And the expressions of the Karāmiyya differed in that: some of them claimed that He is settled (mustaqirr) upon the Throne; some claimed that He is in contact (mumāss) with the Throne; some claimed that He is adjacent (mulāq) to the Throne; some refrained from using these expressions and claimed that He is upon the Throne such that there is no intermediary between them. Then these Karāmiyya differed among themselves: some claimed that He, exalted, is unlimited in five directions and limited in one direction, which is the lower direction by which He contacts the Throne, and He has filled the courtyard of the Throne, and overflowed from it from all four sides; some said: He is the measure of the courtyard of the Throne, not exceeding it; some said: He is upon part of the parts of the Throne; some said: He is one, and He is upon all parts of the Throne by His greatness. And among the Mushabbiha is one who claims that He is upon the Throne and His feet are upon the Kursi – exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the first group of opponents who claim God is in a specific place, specifically the Throne. The author identifies these groups as extremists among the Rawāfiḍ, the Jews, the Karāmiyya, and the Mujassima (anthropomorphists). They all claim that God is upon the Throne, which they interpret literally as a physical throne carried by angels, citing Quranic verses (69:17 and 39:75). The author then delves into the internal disagreements among the Karāmiyya, a major sect within this group: some say God is 'settled' (mustaqirr) on the Throne, some say He is 'in contact' (mumāss), some say 'adjacent' (mulāq), and others refrain from these terms, simply saying He is upon it without an intermediary. Further subdivisions among the Karāmiyya concern the extent of God's spatial occupation: some say He is unlimited in five directions and limited in the lower direction where He contacts the Throne, having filled the Throne's courtyard and overflowed; some say He exactly matches the Throne's area; some say He is on a part of the Throne; some say He is one and occupies all parts by His greatness. Finally, the author mentions the Mushabbiha (comparisonists) who go so far as to say God's feet are on the Footstool (Kursi). The passage concludes with a Quranic phrase rejecting such statements.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All anthropomorphists hold the same view about God being on the Throne.
Correction: The passage shows that while many groups claim God is on the Throne, they differ significantly in details, as further sections will show.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Nasafi identifies four main literalist groups that claim God is physically on the Throne, using literal readings of Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Nasafi lists four groups that interpret the Throne literally, do you ever find yourself leaning toward a literal, physical understanding of Allah because it feels more tangible? How does that challenge your belief in His absolute transcendence?
?
The Karāmiyya were known for deep devotion yet held this anthropomorphic belief. Are there areas in your worship where your desire to feel close to Allah might lead you to imagine Him in human terms? What would it cost you to surrender that image?
?
The Jews here are singled out for their anthropomorphic tendencies. Have you ever judged an entire group based on a label without understanding their nuanced beliefs? Commit to learning the actual positions of others before forming conclusions.

Internal Divisions Among the Karāmiyya and Mushabbiha
Nasafi details the internal disagreements among the Karāmiyya and the extreme claim of the Mushabbiha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diverse positions held by the Karāmiyya sect regarding God's relationship to the Throne, including their various expressions and spatial descriptions, as well as the extreme claim of the Mushabbiha.

Core Idea
The Karāmiyya differ wildly on how God is on the Throne — from contact to adjacency to exact measure.

While all Karāmiyya affirm God is on the Throne, they cannot agree on what that means. Nasafi details their contradictory statements, exposing the incoherence of literalist theology. This lesson examines those internal divisions and the even more extreme claim of the Mushabbiha about God's feet on the Footstool.

Guided Path
1
Note the different expressions
Read how some Karāmiyya say God is 'settled' (mustaqirr), others say 'in contact' (mumāss), others 'adjacent' (mulāq), and some avoid such terms.
This shows their confusion over how God relates to the Throne.
2
Examine the spatial subdivisions
Notice how they further differ on whether God fills the Throne's area, exceeds it, or is exactly its size.
3
Identify the Mushabbiha claim
See that one group says God's feet are on the Kursi, an even more anthropomorphic statement.
The author ends with a Quranic verse denouncing them.
4
Reflect on the contradictions
Think about why such disagreements arise from a literalist approach. The Maturidi position avoids these problems by affirming transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبه القائلين بأنه تعالى في مكان]: وخالفنا في ذلك طوائف من الناس، أحدها من زعم أنه في مكان مخصوص كغلاة الروافض واليهود والكرامية، وجميع أنواع المجسمة فإنهم يقولون: إنه تعالى على العرش، والعرش عندهم السرير المحمول بالملائكة عليهم السلام المحفوف بهم على ما قال تعالى: وَيَحِلُ عَرْشَ رَبِّكَ فَوْقَهُمْ يَوْمَذٍ تَمَنيَةٌ . وقال تعالى: وَتَرَى الْمَلَيْكَةَ حَافِينَ مِنْ حَوْلِ الْعَرْشِ و اختلفت عبارات الكرامية في ذلك فزعم بعضهم أنه مستقر على العرش، وزعم بعضهم أنه مماس للعرش، وزعم بعضهم أنه ملاق للعرش، وامتنع بعضهم عن إطلاق هذه العبارات، وزعم تعالى على العرش بحيث لا واسطة بينهما، ثم اختلفت هؤلاء الكرامية فيما بينهم فمنهم من زعم أنه تعالى غير متناه من خمس جهات متناه بجهة واحدة وهي جهة السفل التي يلاقي بها العرش وقد ملأ ساحة العرش، وفضل عنه من الجوانب الأربع كلها، ومنهم من قال: هو مقدار ساحة العرش لا يفضل عنها، ومنهم من قال: إنه على جزء من أجزاء العرش، ومنهم من قال : إنه واحد وهو على سائر أجزاء العرش العظمته ومن المشبهة من يزعم أنه على العرش وقدماه على الكرسي - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا.
The doubt of those who say that He, exalted, is in a place: And in that we have been opposed by groups of people. One of them is those who claim that He is in a specific place, like the extremists of the Rawāfiḍ, the Jews, the Karāmiyya, and all types of the Mujassima. For they say: He, exalted, is upon the Throne, and the Throne according to them is the bed carried by the angels, peace be upon them, surrounded by them, according to what He said: 'And on that day, the Throne of your Lord will be borne above them, eight [angels]' (69:17), and He said: 'And you see the angels encircling the Throne' (39:75). And the expressions of the Karāmiyya differed in that: some of them claimed that He is settled (mustaqirr) upon the Throne; some claimed that He is in contact (mumāss) with the Throne; some claimed that He is adjacent (mulāq) to the Throne; some refrained from using these expressions and claimed that He is upon the Throne such that there is no intermediary between them. Then these Karāmiyya differed among themselves: some claimed that He, exalted, is unlimited in five directions and limited in one direction, which is the lower direction by which He contacts the Throne, and He has filled the courtyard of the Throne, and overflowed from it from all four sides; some said: He is the measure of the courtyard of the Throne, not exceeding it; some said: He is upon part of the parts of the Throne; some said: He is one, and He is upon all parts of the Throne by His greatness. And among the Mushabbiha is one who claims that He is upon the Throne and His feet are upon the Kursi – exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation.

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the first group of opponents who claim God is in a specific place, specifically the Throne. The author identifies these groups as extremists among the Rawāfiḍ, the Jews, the Karāmiyya, and the Mujassima (anthropomorphists). They all claim that God is upon the Throne, which they interpret literally as a physical throne carried by angels, citing Quranic verses (69:17 and 39:75). The author then delves into the internal disagreements among the Karāmiyya, a major sect within this group: some say God is 'settled' (mustaqirr) on the Throne, some say He is 'in contact' (mumāss), some say 'adjacent' (mulāq), and others refrain from these terms, simply saying He is upon it without an intermediary. Further subdivisions among the Karāmiyya concern the extent of God's spatial occupation: some say He is unlimited in five directions and limited in the lower direction where He contacts the Throne, having filled the Throne's courtyard and overflowed; some say He exactly matches the Throne's area; some say He is on a part of the Throne; some say He is one and occupies all parts by His greatness. Finally, the author mentions the Mushabbiha (comparisonists) who go so far as to say God's feet are on the Footstool (Kursi). The passage concludes with a Quranic phrase rejecting such statements.

Is there any area of your faith where you are holding onto a literal reading that actually diminishes your awe of God's majesty? Make a concrete intention to return to the early Muslim understanding of tanzīh.
