The 'Every Place' Claim: Mu'tazila and Najjariyya
Nasafi presents the second group of opponents and their two interpretations of God being in every place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct interpretations of the claim that God is in every place, as held by the Mu'tazila and the Najjariyya.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila say 'everywhere' means knowledge; some Najjariyya say it means essence.

In this passage, Nasafi introduces the second group of opponents who assert that God is not in any specific place but is present everywhere. However, this statement is ambiguous: does it mean God's essence is everywhere, or does it refer to His knowledge and governance? The Mu'tazila and the Najjariyya differ on this point, and we will examine their views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the internal disagreement within the Najjariyya.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the main position (knowledge/governance) and the alternative (essence) as reported by al-Nawbakhti.
3
Compare the interpretations
Compare the Mu'tazila's metaphorical presence with the literal essence-presence of some Najjariyya.
This highlights the spectrum of views on divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطائفة الثانية من المخالفين يقولون: إنه تعالى ليس في مكان مخصوص بل هو بكل مكان ثم يفسرون هذه العبارة فيقولون: لا تعني أنه بذاته في شيء من الأمكنة، بل نعني بذلك أنه عالم بها، مدبر لها وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة، والنجارية وحكى أبو محمد النوبختي عمن رآه وناظره من أصحاب الحسين النجار أنه قال: إن الله تعالى بكل مكان بذاته لا بمعنى العلم والتدبير والطائفة الثالثة من المخالفين المتأخرون من الكرامية أنه تعالى ليس على العرش بل هو فوق العرش وبينهما مسافة ولا يثبتون إلا الجهة، وساعدونا على بطلان كونه على العرش على طريق المماسة والاستقرار.
And the second group of the opponents say: He, exalted, is not in a specific place, but rather He is in every place. Then they interpret this expression and say: It does not mean that He is by His essence in any location, but rather we mean by that that He is knowledgeable of them, a disposer of them. And to this went the Mu'tazila and the Najjariyya. And Abu Muhammad al-Nawbakhti narrated from someone who saw and debated with the followers of al-Husayn al-Najjār that he said: 'Indeed God, exalted, is in every place by His essence, not in the sense of knowledge and disposition.' And the third group of the opponents are the later ones from the Karāmiyya: [they say] that He, exalted, is not upon the Throne, but rather He is above the Throne, and between them is a distance, and they only affirm direction. And they have aided us regarding the invalidity of His being upon the Throne in the manner of contact and settlement.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents the second and third groups of opponents. The second group are those who say God is not in a specific place but in every place. They interpret this as a metaphorical presence: He is everywhere by His knowledge and governance, not by His essence. This is the position of the Mu'tazila and the Najjariyya. However, the author notes a variation: according to a report from Abu Muhammad al-Nawbakhti, some followers of al-Husayn al-Najjār (founder of the Najjariyya) affirm that God is in every place by His essence, not merely by knowledge. The third group consists of later Karāmiyya who hold that God is not on the Throne (in the sense of contact or settlement) but is above the Throne with a distance separating them; they only affirm a direction (jiha) for God. The author notes that this third group agrees with him that God being on the Throne in the manner of contact or settlement is false, though they still affirm a direction.

Key Terms

نجارية
حسين النجار
علم
تدبير
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all who say 'God is everywhere' mean His essence is literally everywhere.
Correction: The Mu'tazila explicitly deny essence-presence and interpret it as knowledge and governance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the Mu'tazila interpret the statement that God is in every place?
A) They mean God's essence is physically present everywhere.
B) They mean God is knowledgeable of and governs all places.
C) They mean God is only in the heavens.
D) They mean God is not present anywhere.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila and most Najjariyya deny God's essence is in any place, interpreting 'everywhere' as His knowledge and governance, but some Najjariyya affirm essence-presence everywhere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you casually say 'God is everywhere,' what do you truly believe in your heart? Have you ever paused to consider whether you mean His knowledge, His power, or His very essence—and does that distinction change how you live?
?
The Najjariyya split reveals that even sincere believers can hold contradictory views on the same phrase. How carefully do you examine your own theological assumptions? Are you willing to let the precise words of revelation correct an imprecise faith?
?
If you knew that God's actual essence is not physically present in the place where you sin, would you feel less watched—or more accountable, knowing He is nonetheless fully aware and in control? Examine your own conscience: which position makes you more careful with your actions?

The Later Karramiyya: Above the Throne but Not on It
Nasafi presents the view of the later Karramiyya about God being above the Throne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of the later Karramiyya regarding God's relationship to the Throne and direction.

Core Idea
The later Karramiyya affirm direction but deny contact with the Throne.

Nasafi now turns to the third group of opponents: the later Karramiyya. They hold a nuanced position: God is not on the Throne as a physical contact, but He is above the Throne with a distance. They affirm direction (jiha) but reject the notions of contact (mumassa) and settlement (istiqrar). Interestingly, the author notes that they agree with him that God being on the Throne in the manner of contact is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the third group

3
Understand the common ground
The author says they have 'aided us' regarding the invalidity of contact and settlement. Reflect on how this partial agreement functions in the argument.
This shows a shared rejection of anthropomorphism.

Anthropomorphist Views on Allah's Location on the Throne
Nasafi enumerates various anthropomorphist claims about Allah on the Throne and refutes them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various anthropomorphist claims about Allah's physical location on the Throne and how the author refutes them.

Core Idea
Claims of Allah sitting on the Throne reduce Him to a body.

The author begins by describing the extreme views of those who liken Allah to creation. Among them, some say the Throne is filled with Allah's entity, others say He is on a part of the Throne, and some even attribute feet to Him. This lesson examines these claims and the author's decisive rejection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the various anthropomorphist opinions listed.
2
Identify the different claims
Notice how the passage lists several opinions: some say the Throne is filled, others say it is exactly the measure, some say He is on a part, others on all parts.
3
Understand the rejection
The author ends with 'exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say', showing these claims are false.
This is a strong refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السفل التي يلاقي بها العرش وقد ملأ ساحة العرش، وفضل عنه من الجوانب الأربع كلها، ومنهم من قال: هو مقدار ساحة العرش لا يفضل عنها، ومنهم من قال: إنه على جزء من أجزاء العرش، ومنهم من قال : إنه واحد وهو على سائر أجزاء العرش العظمته ومن المشبهة من يزعم أنه على العرش وقدماه على - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا. (۱) الكرسي الله تعالى في كل مكان بمعنى العلم والتدبير عند المعتزلة]: والطائفة الثانية من المخالفين يقولون: إنه تعالى ليس في مكان مخصوص بل هو بكل مكان ثم يفسرون هذه العبارة فيقولون: لا تعني أنه بذاته في شيء من الأمكنة، بل نعني بذلك أنه عالم بها، مدبر لها وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة (۲)، والنجارية وحكي أبو محمد النوبختي عمن رآه وناظره من أصحاب الحسين النجار أنه قال: إن الله تعالى بكل مكان بذاته لا بمعنى العلم والتدبير والطائفة الثالثة من المخالفين المتأخرون من الكرامية أنه تعالى ليس على العرش بل هو فوق العرش وبينهما مسافة ولا يثبتون إلا (۳) الجهة، وساعدونا على بطلان كونه على العرش على طريق المماسة والاستقرار.
The lower [parts] that meet the Throne have filled the court of the Throne, and exceed it from all four sides. And some of them said: it is the measure of the court of the Throne, it does not exceed it. And some of them said: it is upon a part of the parts of the Throne. And some of them said: it is one and it is upon the rest of the parts of the Throne due to His greatness. And among the anthropomorphists is one who claims that He is upon the Throne and His feet are upon — exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. (1) The Kursi. Allah Almighty is in every place in the sense of knowledge and governance according to the Mu'tazila: And the second group of the opponents says: He Almighty is not in a specific place, but rather He is in every place. Then they interpret this expression and say: it does not mean that He is by His essence in any of the places, but we mean by that that He is Knower of them, Disposer of them. This is the view of the Mu'tazila (2), and the Najjariyya. Abu Muhammad al-Nawbakhti related from one he saw and debated among the followers of al-Husayn al-Najjar that he said: Allah Almighty is in every place by His essence, not in the sense of knowledge and governance. And the third group of the opponents, the later ones among the Karramiyya, [say] that He Almighty is not upon the Throne, but rather He is above the Throne, and there is a distance between them, and they affirm nothing but (3) direction, and they have agreed with us on the invalidity of His being upon the Throne in the way of contact and settlement.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage surveys various deviant opinions about Allah's location. The author first describes the views of the anthropomorphists (Mushabbiha) regarding the Throne, with some saying the Throne fills the court and exceeds it, others that it is exactly the measure, some that it is on a part of the Throne, and some that it is one part upon all parts. Then he mentions that some anthropomorphists claim Allah is on the Throne with His feet. The author exclaims that Allah is exalted above such claims. Next, he turns to the second group: the Mu'tazila and Najjariyya. The Mu'tazila say Allah is in every place, but they interpret this to mean He is knower and disposer, not by essence. However, a report from al-Nawbakhti states that al-Najjar himself held that Allah is in every place by essence, not just by knowledge. The third group is the later Karramiyya, who say Allah is above the Throne with a distance between them, affirming only direction (jiha) but not contact or settlement on the Throne. The author notes that on the point of rejecting contact and settlement on the Throne, these Karramiyya agree with the Maturidis.

Key Terms

العرش
الكرسي
المماسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah physically sits on the Throne like a creature.
Correction: Allah is transcendent and not confined to space.

Checkpoint

A) That Allah fills the Throne.
B) That Allah is exactly the measure of the Throne.
C) That Allah has feet upon the Throne.
D) That Allah is on all parts of the Throne.

Key Takeaway
Anthropomorphists wrongly claim Allah occupies space on or above the Throne, but Allah is far above such limitations.

Mu'tazila and Najjariyya on 'Allah in Every Place'
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila and Najjariyya positions on Allah's omnipresence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between the Mu'tazila interpretation of 'Allah is everywhere' as knowledge and the Najjariyya view of literal essence.

Core Idea
Saying 'Allah is everywhere' can mean knowledge or essence — a crucial distinction.

The second group of opponents claim Allah is not in a specific place but everywhere. However, they disagree on what that means. The Mu'tazila interpret it as knowledge and governance, while al-Najjar and his followers assert that Allah is physically present everywhere by His essence. This lesson clarifies these two positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the Mu'tazila and Najjariyya views.
2
Distinguish the two views
Note that the Mu'tazila say 'in every place' means knowledge, while al-Najjar says it means essence.
3
Understand the implication
This distinction is important because it shows that even among those who affirm ubiquity, some deny Allah's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السفل التي يلاقي بها العرش وقد ملأ ساحة العرش، وفضل عنه من الجوانب الأربع كلها، ومنهم من قال: هو مقدار ساحة العرش لا يفضل عنها، ومنهم من قال: إنه على جزء من أجزاء العرش، ومنهم من قال : إنه واحد وهو على سائر أجزاء العرش العظمته ومن المشبهة من يزعم أنه على العرش وقدماه على - تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا. (۱) الكرسي الله تعالى في كل مكان بمعنى العلم والتدبير عند المعتزلة]: والطائفة الثانية من المخالفين يقولون: إنه تعالى ليس في مكان مخصوص بل هو بكل مكان ثم يفسرون هذه العبارة فيقولون: لا تعني أنه بذاته في شيء من الأمكنة، بل نعني بذلك أنه عالم بها، مدبر لها وإليه ذهبت المعتزلة (۲)، والنجارية وحكي أبو محمد النوبختي عمن رآه وناظره من أصحاب الحسين النجار أنه قال: إن الله تعالى بكل مكان بذاته لا بمعنى العلم والتدبير والطائفة الثالثة من المخالفين المتأخرون من الكرامية أنه تعالى ليس على العرش بل هو فوق العرش وبينهما مسافة ولا يثبتون إلا (۳) الجهة، وساعدونا على بطلان كونه على العرش على طريق المماسة والاستقرار.
The lower [parts] that meet the Throne have filled the court of the Throne, and exceed it from all four sides. And some of them said: it is the measure of the court of the Throne, it does not exceed it. And some of them said: it is upon a part of the parts of the Throne. And some of them said: it is one and it is upon the rest of the parts of the Throne due to His greatness. And among the anthropomorphists is one who claims that He is upon the Throne and His feet are upon — exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. (1) The Kursi. Allah Almighty is in every place in the sense of knowledge and governance according to the Mu'tazila: And the second group of the opponents says: He Almighty is not in a specific place, but rather He is in every place. Then they interpret this expression and say: it does not mean that He is by His essence in any of the places, but we mean by that that He is Knower of them, Disposer of them. This is the view of the Mu'tazila (2), and the Najjariyya. Abu Muhammad al-Nawbakhti related from one he saw and debated among the followers of al-Husayn al-Najjar that he said: Allah Almighty is in every place by His essence, not in the sense of knowledge and governance. And the third group of the opponents, the later ones among the Karramiyya, [say] that He Almighty is not upon the Throne, but rather He is above the Throne, and there is a distance between them, and they affirm nothing but (3) direction, and they have agreed with us on the invalidity of His being upon the Throne in the way of contact and settlement.

This passage surveys various deviant opinions about Allah's location. The author first describes the views of the anthropomorphists (Mushabbiha) regarding the Throne, with some saying the Throne fills the court and exceeds it, others that it is exactly the measure, some that it is on a part of the Throne, and some that it is one part upon all parts. The third group is the later Karramiyya, who say Allah is above the Throne with a distance between them, affirming only direction (jiha) but not contact or settlement on the Throne. The author notes that on the point of rejecting contact and settlement on the Throne, these Karramiyya agree with the Maturidis.

Karramiyya View: Direction without Contact
Nasafi outlines the Karramiyya claim that Allah is above the Throne with direction but no contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya position that Allah is above the Throne with direction but without contact or settlement.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya affirm direction above but reject contact.

The third group, the later Karramiyya, attempt to stake a middle ground. They deny that Allah is on the Throne in the sense of contact or settlement, but they insist that He is above the Throne with a distance between them, and they affirm only direction (jiha). Notably, they agree with the Maturidis in rejecting physical contact with the Throne.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to see the Karramiyya view.
2
Identify what they affirm and deny
They affirm direction and distance, but deny contact and settlement.
3
Note the agreement with Maturidis
The passage says they 'helped us' in rejecting contact and settlement on the Throne.
This shows a point of agreement despite other errors.

Arguments of Those Who Claim Allah is in a Specific Place
Nasafi enumerates the opponents' textual and rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key textual and rational arguments used by those who claim Allah is in a specific place, and how these arguments are based on common interpretations of scripture and natural disposition.

Core Idea
Those who claim Allah is in a specific place rely on both explicit verses and rational arguments about existence and direction.

Before presenting his refutation, Imam al-Nasafi lays out the full case made by his opponents. This passage collects the verses they cite, the Muslim practice of raising hands, and three rational arguments centered on two self-subsistent entities and the necessity of direction. Understanding their position is essential before seeing how it is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two categories of proof
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage first presents textual evidences (Quranic verses and Muslim practice) followed by three rational arguments.
The rational arguments are marked by 'one of them', 'the second', and 'the third'.
2
Examine the textual proofs: the verses cited
List the verses mentioned: about istiwā' on the Throne, 'who is in the heaven', 'in the heaven God', 'above His servants', and the concept of 'sending down'. Reflect on how each is interpreted to imply a physical direction.
These verses are often cited by anthropomorphist groups.
3
Consider the argument from Muslim practice and natural disposition
The opponents point to the universal practice of raising hands upwards in supplication and claim it reflects an innate belief that Allah is above. Note how this argument appeals to common experience.
Nasafi later refutes this by showing it is merely a convention.
4
Analyze the three rational arguments
Each rational argument is based on the idea that two self-subsistent entities (Allah and the world) must relate spatially. Break down each: (1) two self-subsistent entities require direction, (2) creation must be either inside or outside Allah's essence, (3) two existents imply one of six directions.
These are metaphysical claims that the author will challenge.
5
Reflect on why these arguments appear persuasive
Consider why these textual and rational arguments might convince someone. They rely on apparent meanings of scripture and common sense intuitions about existence. This sets the stage for the refutation.
The refutation will address each point systematically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على القائلين بأنه تعالى في مكان]: أما القائلون بأنه تعالى في مكان مخصوص فإنهم يحتجون بالنصوص من نحو قوله تعالى: (١) الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى (٢) ، وقوله : ثُمَّ اسْتَوَى عَلَى الْعَرْشِ (۳)، وقوله: أمنكُم مِّن فِي السَّمَاء (4) ، وقوله : ( وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ إِلَهُ وَفي ( وَفِي الْأَرْضِ اله ) ، وقوله : وَهُوَ الْقَاهِرُ فَوْقَ عِبَادِهِ (٦)، قوله : إِنَّا أَنزَلْتَهُ فِي لَيْلَةِ الْقَدْرِ () والإنزال هو ﴿ الإرسال من الأعلى إلى الأسفل، وأجمع المسلمين عليه فإنهم عند سؤالهم الحاجات (۸) ، واشتغالهم بالدعاء والمناجاة يرمون بأبصارهم، ويرفعون أيديهم إلى جهة العلو، وكذا الناس مجبولون على هذا، فإنهم لو تركوا وما هم عليه من الجبلة ولم ينقلوا عنه بالحيل لما اعتقدوا إلا ذلك أعني أنه تعالى بذاته في الجهة العلوية دون غيرها من الجهات، ويتعلقون أيضا بشبه لهم معقولة أحدها: أن الله تعالى موجود قائم بنفسه، والعالم موجود قائم بنفسه، ولن يعقل القائمان بأنفسهما إلا وأحدهما في جهة (من صاحبه) (۹) . والثانية: أنه تعالى لما خلق العالم إما أن خلقه في ذاته، وإما إن خلقه خارج ذاته، فإن خلقه في ذاته فهو محيط بالعالم من جميع جهاته، وإن خلقه خارج ذاته بقي من العالم بجهة من الجهات لا محالة. والثالثة أن الموجودين لا يعقلان موجودين إلا وأحدهما في جهة من الجهات الست من صاحبه
Refutation of those who say that He Almighty is in a place]: As for those who say that He Almighty is in a specific place, they adduce textual proofs such as His saying: (1) 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' (2), and His saying: 'Then He established Himself upon the Throne' (3), and His saying: 'Are you safe from Him who is in the heaven' (4), and His saying: 'And He is the one who in the heaven is God and in the earth is God' (5), and His saying: 'And He is the Subduer above His servants' (6), His saying: 'Indeed We sent it down in the Night of Decree' (7) — and sending down is sending from the higher to the lower — and the consensus of the Muslims upon it, for when they ask for needs (8) and engage in supplication and private prayer, they point their eyes and raise their hands towards the direction of above. Likewise, people are naturally disposed to this, for if they were left to their natural disposition and were not diverted by tricks, they would not believe except that — that is, that He Almighty is by His essence in the upper direction to the exclusion of other directions. And they also hold to rational arguments: one of them is that Allah Almighty is existent, subsistent by Himself, and the world is existent, subsistent by itself, and two entities subsistent by themselves cannot be conceived except that one of them is in a direction (from the other) (9). The second: that when He Almighty created the world, either He created it within His essence, or He created it outside His essence. If He created it within His essence, then He surrounds the world from all its directions; if He created it outside His essence, then He occupies a direction from the world inevitably. The third: that two existents cannot be conceived as existent except that one of them is in one of the six directions from the other.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage begins the refutation of those who claim Allah is in a specific place. The author first presents the textual proofs they rely on: verses about istiwā' (establishing on the Throne), 'who is in the heaven', 'in the heaven God', 'above His servants', and the concept of 'sending down' (inzāl) implying higher to lower. They also argue from the universal Muslim practice of raising hands upward in supplication, claiming it indicates a natural human disposition to believe Allah is in the upper direction. Then the author lists three rational arguments: (1) Two self-subsistent entities must be in a direction relative to each other; (2) When creating the world, Allah either created it within Himself (making Him surround it) or outside Himself (making Him in a direction from it); (3) Two existents always occupy one of the six directions relative to each other. The passage ends mid-sentence, and the author's refutation of these arguments likely follows in the subsequent text.

العرش
الاستواء
الجهة
الإنزال
الجِبِلَّة
القائمان بنفسه
الجهات الست

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Raising hands in supplication proves that Allah is physically above or in a specific direction.
Correction: Nasafi later shows that this practice is merely a convention and does not indicate that Allah is in a spatial location; it is a respectful gesture, not a theological proof.

Checkpoint
What is the first rational argument presented by those who claim Allah is in a specific place?
A) The world is finite and must have a boundary.
B) Two self-subsistent entities must be in a direction relative to each other.
C) Allah created the world in Himself.
D) The Qur'an explicitly says Allah is on the Throne.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' case rests on literal readings of verses, a practice-based appeal, and three rational arguments that assume spatial relation between two coexisting entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you raise your hands to pray, do you ever find yourself imagining Allah as being 'up there'? How does this lesson challenge you to purify your understanding of where Allah is?
?
The opponents argue that people are naturally disposed to believe Allah is above. Have you ever held such a belief without questioning it? What does this passage compel you to re-examine in your own heart about the nature of Allah?
?
The rational arguments treat Allah and the world as two comparable existents. Have you ever thought about Allah in such a comparative way? What is the danger of applying human logic to the Creator, and how can you guard your heart against that?

Refuting Direction by Divine Incomparability
Refutation of direction using scriptural and rational arguments on similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Haqq use the verse 'There is nothing like Him' combined with rational analysis of place and equality to refute the attribution of direction to Allah.

Core Idea
Affirming a place for Allah implies similarity, contradicting His incomparability.

This passage presents a key Maturidi argument against those who claim Allah is in a direction. The refutation is both scriptural and rational: starting from the Qur'anic negation of likeness, the author argues that place and occupant are equal in measure, and equality is a type of similarity. Thus, if Allah had a place, He would be like that place—which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the source text
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice that the passage ends abruptly with 'والله'; the argument is complete in structure.
2
Understand similarity as a genus
The author defines similarity (مماثلة) as a genus comprising types like equality (مساواة). Two equal things are considered similar.
3
Examine the place-occupant equality
The argument states that a place and the thing occupying it are equal in measure. The place is precisely the measure occupied; anything beyond is not truly the place.
The chair example clarifies the distinction between literal and metaphorical place.
4
Trace the contradiction
If Allah were in a place, He would be equal to that place in measure, implying similarity. But the verse 'There is nothing like Him' negates all likeness. Therefore, Allah cannot be in a direction.
5
Reflect on the refutation's strength
Consider how this argument combines scripture (the verse) with rational reasoning (analysis of place and equality). It refutes direction without positing any specific location for Allah.
This is a classic Maturidi approach: use revealed text and reason together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أهل الحق فإنهم تعلقوا بقول الله تعالى: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ، فالله تعالى نفى أن يكون له مثل من الأشياء، وقد بينا أن المتساويين من حيث الكمية مثلان لما مر من كون المماثلة جنسا تحته أنواع، وهي المشابهة، والمساواة، والمشاكلة، والمضاهاة، وكل نوع منها ينطلق عليه اسم المماثلة، فالمتساويان إذا متماثلان والمكان والمتمكن متساويان قدرا إذ مكان كل متمكن هو القدر الذي تمكن فيه المتمكن، فأما ما فضل عنه فليس بمكان له حقيقة، ولو سمي مكانا له كان ذلك مجازا، فمكان الجالس على السرير قدر ما تمكن فيه الجالس من السرير، وما وراء ذلك ليس بمكان له حقيقة، ويسمى السرير مكانا له مجازا لاشتماله على ما هو مكانه، ولهذا قسم الأوائل الظروف إلى القريب منها والبعيد في الزمان والمكان جميعا، وإذا ثبت هذا ثبتت مماثلة المكان والمتمكن لاستوائهما في القدر، والله
As for the people of truth, they adhere to the saying of God, exalted: 'There is nothing like unto Him.' So God, exalted, negated that He has any likeness among things. And we have explained that two equal things in terms of quantity are similar, according to what has passed regarding similarity being a genus under which are types: resemblance, equality, similarity, and analogy, and each type is called by the name of similarity. So two equal things are similar. And place and the thing placed are equal in measure, for the place of each thing placed is the measure in which the thing placed is located. As for what is extra, it is not truly a place for it, and if it is called its place, that is metaphorical. So the place of the one sitting on a chair is the measure occupied by the sitter on the chair, and what is beyond that is not truly a place for him, and the chair is called his place metaphorically because it includes what is his place. For this reason, the ancients divided containers into near and far in both time and space. And when this is established, the similarity of place and thing placed is established due to their equality in measure. And God...
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This lesson provides the response of Ahl al-Haqq to those who affirm direction. The refutation hinges on the verse 'ليس كمثله شيء' (There is nothing like Him). The author explains that similarity (مماثلة) is a genus with types including equality (مساواة) in quantity. Two equal things are similar. Then he argues that a place (مكان) and the thing placed (متمكن) are equal in measure, because the place is precisely the measure occupied. Anything beyond that is not truly a place but a metaphorical extension. For example, a sitter's place is only the part of the chair he occupies; the rest of the chair is called place metaphorically. Thus, if Allah were in a place, He would be equal in measure to that place, implying similarity. Since Allah negated any likeness, He cannot be in a place or direction. The argument uses both scriptural and rational reasoning, showing that affirming direction leads to anthropomorphism.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation of direction is purely based on scripture (the verse).
Correction: The argument also uses rational analysis: it demonstrates that place and occupant are equal in measure, and equality is a type of similarity. Thus, affirming a place leads to anthropomorphism logically.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Allah's incomparability (tanzih) prohibits attributing a place or direction to Him, as it would imply similarity between Creator and creation.

Rational Proof for Allah's Direction Without Place
The author presents the argument of those who affirm a direction for Allah without place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for affirming a direction (jiha) for Allah without place, and how it distinguishes direction from place and specifies the direction as above.

Core Idea
Affirming direction for Allah is distinct from place and necessary to avoid implying nonexistence.

This passage presents a rational argument used by those who affirm a direction for Allah without confining Him to a place. It begins by comparing substances and their 'where', then argues that negating Allah from all directions would imply nonexistence. Finally, it specifies the direction as 'above' based on textual and rational grounds. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic source once to get a feel for its rhythm and structure. Then read the English translation to ensure you grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the two distinct argumentative moves: the substances comparison and the stronger negation.
2
Understand the first argument: substances and 'where'
The author compares two substances, each having its own 'where' and accidents in that where. The Creator exists, the world exists, but the Creator is not in the world's 'where', so He must be in a direction relative to the world. Grasp this analogy.
This establishes a direction (jiha) without requiring a place (makan).
3
Analyze the stronger negation argument
The second formulation says that negating an entity from all six directions is the most thorough negation. If Allah were negated from all directions, that would imply His nonexistence. Therefore, He must have a direction.
This argument relies on the necessity of direction for existence.
4
See how the direction is specified as 'above'
After establishing an absolute direction, the passage specifies it as 'above' using scriptural texts and the reasoning that above is praise and below is blame. Allah is praised, so He must be above.
This bridges rational proof with revealed evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بحيث هو، فإن الجوهرين كل واحد منهما في جهة من صاحبه فأعراض كل جوهر بحيث هو، والباري موجود، وكذا العالم، وليس الباري جل وعلا بحيث العالم فثبت أنه بجهة من العالم، وربما يقررون هذا من وجه آخر فيقولون: لا نفى للمذكور أشد تحقيقا من نفيه من الجهات الست فمن نفى الباري جل وعلا من الجهات كلها فقد أخبر بعدمه. وهذه المعقولات يتعلق بها من يثبت الجهة دون المكان. ويقولون: يثبت بهذه المعقولات جهة مطلقة ثم يتعين جهة من الجهات الست وهي جهة العلو بالنصوص التي مر ذكرها ولأن جهة فوق جهة مدح، وجهة تحت جهة ذم، والله تعالى ممدوح محمود، منزه عما يوجب الذم والنقيصة.
Or ‘in the where of it,’ for the two substances each of them is in a direction from its companion, so the accidents of each substance are in its ‘where.’ And the Creator exists, and likewise the world, but the Creator, majestic and exalted, is not in the ‘where’ of the world, so it is established that He is in a direction from the world. And perhaps they formulate this from another angle, saying: There is no negation of the mentioned thing more thorough than negating it from the six directions; whoever negates the Creator, majestic and exalted, from all directions has indeed reported His nonexistence. And these rational concepts are adhered to by those who affirm direction without place. And they say: By these rational concepts an absolute direction is established, then one of the six directions is specified, namely the direction of above, by the texts that have been mentioned previously, and because a direction of above is praise, and a direction of below is blame, and Allah, exalted, is praised and lauded, free from what necessitates blame and deficiency.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents the rational argument used by those who affirm a direction (jiha) for Allah without affirming a specific place (makan). The argument begins with a comparison: two substances each have their own ‘where’ (haythu huwa), and their accidents exist in that where. The Creator exists, and the world exists, but the Creator is not in the same ‘where’ as the world, so He must be in a direction relative to the world. A stronger formulation is then given: denying an entity from all six directions (above, below, front, back, right, left) is the most thorough negation, and if Allah is negated from all directions, that would imply His nonexistence. Therefore, those who affirm direction without place use this rational proof to establish an absolute direction for Allah, and then specify that direction as ‘above’ (al-‘ulu) based on scriptural texts. They also argue that being above is a praise and being below is a blame, and Allah is praiseworthy, so He must be above. The passage thus presents the proof for jiha from rational and textual considerations, while explicitly distinguishing direction from place.

Key Terms

جوهر (jawhar)
عرض (arad)
حيث (hayth)
جهات الست (six directions)
العلو (above)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming direction for Allah necessarily implies that He occupies a place (makan), thus anthropomorphism.
Correction: The passage explicitly distinguishes between direction (jiha) and place (makan). Direction does not entail spatial occupancy or physical containment; it is a rational necessity to avoid denial of existence.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is the most thorough form of negation

D) Because the six directions are only applicable to bodies

Key Takeaway
The passage argues that Allah must have a direction (jiha) without a place, based on rational necessity, and that direction is specified as 'above' by texts and the logic of praise.

Is your faith in Allah's existence merely inherited, or have you personally grappled with the necessity of affirming His attributes—even uncomfortable ones like direction?

The passage states that 'above' is praise and 'below' is blame. In your life, do you live as though Allah is above all—your priorities, desires, and fears—or do you treat Him as beneath your immediate concerns? What concrete change would it take today to honor His exaltedness in your heart?

Refutation of Place Using 'Laysa Kamithlihi Shay'
The author refutes place for Allah using negation of similitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the negation of similitude in the Qur'an refutes the doctrine that Allah occupies a place.

Core Idea
If Allah had a place, He would be like that place—contradicting 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'.

The Ahl al-Haqq defend divine transcendence by appealing to a definitive Qur'anic text. They argue that affirming a place for Allah inevitably creates a likeness between Him and that place, because place and occupant are equal in measure. This lesson unpacks that logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the full translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the technical terms used: tamathul, mutamakkin, etc.
2
Understand the definition of similitude
The author explains that similitude is a genus with types: resemblance, equality, similarity, and imitation. Two equal things in quantity are similar.
This definition is crucial for the argument.
3
Grasp the place-occupant equality argument
A place and its occupant are equal in measure because the occupant occupies exactly the measure of its place. Anything beyond that is metaphorical.
Example: a person sitting on a bed only occupies part of the bed.
4
Apply to Allah and see the contradiction
If Allah had a place, He would be equal in measure to that place, making Him similar to it. But Allah negates all likeness in the verse.
Thus affirming place denies the definitive text.
5
Review the Ash'arite remark
The author notes that Ash'arites also use this verse, though their principle requires equality in all aspects for similitude. This inconsistency aids the Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أهل الحق فإنهم تعلقوا بقول الله تعالى: لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ، فالله تعالى نفى أن يكون له مثل من الأشياء، وقد بينا أن المتساويين من حيث الكمية مثلان لما مر من كون المماثلة جنسا تحته أنواع، وهي المشابهة، والمساواة، والمشاكلة، والمضاهاة، وكل نوع منها ينطلق عليه اسم المماثلة، فالمتساويان إذا متماثلان والمكان والمتمكن متساويان قدرا إذ مكان كل متمكن هو القدر الذي تمكن فيه المتمكن، فأما ما فضل عنه فليس بمكان له حقيقة، ولو سمي مكانا له كان ذلك مجازا، فمكان الجالس على السرير قدر ما تمكن فيه الجالس من السرير، وما وراء ذلك ليس بمكان له حقيقة، ويسمى السرير مكانا له مجازا لاشتماله على ما هو مكانه، ولهذا قسم الأوائل الظروف إلى القريب منها والبعيد في الزمان والمكان جميعا، وإذا ثبت هذا ثبتت مماثلة المكان والمتمكن لاستوائهما في القدر، والله تعالى نفى المماثلة بين ذاته وبين غيره من الأشياء؛ فيكون القول بإثبات المكان له ردا لهذا النص المحكم الذي لا احتمال فيه لوجه ما سوى ظاهره، وراد النص كافر عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك. تعلق الشيخ الإمام أبو منصور بهذه الآية، وكذا الأشعريون تعلقوا بها، وإن كان من مذهبهم أن المماثلة لا تثبت بمجرد الاشتراك في القدر ما لم يثبت الاشتراك من جميع الوجوه، فهذا منهم ترك لأصلهم ومساعدة لنا في إثبات المماثلة بوجه عند المساواة فيه.
As for the people of truth, they adhere to the saying of Allah, the Exalted: 'There is nothing like unto Him.' So Allah, the Exalted, negated that He has any likeness among things. And we have explained that two equal things in terms of quantity are similar due to what has passed of similitude being a genus under which there are types, namely resemblance, equality, similarity, and imitation; each type of them is called by the name similitude. So two equal things are similar, and place and its occupant are equal in measure, for the place of every occupant is the measure in which the occupant occupies; as for what exceeds that, it is not a place for it in reality, and if it were called its place it would be metaphorical. So the place of the one sitting on a bed is the measure of the bed that the sitter occupies, and what is beyond that is not a place for it in reality, and the bed is called its place metaphorically because it encompasses what is its place. For this reason, the ancients divided containers into the near and the far in both time and place. And if this is established, the similitude of the place and the occupant is established due to their equality in measure. And Allah, the Exalted, negated similitude between His essence and other things; therefore, the statement affirming place for Him is a rejection of this definitive text, which has no possibility for any aspect other than its apparent meaning, and the rejecter of the text is a disbeliever—may Allah protect us from that. The Shaykh Imam Abu Mansur clung to this verse, and likewise the Ash'arites clung to it, although their doctrine is that similitude is not established merely by sharing in measure unless sharing in all aspects is established. So this is, on their part, an abandonment of their principle and an assistance to us in establishing similitude in one aspect when equality in that aspect exists.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents the response of the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) to those who affirm a place (makan) for Allah. They rely on the Qur'anic verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'' (There is nothing like unto Him). The author explains that similitude (tamathul) is a genus with types such as resemblance, equality, similarity, and imitation. Two things that are equal in quantity (kammiyya) are considered similar. Then the argument applies this to place and its occupant: the place and the occupant are equal in measure, because the occupant's place is precisely the amount of space it occupies. If we say a person is sitting on a bed, his actual place is only the part of the bed he occupies; calling the whole bed his place is metaphorical. Therefore, if Allah were to occupy a place, He would be equal in measure to that place, making Him similar to it. But Allah has negated all likeness from Himself. So affirming place for Allah contradicts the clear text. The author notes that even the Ash'arites use this verse, although their own principle is that similitude requires equality in all aspects, not just one. This inconsistency, the author points out, actually aids the Maturidi position that equality in one aspect is sufficient for similitude in that context.

مماثلة
مكان
متمكن
قدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling the whole bed a place for the sitter is literal, not metaphorical.
Correction: The author clarifies that the real place is only the portion occupied; calling the whole bed a place is metaphorical because it encompasses the real place.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that affirming a place for Allah leads to a contradiction with the verse 'Laysa kamithlihi shay'?
A) Because place and occupant are equal in measure, creating a likeness
B) Because Allah cannot be in a place due to His greatness
C) Because the verse only negates literal likeness
D) Because place is a contingent attribute

Key Takeaway
Affirming a place for Allah creates a likeness that contradicts the explicit negation of all similitude in the Qur'an.

?

?

?

Place Implies Contingency: The Maturidi Rational Argument
Rational argument against affirming place for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming a place for Allah implies that He would acquire a contingent attribute, which is impossible for the Eternal.

Core Idea
Place would make Allah contingent, which is impossible.

This lesson explores a foundational Maturidi argument from Imam al-Nasafi. The author first establishes that any sign of contingency cannot apply to Allah. Then he applies this principle to the concept of 'place' (makan), showing that affirming a place for Allah would necessitate a new, contingent attribute in Him—a clear impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the two-part structure: a general principle followed by its application to place.
The text ends mid-sentence, but the core argument is complete enough.
2
Understand the general principle
The author argues that establishing signs of contingency in the Eternal leads to absurdity: either the Eternal becomes contingent or the contingent becomes eternal. Grasp this logical dilemma.
This principle is key to all Maturidi arguments against anthropomorphism.
3
Apply the principle to place
See how affirming a place for Allah would introduce a sign of contingency. Specifically, occupation in a place would be a new attribute arising after non-existence, making it contingent.
Both parties agree place is contingent, which strengthens the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Even though the text ends abruptly, the conclusion is clear: Allah cannot have a place because it would imply a contingent attribute, contradicting His eternity.
Try to complete the sentence in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعقول هو : أن الله تعالى قديم، وثبوت أمارات الحدث في القديم محال، لأنه يؤدي إلى أحد أمرين : إما حدوث القديم، وإما قدم الحادث، وكلا الأمرين محال، وهذا لأن هذه الأمارات إن لم تبطل دلالتها فقد ثبت حدوث القديم لثبوتها فيه وإن بطلت دلالتها فيه، أو لم توجب حدوثه بطلت دلالتها في العالم ولم يوجب حدوثه، وذلك محال ، وإذا تقرر هذا الكلام فنقول : إن إثبات المكان لله تعالى وجعله متمكنا فيه إثبات أمارات الحدوث فيه من وجوه أحدها : أنه تعالى كان ولا مكان بلا خلاف بيننا وبين الخصوم لأنهم أقروا بحدوث المكان ، وإذا كان كذلك علم يقينا أنه لم يكن متمكنا في الأزل في مكان لاستحالة التمكن في العدم، فلو صار متمكنا بعد وجود المكان لصار متمكنا بعد أن لم يكن متمكنا ثم لا شك أن تمكنه معنى وراء ذاته قبل حدوث المكان ولا تمكن، ولا شك أن هذا المعنى حادث لأنه لم يكن قبل حدوث المكان، لأنه لم يكن في الأزل متمكنا، وحدوث
And the rational (argument) is: that Allah, the Exalted, is eternal, and the establishment of signs of contingency in the Eternal is impossible, because it leads to one of two matters: either the contingency of the Eternal, or the eternity of the contingent, and both matters are impossible. This is because these signs—if their indication is not invalidated, then the contingency of the Eternal is established due to their existence in it; and if their indication is invalidated in it, or they do not necessitate its contingency, then their indication is invalidated in the world and do not necessitate its contingency—and that is impossible. And when this discourse is settled, we say: Indeed, the affirmation of place for Allah, the Exalted, and making Him an occupant therein is an affirmation of signs of contingency in Him from several aspects. One of them: that He, the Exalted, was and there was no place, without disagreement between us and the opponents, because they acknowledge the contingency of place. And if that is so, it is known with certainty that He was not an occupant in pre-eternity in a place, because occupation in nonexistence is impossible. So if He became an occupant after the existence of place, He would become an occupant after not having been an occupant. Then there is no doubt that His occupation is a meaning (ma'nan) beyond His essence before the existence of place with no occupation, and there is no doubt that this meaning is contingent because it did not exist before the existence of place, since He was not an occupant in pre-eternity, and the contingency of
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage presents the core rational argument (al-ma'qul) of the Maturidi school against affirming a place for Allah. The argument proceeds in two stages. First, the general principle: Allah is eternal (qadim), and establishing any signs of contingency (amarat al-hadath) in the Eternal is impossible. The reason is that if such signs were present and indicative, they would make the Eternal contingent; if they were present but not indicative, they would lose their indicative value in the world as well, which is absurd. So no sign of contingency can apply to Allah. Second, the argument applies this to place: affirming a place (makan) for Allah would involve a sign of contingency in one of several aspects. The author details one aspect: Allah existed when there was no place (both parties agree place is contingent). He was not an occupant (mutamakkin) in pre-eternity because occupation in nonexistence is impossible. If He became an occupant after place came into existence, He would undergo change—acquiring a new attribute (ma'na) of occupation that He did not have before. That new attribute is clearly contingent because it did not exist previously. Thus, affirming place for Allah necessitates a contingent attribute in Him, which contradicts His eternity. The text ends mid-sentence listing 'wa huduth' (and the contingency of...), presumably continuing with the conclusion that this is impossible.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim)
حادث (hadith)
أمارات الحدث (signs of contingency)
مكان (makan)
تمكن (tamakkun)
أزل (pre-eternity)
معنى (ma'na/attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that place is eternal and that Allah cannot be in it because it would be like a body in space.
Correction: The argument does not assume place is eternal; it builds on the agreed-upon contingency of place to show the logical consequence: if Allah were in place, He would acquire a new contingent attribute, which is impossible for the Eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the rational argument, why is it impossible for Allah to become an occupant of place after not being one?
A) Because place is eternal and can't be occupied
B) Because that would imply a new contingent attribute in Allah
C) Because Allah cannot be in a place due to His immateriality
D) Because the opponents already admit place is eternal

Key Takeaway
Affirming a place for Allah would introduce a contingent attribute (occupation) into His essence, which is impossible because Allah is Eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you unconsciously imagine Allah in a specific direction, like above the heavens? How does this proof that even a spiritual 'occupancy' would be contingent challenge you to purify your worship of any spatial assumptions about Allah? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself that Allah is beyond place?
?
You instinctively refer to Allah as 'here' or 'there' in casual conversation. If Allah is truly beyond all place, how should that change the way you speak about Him? This lesson demands that you treat every attribute you ascribe to Allah as eternal. Are there attributes you casually give to Allah that are actually temporal? How will you correct yourself?
?
The argument shows that any change or acquisition in Allah is impossible. Think of a moment in your life when you felt Allah was 'distant' or 'near'. Did you assume He moved relative to you? How does realizing that Allah never changes—and that your feelings do not affect His nearness—transform your reliance on Him in difficult times? Commit to one action today that reflects this truth.

Affirming Place Rejects a Definitive Qur'anic Text
Author uses Q. 42:11 to negate place for Allah and declares rejection as kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why affirming a location for Allah contradicts the definitive Qur'anic negation of likeness and the severe consequences of rejecting such a text.

Core Idea
Allah's transcendence is explicitly stated in the Qur'an; rejecting it is disbelief.

The author begins by citing the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' as a definitive text. He argues that one cannot affirm a place for Allah because that would imply a likeness to created things, contradicting the clear meaning of the verse. This lesson establishes the scriptural foundation for Allah's transcendence from space.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its application
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the verse and how the author uses it to negate place for Allah.
The verse is Qur'an 42:11.
2
Understand 'definitive text' (naṣṣ muḥkam)
The author describes the verse as definitive with no possibility for another meaning. Consider what makes a Qur'anic text definitive and why it matters.
3
Grasp the consequence
The author declares that rejecting such a definitive text renders one a disbeliever. Ask yourself why this severe judgement is applied.
This ties to the core idea of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى نفى المماثلة بين ذاته وبين غيره من الأشياء؛ فيكون القول بإثبات المكان له ردا لهذا النص المحكم الذي لا احتمال فيه لوجه ما سوى ظاهره، وراد النص كافر عصمنا الله تعالى عن ذلك. تعلق الشيخ الإمام أبو منصور بهذه الآية، وكذا الأشعريون تعلقوا بها، وإن كان من مذهبهم أن المماثلة لا تثبت بمجرد الاشتراك في القدر ما لم يثبت الاشتراك من جميع الوجوه، فهذا منهم ترك لأصلهم ومساعدة لنا في إثبات المماثلة بوجه عند المساواة فيه.
He, exalted, negated likeness between His essence and other than Him from among things; so the statement of affirming place for Him is a rejection of this definitive text which has no possibility for any meaning besides its apparent meaning, and the rejecter of the text is a disbeliever – may Allah protect us from that. The shaykh, the imam Abu Mansur adhered to this verse, and likewise the Ash'aris adhered to it, even though their doctrine is that likeness is not established by mere sharing in an attribute unless sharing from all aspects is established. So this from them is abandonment of their principle and assistance to us in establishing likeness in one respect when equality exists in it.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage provides a scriptural proof against attributing a location (makān) to Allah. The author begins by citing the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Qur'an 42:11), which he considers a definitive (muḥkam) text with only one possible apparent meaning: the absolute negation of any likeness between Allah and His creation. Therefore, any claim that Allah occupies a place (makān) directly contradicts this verse, and whoever rejects such a definitive text is considered a disbeliever (kāfir). Then the author notes that both Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and the Ash'aris use this same verse as evidence. However, he points out an inconsistency: the Ash'ari school holds that likeness (mumāthala) is not established by mere sharing in one attribute (like existence) unless there is sharing in all aspects. By using this verse, they are, in effect, abandoning their own principle and actually helping the Maturidi position, which holds that sharing in one attribute (like existence) is sufficient to establish a degree of similarity that negates absolute otherness. The passage thus underscores the decisive authority of the Qur'anic text and exposes an internal tension in the Ash'ari use of that text.

Key Terms

مكان
نص محكم
كافر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' allows for metaphorical interpretation regarding place.
Correction: The author considers the text definitive (muḥkam) with no possibility for anything beyond its apparent meaning, so affirming place directly rejects it.

Checkpoint

B) Because it rejects a definitive Qur'anic text (Q. 42:11) which negates likeness.
C) Because it implies Allah is created.
D) Because it contradicts the Ash'ari doctrine of likening.

Key Takeaway
Affirming a place for Allah is a direct contradiction of the definitive Qur'anic negation of likeness, and thus constitutes rejection of divine revelation.

The Maturidi-Ash'ari Debate on Likeness
Author highlights Ash'ari inconsistency in using Q. 42:11 given their own doctrine on likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how both Maturidis and Ash'aris use the same verse but with an inconsistency in Ash'ari reasoning regarding likeness.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari principle on likeness contradicts their use of Q. 42:11, aiding the Maturidi position.

The author notes that both Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and the Ash'aris rely on the same verse. However, the Ash'ari school holds that likeness requires sharing in all aspects, not just one. By using this verse, they inadvertently support the Maturidi view that sharing in one attribute (existence) does imply a degree of likeness. This lesson explores this theological nuance.

Guided Path
1
See that both schools cite the verse
Read the passage noting that both Abu Mansur (Maturidi) and the Ash'aris use Q. 42:11 as evidence.
2
Understand the Ash'ari principle on likeness
The Ash'ari doctrine holds that mumāthala (likeness) is not established by mere sharing in one attribute (e.g., existence) unless sharing occurs in all aspects. Grasp this principle.
This is key to seeing the inconsistency.
3
Recognize the inconsistency
The author argues that by using this verse, the Ash'aris abandon their own principle and help the Maturidi position. Consider how this happens.
The Maturidi view accepts that sharing in one aspect is sufficient for a kind of likeness.

Source Text

The shaykh, the imam Abu Mansur adhered to this verse, and likewise the Ash'aris adhered to it, even though their doctrine is that likeness is not established by mere sharing in an attribute unless sharing from all aspects is established. Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage provides a scriptural proof against attributing a location (makān) to Allah. The author begins by citing the verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Qur'an 42:11), which he considers a definitive (muḥkam) text with only one possible apparent meaning: the absolute negation of any likeness between Allah and His creation. Therefore, any claim that Allah occupies a place (makān) directly contradicts this verse, and whoever rejects such a definitive text is considered a disbeliever (kāfir). Then the author notes that both Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and the Ash'aris use this same verse as evidence. The passage thus underscores the decisive authority of the Qur'anic text and exposes an internal tension in the Ash'ari use of that text.

Proof from Impossibility of Contact with Throne
Author argues that Allah's location would entail temporal contact, impossible for the Eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing location to Allah leads to the absurd conclusion that He would have temporal contact with the Throne, which is impossible for the Eternal.

Core Idea
Location would force Allah into temporal contact — impossible for the Eternal.

This passage continues the rational proof against Allah being located in a place. The author builds on the earlier principle that temporal events cannot occur in the eternal essence. Here he argues that if Allah were located in the Throne, He would have to acquire a relation of contact (mumāssa) that did not exist before — a clear change. This is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow become familiar.
2
Identify the core argument
Notice that the author starts with the premise that Allah was not in contact with the Throne before its creation. Then he argues that if location were possible, contact and meeting would arise after not having existed.
This is the key conditional: location → new contact.
3
Understand the impossibility reasoning
The author explains that contact requires two bodies, and acquiring it newly implies a temporal change in the eternal essence. He also shows that allowing such contact leads to absurd consequences, like a body touching something it wasn't touching without any change in the other.
This connects to the broader principle against temporal events in Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنه لم يكن مماسا للعرش ولا ملاقيا إياه، وبالتمكن فيه تحدث المماسة والملاقاة بعد أن لم تكن، لاستحالة قيام المماسة به في القدم قبل حدوث المكان لأن قيام المماسة بذات بدون قيام مماسة أخرى بذات آخر محال، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يكون جسم مماسا لما ليس مماس له وهذا خروج عن المعقول، وكذا الاجتماع على هذا، وقد مر بيان استحالة حدوث الحوادث في ذات القديم.
And because He was not in contact (mumāssan) with the Throne nor meeting (mulāqiyan) it, and by being located in it, contact and meeting would occur after not having been, due to the impossibility of the establishment of contact with Him in pre-eternity before the occurrence of place, because the establishment of contact with one essence without the establishment of another contact with another essence is impossible. And if that were permissible, it would be permissible for a body to be in contact with that which it is not in contact with, and this is a departure from reason. And likewise meeting (al-ijtimā‘) upon this. And the exposition of the impossibility of the occurrence of temporal events in the essence of the eternal has already passed.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage provides a further rational argument based on the concept of contact (mumāssa) and meeting (mulāqā). The author states that before the creation of place, Allah was not in contact or meeting with the Throne (since both the Throne and place are created). If one claims that Allah becomes located (mutamakkin) in a place, then contact and meeting with the Throne would arise after not having existed – a temporal change. This is impossible because contact requires two bodies, and to say that the eternal essence acquires contact after previously lacking it implies that the eternal is subject to temporal accidents. Moreover, if such contact could occur without a pre-existing relation, then one could absurdly claim that a body is in contact with something it was not in contact with, without any change in the other object – this contradicts reason (al-ma‘qūl). The same applies to the concept of meeting (ijtimā‘). The author concludes by reminding the reader that the general proof against the occurrence of temporal events (ḥawādith) in the eternal essence has already been given earlier (presumably in the previous lesson). Thus, the argument against Allah being located in a place is strengthened by the absurdity of attributing contact and meeting to Him in time.

Key Terms
مماسة
ملاقاة
تمكن
عرش
جسم
اجتماع
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Allah can be in contact with the Throne without implying change, because contact is a static relation.
Correction: The author shows that contact itself is a temporal relation that would necessarily be new if it arises after non-existence. Since Allah is eternal and changeless, any new relation is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that Allah could become located (mutamakkin) and thus be in contact with the Throne?
A) Because the Throne is too great for Allah.
B) Because contact implies spatial extension and multiplicity, which are impossible for Allah.
C) Because such contact would be a temporal event, and temporal events cannot occur in the eternal essence.
D) Because the Qur'an denies the Throne.

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be located in a place because location would entail acquiring temporal contact, which is impossible for the Eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows that any relation of Allah to creation that appears 'new' would imply change in Him. How does this challenge your understanding of how Allah relates to your prayers or to events in your life? Are you comfortable with a God who is utterly unchanging?
?
The argument relies on the principle that temporal events cannot occur in the Eternal. Examine your own belief: Do you truly accept that Allah is beyond time and change, or do you secretly imagine Him reacting to your actions? What will you do today to purify your tawḥīd from such subtle anthropomorphism?

God's Essential Freedom from Place: The Nasafian Argument
Imam Nasafi argues that God's eternal freedom from place is essential, so place cannot attach to Him later.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal divine essence cannot acquire spatial location, based on the logical dilemma presented by Imam Nasafi.

Core Idea
God's freedom from place is eternal and essential; it cannot be lost or gained.

Imam Nasafi presents a powerful argument that God's transcendence of place is not accidental but flows from His very essence. This lesson unpacks the logical dilemma he uses to show that spatial location is impossible for the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: الأزل, متعريا, خاليا, التمكن, المكان, المعنى.
The Arabic uses a condensed logical style; read slowly.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The argument splits into two scenarios: either God's freedom from place is by His essence or by a separate meaning. Follow how each leads to impossibility.
The dilemma is a standard kalam technique.
3
Trace the logical chain
Work through the steps: if by essence → occupation impossible. If by meaning → then meaning must be eternal or originated. If eternal → still impossible. If originated → essence holds two originated things → essence originated → absurd.
Pay attention to the shift from 'meaning' to 'originated'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand why the argument concludes that God cannot be in place, and how this supports divine transcendence.
This is a foundational argument in Maturidi kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه تعالى كان في الأزل متعريا عن التمكن في المكان خاليا عنه فلو كان خلوه عن ذلك وتعريه عنه لذاته فلا يتصور ثبوت التمكن في المكان، ولو كان المعنى لا يتصور أيضا ثبوت التمكن المعنى الموجب لكونه متعريا، خاليا عنه، فلو ثبت التمكن لابد من القول بعدم المعنى، وإذا قبل ذلك المعنى العدم فهو إذا محدث لاستحالة العدم على القديم، والتمكن أيضا حادث، وهو لا يخلو عن أحد هذين الحادثين، وما لا يخلو عن الحادث فهو حادث، فالصانع إذا حادث وهو محال.
That He, exalted, was in pre-eternity (al-azal) devoid of (mutaʿarriyan) and free from (khāliyan) occupation of place (al-tamakkun fī al-makān). So if His being devoid of that and His being free from it is by His essence, then the establishment of occupation of place is inconceivable. And if it is by a meaning (maʿnā), then also the establishment of occupation of place which is necessitated by the meaning that necessitated His being devoid and free is inconceivable. So if occupation of place were established, it would be necessary to affirm the non‑existence (ʿadam) of that meaning. And if that meaning is assumed to be non‑existent, then it is originated (muḥdath), because non‑existence is impossible for the Eternal (al-Qadīm). And occupation of place is also originated. And it [sc. the essence] cannot be free of one of these two originated things. And whatever cannot be free of what is originated is itself originated. Thus the Creator would be originated, and that is impossible (muḥāl).
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God cannot occupy place because His freedom from place is essential or caused by a meaning. The argument is structured as a dilemma: Either God's lack of spatial location is essential or caused by a meaning. If essential, then occupation of place can never occur. If caused by a meaning, then that meaning must be eternal or originated. But a meaning causing immateriality cannot be originated because it would then be temporally contingent, and if eternal, its effect (non‑occupation) would be eternal, so occupation cannot occur. If one tries to say that the meaning ceased to exist (became non‑existent) to allow occupation, then the meaning itself is originated (since non‑existence is impossible for the eternal), and occupation of place is also originated. The essence would then be a locus for two originated things, making it originated itself—an absurdity. Thus spatial location is impossible for God.

Key Terms
الأزل
متعريا
خاليا
التمكن
المكان
المعنى
القديم
المحدث
الحادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's lack of place is merely a temporary state or that He could choose to occupy space if He wished.
Correction: The argument shows that God's transcendence of place is essential and eternal, not a contingent state. If it were contingent, then God would be subject to change and temporality, which is impossible for the eternal Creator.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude if God's freedom from place is by His essence?
A) Occupation of place is possible through a new meaning.
B) Occupation of place is inconceivable.
C) God must have a place.
D) The essence changes.

Key Takeaway
God's essential and eternal freedom from place makes spatial location absolutely impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is beyond all place, do you still imagine Him in a particular direction when you supplicate? How will you train your heart to worship a Being who is not confined to any space?
?
The argument proves that God's nature cannot change. Have you ever assumed that God's mercy or anger varies with your actions as if He were changeable? How does this truth correct your understanding?
?
Since spatial location is impossible for God, what does that imply about your physical acts of worship—do you perform them as if they reach a distant God? How can you deepen your awareness that He is present without being in a place?

The Impossibility of God's Contact with the Throne
Nasafi proves God's transcendence by showing contact with the Throne entails change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi argues that God's contact with the Throne is impossible because it would entail an originated accident in the Eternal.

Core Idea
Contact with the Throne would force change upon the Eternal.

Imam Nasafi presents a logical argument to refute the anthropomorphic view that God occupies the Throne. He shows that contact would imply a new relation in God, which is impossible because it would mean an originated accident in the Eternal. Let's examine his proof closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the literal meaning and flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'mumāssa' (touching) and 'mulāqāh' (meeting).
2
Identify key terms
List the key terms: المماسة, الملاقاة, التمكن, القديم, المكان, الحوادث. Understand their significance in the argument.
These terms are central to the proof.
3
Follow the logical steps
Trace the argument: (1) God was not touching the Throne before creation. (2) If He were to occupy it after creation, touching would occur after not having been. (3) This would be an originated accident in the Eternal, which is impossible.
The reductio ad absurdum about mutual touching reinforces the point.
4
Connect to prior principles
Recall the previously established principle that no originated things (ḥawādith) can subsist in the Eternal. This argument applies that principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنه لم يكن مماسا للعرش ولا ملاقيا إياه، وبالتمكن فيه تحدث المماسة والملاقاة بعد أن لم تكن، لاستحالة قيام المماسة به في القدم قبل حدوث المكان، لأن قيام المماسة بذات بدون قيام مماسة أخرى بذات آخر محال، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يكون جسم مماسا لما ليس مماس له، وهذا خروج عن المعقول، وكذا الاجتماع على هذا، وقد مر بيان استحالة حدوث الحوادث في ذات القديم.
And because He was not touching (mumāssan) the Throne nor meeting it (mulāqiyan). And by occupation (al-tamakkun) of it, touching and meeting occur after they were not, due to the impossibility of touching subsisting in Him in pre‑eternity before the occurrence of place. For the subsistence of touching in one essence without the subsistence of another touching in another essence is impossible. And if this were permissible, it would be permissible for a body to be touching that which is not touching it—and this is beyond reason. And likewise for conjunction (al-ijtimāʿ) on this. And the exposition of the impossibility of the occurrence of originated things (ḥawādith) in the essence of the Eternal has already been given.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is an additional proof (dalīl) against the claim that God occupies the Throne. It argues from the impossibility of contact (mumāssa) and meeting (mulāqāt). The argument: Before the creation of the Throne, God was not touching it. If He were to occupy it after creation, He would become touching and meeting it after not having been so. This would mean that a new relation (touching) arises in God, which is an originated accident. But it is impossible for originated things to subsist in the Eternal, as already established. Moreover, touching requires two essences that are mutually touching; if one could be touching without the other, that would be absurd. This would lead to the impossible scenario of a body touching something that does not touch it. Therefore, spatial occupation of the Throne is impossible.

Key Terms
المماسة
الملاقاة
التمكن
القديم
المكان
الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that denying contact denies God's knowledge or power over the Throne.
Correction: The argument only denies physical contact, not God's absolute knowledge and power.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would be required for God to be touching the Throne?
A) A change in the Throne.
B) The occurrence of an originated relation in God.
C) The Throne to be eternal.
D) God to be a body.

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence means He cannot be in contact with creation in a way that implies change or originated accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God being 'near' to you, do you ever picture Him as physically close—like a presence you could almost touch? How does this passage demand that you purify that mental image, even if it feels less comforting?
?
Have you ever imagined God sitting or settling on the Throne as a way to make His greatness more concrete? In what ways does this argument show that such imagination, even if well-intended, undermines His transcendence? What will you replace that image with?
?
The proof says any change in God is impossible—even a new relation of contact with creation. Do you ever think of God as 'responding' to your dua in a way that implies He just now decided or felt something? How does this passage challenge you to affirm His eternal unchangeability while still trusting that He hears and answers?
